orough history and PE considered in the context of labo-
ratory and other diagnostic test results can oen help identify 
concurrent disorders. Successful management of concurrent con-
ditions while treating the DKA improves likelihood of therapeutic 
success. In dogs, the most common concurrent disorders include 
acute pancreatitis (ch. 277) and bacterial pneumonia (ch. 217).15
In cats, common concurrent diagnoses include acute pancreatitis 
(ch. 278), hepatic lipidosis (ch. 274), cholangiohepatitis (chs. 271, 
272), urinary tract infection (UTI, ch. 307), acute kidney injury 
(AKI, ch. 300) and chronic kidney disease (CKD, ch. 301).16 17
e history and PE in pets with HHS commonly reveal 
anorexia or inappetence, lethargy, vomiting, PU/PD, behavioral 
changes, diarrhea, severe dehydration, abnormal mentation (dull, 
depressed mentation, coma), and respiratory abnormalities.18 19
e most common concurrent diseases diagnosed in dogs with 
HHS are UTI (ch. 307), acute pancreatitis (ch. 277), and hyper-
adrenocorticism (ch. 293)19; in cats they include CKD (ch. 301), 
infection, CHF (ch. 229), neoplasia (chs. 322-330), and gastroin-
testinal disease (chs. 261 264).18
DIAGNOSIS
DEFINITIONS OF DKA AND HHS
Although the diagnosis of DKA and HHS can be suspected clini-
cally, conrmation requires laboratory tests. Recently, the Euro-
pean Society for Veterinary Endocrinology (ESVE) established 
the Project ALIVE (Agreeing Language in Veterinary Endocri-
nology) to clarify terminology and denitions used in veterinary 
endocrine diseases. eir diagnostic criteria for DKA included 
(i) diagnosis of DM according to ALIVE criteria; (ii) ketonemia: 
increased BHB concentrations, ketonuria, and/or detectable AcAc 
(using nitroprusside test strips); (iii) metabolic acidosis dened as 
a venous/arterial blood pH <7.35 and decreased bicarbonate. If 
blood gas analysis is unavailable, an ill pet whose condition meets 
the above criteria should be suspected of suering from DKA.
Diagnostic criteria for HHS have not been uniformly dened 
and vary slightly in the literature. Usually, criteria for HHS include 
severe hyperglycemia (BG >600 mg/dL; >34 mmol/L), serum osmo-
lality >325-330 mOsm/kg in dogs19 20 and >330-350 mOsm/kg in 
cats,18 20 21 dehydration, and absence of signicant ketoacidosis.
IDENTIFYING KETONE BODIES
Recognition of KBs in urine is the basis of diagnosing DKA in most 
pets. Ketone test squares are included in routine urine reagent 
strips. Less commonly, plasma can be checked for KBs.22 23 is 
test, using the nitroprusside reaction, provides a semiquantitative 
estimation of AcAc and acetone concentrations. It does not detect 
BHB, the predominant KB produced during DKA.24 us, urine 
and blood results may underestimate the degree of ketosis. Direct 
measurement of blood BHB using a quantitative enzymatic assay 
or portable blood ketometers is available and provides an accurate 
indication of ketoacidosis.25-31
TEST RESULTS REQUIRED FOR DKA MANAGEMENT
Results of blood gas analysis, complete blood count (CBC), serum 
biochemistry panel that includes electrolyte concentrations, and 
urinalysis (UA) are required to understand patient status and to 
identify issues that need to be monitored or treated. Other tests 
(e.g., urine culture, thoracic radiographs, abdominal ultrasound, 
serum pancreatic-specic lipase, antigen testing for feline leuke-
mia virus, and antibody testing for feline immunodeciency virus 
in cats) may be indicated. Results of BG, serum electrolytes, acid-
base, and urea nitrogen (BUN) should be available within minutes 
of obtaining blood and must be available for frequent rechecks.
THERAPY OF DIABETIC KETOACIDOSIS
OVERVIEW
Classically, the goals of treating DKA are to replace uid and electro-
lyte decits, provide adequate amounts of insulin to normalize inter-
mediary metabolism, correct acidosis, identify and treat concurrent 
diseases, and to provide carbohydrate substrate when required. 
Acidosis is usually managed adequately through uid resuscitation, 
restoring electrolyte balances, and by giving insulin to inhibit lipoly-
sis, ketogenesis and gluconeogenesis. e quest to identify any con-
comitant disorder should never delay initiating treatment for DKA.
It is appreciated that dogs and cats recovering from DKA or 
HHS need to eat as soon as possible. Prolonged anorexia can be 
associated with worrisome complications, especially in obese cats 
at risk for hepatic lipidosis (chs. 269 and 271). Low-carbohydrate 
foods are preferred, but any reasonable food can be oered initially 
to encourage voluntary food intake. Force feeding is discouraged. 
Tube feeding is uncommonly necessary. Diabetics are notoriously 
hungry. Anorexia or inappetence is almost always explained by 
unresolved DKA, concurrent illness, or iatrogenic conditions 
(e.g., hypokalemia). Overly aggressive therapy can have danger-
ous consequences while slow and progressive improvement in 
abnormal parameters (over a period of 24 to 48 hours) increases 
the likelihood of therapeutic success.
FLUID THERAPY
Benefits of Intravascular (IV) Fluids
Ill pets with DKA are invariably dehydrated and unable to meet 
their uid needs. While IV uids are essential for any severely 
dehydrated pet, uids alone do not suppress ketogenesis (ch. 
120).32,33 Fluids should be administered for 1 to several hours 
before starting insulin therapy to limit complications exacerbated 
by initiating uids and insulin together (i.e., hypoglycemia [ch. 
61], hypokalemia [ch. 68], hypophosphatemia [ch. 69]). Appro-
priate uid therapy restores IV volume, lowers BG, raises blood 
pressure, improves tissue perfusion, facilitates resolution of meta-
bolic acidosis, and can begin to correct electrolyte deciencies 
while limiting further imbalances induced by insulin therapy.
Fluid Choice
Because animals with DKA have interstitial space and intracellular 
dehydration, crystalloid uids are indicated (ch. 120). Although 
0.9% saline solution could be used for water and Na replacement, 
it is an unbuered solution which in humans is associated with 
hyperchloremic metabolic acidosis that can prolong recovery from 
acidosis.34 Until evidence in dogs and cats is developed on this mat-
ter, balanced crystalloids with a buer are recommended for uid 
resuscitation (e.g., Ringer’s solution, Plasma-Lyte 148, Normosol-R).
ese uids contain trivial amounts of K. Hypotonic uids (e.g., 
0.45% saline) are rarely indicated, even when severe hyperosmolal-
ity is present. Hypotonic uids are unsuitable for correcting Na and 
water decits and increase the risk of decreasing extracellular uid 
(ECF) osmolality too quickly which could result in cerebral edema.
Rate of Administration
Fluid rates should be based on assessment of hydration, degree of 
shock, and the presence of concurrent diseases which may limit 
the rate of infusion (ch. 120). Dogs and cats with DKA receiving 
IV uids require close monitoring subjectively and objectively to 
avoid complications resulting from giving excessive or insucient 
uid volumes. Too much can lead to overhydration, pulmonary 
edema, or other third-space uid loss. Too little prolongs poor tis-
sue perfusion, hypoxia, and prerenal azotemia. e formula used 
to calculate the uid decit, based on estimation of dehydration 
(with % dehydration expressed as decimal, e.g., 10% = 0.1), is:

Fluid decit (mL) = % dehydration × body weight (kg) × 1000 mL/kg
is decit should be gradually restored over 24 hours (about 
60-80% within the rst 12 hours and the remaining 20-40% in 
the next 12 hours) while also supplying maintenance uid needs 
and matching ongoing losses due to PU, vomiting and diarrhea 
(Figure 290.2). When the patient is no longer critically ill, the rate 
of IV uids should be adjusted to meet the pet’s hemodynamic 
and electrolyte needs. Fluid rates 1.5 to 2 times maintenance (i.e., 
60 to 100 mL/kg/24 h) are usually used initially. If the patient is in 
shock (ch. 121) at the time of admission, boluses of uids are to be 
administered at 5-10 mL/kg in 20 minutes until the heart rate and 
blood pressure are adequate (chs. 120 and 121).
ELECTROLYTES
It should be assumed that all pets with DKA are K+-depleted. eir 
“total body depletion” is oen masked initially by translocation 
of K+ from intracellular to extracellular spaces due to acidosis.

erefore, K+ should be added routinely to uids according to 
serum concentration (Table 290.1), except when renal function 
is impaired and urine production is inadequate. If resuscitative 
boluses are needed, it is essential not to exceed 0.5 mEq/kg/h of K+. 
Since changes in serum electrolytes and blood gases are unpredict-
able during the rst 24 hours of treatment, frequent assessments 
(q2-4h) and adjustments in treatment should be made accordingly 
(Table 290.1; chs. 120 and 121).
BLOOD GLUCOSE (BG)
BGs should be monitored every hour for the rst 24 hours of ther-
apy. When the BG decreases ≤250 mg/dL (<14 mmol/L) or when 
the decrease is >50-75 mg/dL/h (>2.8-4.1 mmol/L/h) and the keto-
sis is still marked, dextrose must be added to the IV uid solution. A 
2.5% glucose solution can be used initially and this or a 5% solution 
is usually sucient. One may need to use higher concentrations to 
maintain BG concentration between 150 and 300 mg/dL (8.3-16.7 
mmol/L), rather than decreasing the dose of insulin. It is impor-
tant to remember that IV glucose can cause phlebitis; catheter sites 
should be monitored carefully and the risk is lower when a central 
venous catheter is used (ch. 75). While counter-intuitive, adding 
dextrose to the IV uids is crucial for providing a carbohydrate sub-
strate which allows continued insulin infusion at a rate sucient to 
inhibit lipolysis and ketogenesis and to resolve ketonemia. e dex-
trose helps to avoid hypoglycemic episodes and dramatic osmotic 
shis. Use of factory-calibrated ash glucose monitoring systems 
(FGMSs, FreeStyle Libre, Abbott) can provide valuable data on dogs 
with DKA (ch. 78).35,36 ey can continuously measure interstitial 
glucose concentrations which reect circulating BG concentra-
tions.37-44 e FGMS provides results in real time, does not require 
calibration, and can be used as long as 14 days. Glucose monitors 
are being improved and changed frequently.
ACIDOSIS
Fluid therapy and insulin administration are usually eective in 
slowly resolving the metabolic acidosis of DKA over the rst 12 to 
24 hours. Insulin enhances KB utilization, important since metab-
olism of a mEq of KB produces a mEq of bicarbonate. Sodium 
bicarbonate therapy can be counterproductive when considering 
the risks associated with too rapid correction of acidemia (exac-
erbation of hypokalemia, reduced dissociation of oxygen from 
hemoglobin, worsening of central nervous system function). For 
these reasons, sodium bicarbonate treatment is strictly reserved 
for pets in DKA with plasma bicarbonate concentrations ≤11 
mEq/L despite several hours of uid therapy. Even then, bicarbon-
ate should be given in conservative doses. e formula used to cal-
culate the bicarbonate decit (i.e., the mEq of bicarbonate initially 
needed to correct acidemia to the critical level of 12 mEq/L) is:
bicarbonate decit (mEq) = body weight (kg) × 0.4 × (12 − patient’s 
bicarbonate)
To avoid the adverse eects of Na bicarbonate treatment, only 
half of this decit must be administered over a period of 6 hours, 
aer which the acid-base state should be re-evaluated, and a new 
dosage calculated until plasma bicarbonate concentrations are 
≥12 mEq/L.
INSULIN THERAPY
Physiology
Insulin administration is the cornerstone of treating DKA. Insulin 
inhibits lipolysis which, in turn, slows mobilization of the FFAs 
necessary for hepatic ketogenesis. Insulin suppresses hepatic 
gluconeogenesis, promotes glucose utilization, and enhances KB 
metabolism.6,45 Reductions in BG, urine glucose, and KB concen-
trations aer insulin is given reduces osmotic diuresis and renal 
electrolyte losses, helping to correct acidosis. As glucose moves 
from the extracellular space into cells, it is accompanied by water, 
K, and PO4. Serum electrolyte changes are unpredictable and wor-
risome changes can occur rapidly. eir concentrations must be 
monitored regularly early in treatment (q2-4h). Life-threatening 
hypokalemia, hypophosphatemia, hyperkalemia, or hypernatre-
mia occur, representing some of the challenges that require man-
agement when treating DKA.
Goals of Insulin Administration
e goal of insulin therapy is to slowly lower the BG concentration 
<250 mg/dL (<14 mmol/L) while not allowing it to approach 100 
mg/dL (5.6 mmol/L). is should take place over 6 to 10 hours. 
Hourly decreases of 50-75 mg/dL/h (2.8-4.1 mmol/L) are ideal.46
Fluid therapy alone lowers BG via dilution. Insulin doses that 
decrease BG are invariably eective at suppressing KB produc-
tion. Once the BG decreases to about 250 mg/dL, it is advisable to 
keep glycemia near this range by supplementing the IV uids with 
dextrose. is approach allows insulin to be given safely and con-
tinuously until DKA resolves. One of many challenges in treating 
a critically ill dog or cat with DKA is using insulin correctly. One 
wants to provide enough insulin to restore normal cellular utiliza-
tion of glucose while avoiding adverse eects. Adverse issues are 
avoided by appropriate use of uid therapy, frequent monitoring 
of serum electrolytes and BG, and delaying initial insulin admin-
istration for several hours while uid resuscitation is started. One 
should delay insulin for a minimum of 2 hours if serum K+ con-
centrations are within the normal range, but up to 4 hours if hypo-
kalemia persists. If hypokalemia seems resistant to being resolved, 
it is essential to check that adequate K+ is being supplemented. 
Commercial IV uids, in general, contain little or no K+ (ch. 120). 
One may consider low initial insulin doses to limit intracellular 
shis of K+ and PO4, which can worsen their serum decits.47 In 
one study, starting insulin within 6 hours of admission was associ-
ated with faster resolution of DKA but no dierence in complica-
tion rate compared to starting insulin >6 hours aer admission.48
e switch to long-acting insulin cannot take place until keto-
sis and acidemia are resolved, hydration status is adequate, serum 
electrolytes are normal or near-normal, and appetite returns 
without vomiting. In pets with concurrent insulin-antagonistic 
disease, insulin may be less eective. While the dog or cat ulti-
mately determines their insulin needs, an underlying disease in 
some determines ability to resolve DKA and may be the major 
cause of morbidity and mortality.
The Ideal Insulin
e ideal insulin begins to work within moments and is metabolized 
quickly. ese attributes allow quick assessment of dosage enabling 
rapid adjustments in treatment. It is fair to suggest that choosing 
a dosage of insulin for an ill dog or cat with DKA is, at best, an 
educated guess, an “experiment.” Monitoring the eect of that rst 
dose on BGs determines if too much or too little was given. Since 
pets with DKA are critically ill, quick identication of insulin over-
and underdosing can be lifesaving. e clinician can adjust insulin 
dosage within minutes if it is being given IV continuously or within 
an hour if short-acting insulin was given intramuscularly (IM) 
or subcutaneously (SQ). For decades, Regular® crystalline insulin 
was the only preparation available with immediate activity and an 
extremely short duration of action. Various dosages given IV, IM, 
or SQ have been recommended and used with some success.49-52
When technology allowed continuous rate IV infusions (CRI), 
periodic injections of insulin were generally replaced with low-dose 
continuous Regular® insulin, if facilities were available. Among the

advantages of the CRI is avoiding the potentially erratic absorption 
of insulin given IM or SQ, especially in dehydrated pets with com-
promised tissue perfusion. In addition to hydration and perfusion, 
absorption of insulin can be aected by the structural properties of 
the insulin molecule. Regular® insulin is a solution of zinc-insulin 
hexamers, whose absorption requires dissociation following IM or 
SQ injection. e process of dissociation can delay insulin’s onset 
of action and prolong its duration of eect, resulting in a more 
variable time-action proles. e less predictable changes in BG 
increase the risk of hypoglycemic events. Rapid-acting insulin ana-
logs (lispro, aspart, and glulisine) have been developed with struc-
tures that dier from the original molecule by one or two amino 
acids, hindering hexamerization and speeding absorption and 
metabolism following SQ injection. ese features ensure rapid 
onset and a short duration of hypoglycemic activity.53 In people, 
intermittent SQ injections of insulin analogs were as eective as IV 
Regular® insulin in the treatment of mild-to-moderate DKA.54-56
Low-Dose IV Constant Rate Infusion (CRI) of Insulin
e decision to employ a CRI protocol (ch. 77) is based on clini-
cian preference and availability of technical support and infusion 
pumps. e initial dose of Regular® insulin (2.2 U/kg [dogs]; 1.1 U/
kg [cats])51 should be added to 48 mL of 0.9% saline and adminis-
tered via a line separate from that used for uid resuscitation with 
an infusion pump. e initial dose should be about 2 mL/hour, 
using the 48 mL solution over 24 hours. Adjustments in the infu-
sion rate should be based on BG concentrations (Figure 290.2). 
Dextrose should be added to the IV uids (generally a 2.5% solu-
tion) once BG drops ≤250 mg/dL (≤13.9 mmol/L). Since insulin 
binds to plastic, the 48 mL insulin solution should be le in the 
infusion syringe and line for about 30 minutes.57-59 e entire 48 
mL should then be discarded, and a fresh insulin solution used.
e ecacy and safety of 3 Regular® insulin dosages (1.1 U/
kg/24 h, 2.2 U/kg/24 h, and increasing the dosage from 1.1 to 2.2 
U/kg/24 h) were compared in cats with DKA.52 No signicant 
dierence was identied between groups in the time required to 
reach a BG of 250 mg/dL, in serum K+ and PO4 concentrations, in 
the time required to resolve ketonuria, or the duration of hospital-
ization. e ecacy and safety of an IV lispro insulin CRI in dogs 
and cats and of an IV aspart insulin CRI in dogs were compared to 
IV CRIs of Regular® insulin.60-63 All protocols were equally eec-
tive and safe.
Intramuscular (IM)/Subcutaneous (SQ) Protocols
Regular insulin can be given in a loading dose of 0.1-0.2 U/kg IM 
followed by 0.1 U/kg IM every 1-2 hours (Figure 290.2).47 e 
insulin dosage should be reduced by 25-50% in the rst hours of 
treatment if hypokalemia is a concern. When the BG concentra-
tion approaches 250 mg/dL (13.9 mmol/L), the insulin can be 
administered at a dose of 0.1-0.3 U/kg IM every 4-6 hours if the 
pet is dehydrated but can be given SQ every 6-8 hours if hydration 
status is adequate. A 2.5-5% dextrose solution should be adminis-
tered to maintain BG concentration between 150 and 300 mg/dL 
(8.3-16.7 mmol/L) until DKA resolves.
A study investigated the use of IM/SQ lispro insulin in dogs 
with the aim of simplifying the management of DKA while 
exploiting the advantages associated with its molecular struc-
ture.64 e dogs received IM lispro insulin at an initial dosage of 
0.25 U/kg. If a drop of at least 10% in BG during the next hour was 
not achieved, the insulin dose was repeated hourly; otherwise, the 
insulin dose was repeated q3h. When the BG reached 250 mg/
dL (13.9 mmol/L), the insulin dosage was reduced to 0.125 U/
kg IM or SQ (based on hydration status) q3h and glucose was 
added to the IV uids to create a 2.5% solution. e median time 
to resolution of ketosis was signicantly shorter in dogs treated 
with this protocol compared to dogs treated with an IV CRI of 
Regular® insulin. No dierences were noted in frequency or sever-
ity of adverse events.64 e ecacy of IM insulin glargine (1-2 U/
cat, q2-22h) with or without concomitant SQ glargine (1-3 U/cat 
q12h) was evaluated in cats.65 is protocol was associated with 
successful resolution of DKA in all 14 cats studied.
PROGNOSIS
erapeutic success depends on the clinician’s ability and 
owner willingness to implement one of the treatment strategies 
described for DKA. One cannot overemphasize the importance 
of constant supervision, frequent monitoring of vital parameters, 
hydration, tissue perfusion, BG, K+, Na+, PO4, and acid-base sta-
tus. It is important to quickly identify parameters that require 
attention and adjusting treatment to manage those issues (e.g., 
hypokalemia or hypoglycemia). Any underlying disease should 
be identied and treated, especially those causing insulin resis-
tance (ch. 224). ese measures have reduced mortality from 
25-35%15-17,51 to <10%.52,60,62-66 Severe underlying medical disor-
ders (e.g., pancreatitis, hormonal disorders, sepsis, AKI, CHF) or 
client nancial constraints account for most deaths, rather than 
the metabolic complications of DKA. In cats, remission of DM 
is possible following resolution of DKA, especially in cats with 
concurrent pancreatic disease or cats being treated with glucocor-
ticoids at the time DKA is diagnosed.65,67
THERAPY AND PROGNOSIS OF HYPEROSMOLAR 
HYPERGLYCEMIC STATE
Animals with HHS are critically ill and many have acute kidney 
failure causing death or euthanasia. Treatment of HHS requires 
slow restoration of dehydration decits (24-48 hours) and cor-
rection of severe hyperglycemia to minimize iatrogenic cerebral 
edema. Despite severe hyperosmolarity, the initial uid of choice 
is isotonic 0.9% saline. Serum K+ and PO4 concentrations must 
be closely monitored and supplementation based on adjusting 
their serum concentrations in the context of kidney function and 
urine production. Initial supplementation with 20 mEq/L of KCl is 
recommended. Early insulin therapy is not critical with HHS and 
should be delayed until plasma volume is restored. To avoid the 
risk of cerebral edema, BG concentration should not drop by more 
than 50 mg/dL/h (2.8 mmol/L/h). e insulin protocols are the 
same for HHS as those discussed for DKA, but the initial insulin 
dosage should be reduced by 50% to avoid rapid decreases in ECF 
osmolality. Creating 2.5-5% dextrose IV uids is recommended 
when BG is <250 mg/dL (14 mmol/L). e prognosis of HHS is 
guarded to poor.18,19

INTRODUCTION
D
iabetes mellitus (DM) is a hetero-
geneous group of diseases with 
multiple etiologies characterized by 
hyperglycemia resulting from inadequate 
insulin secretion, inadequate insulin action, 
or both.1 e prevalence of DM in dogs has 
been estimated from about 0.3% in rst opin-
ion practices to about 1% in referral institu-
tion practices.2-5 e reported incidence 
of DM in a population of 182,087 Swedish 
insured dogs was about 13 cases per 10,000 
(0.13%) dog-years at risk.6
CLASSIFICATION AND ETIOLOGY
“TYPES” OF DIABETES MELLITUS
ere are two predominant forms of DM in 
people, one involving a permanent and abso-
lute loss of insulin and the other involving 
insulin resistance that may not be permanent. 
ese 2 conditions were referred to as “juvenile-
onset” and “adult-onset” DM, respectively, then 
updated to “insulin-dependent DM” (IDDM) 
and “non-insulin-dependent DM” (NIDDM). 
e terms type 1 and type 2 DM are now 
employed, with about 10% of diabetic people 
worldwide having type 1 disease and about 
90% with type 2, a condition almost identical 
to the previous NIDDM.7 Several classication 
systems for canine DM have been proposed 
based on the underlying cause of their beta-cell 
dysfunction.8 9 More recently, the European 
Society of Veterinary Endocrinology’s project 
(Agreeing Language in Veterinary Endocrinol-
ogy) proposed a common terminology (Box 
291.1).1 e most common form of DM in dogs 
resembles type 1 DM in people. It is characterized by permanent hypo-
insulinemia, no increase in endogenous serum insulin or C-peptide 
concentrations following administration of an insulin secretagogue 
(e.g., glucose, glucagon, amino acids), and an absolute need for exog-
enous insulin to control glycemia, avoid diabetic ketoacidosis (DKA, 
ch. 290), and survive.10 With rare exceptions, all diabetic dogs require 
exogenous insulin therapy to manage their hyperglycemia.
HISTOLOGY AND POSSIBLE PRIMARY CAUSES
Common histologic observations in pancreatic tissue from 
dogs with DM include reduced size and number of pancreatic 
islets and reduced number of beta cells within islets. Beta-cell 
vacuolization, enlargement, and degeneration are common.11 12
The cause of pancreatic beta-cell dysfunction or destruction 
in dogs has not been established. Genetic predisposition to 
DM is recognized and based on familial associations, pedigree 
analyses of Keeshonden and several other breeds, and genomic 
studies to identify susceptibility and protective major histo-
compatibility complex (MHC) haplotypes.2 13-15 A number of 
genes linked to susceptibility to DM in people are also asso-
ciated with increased risk in dogs.14-16 Canine DM has been 
associated with MHC class II genes (dog leukocyte antigen; 
DLA).17 Similar haplotypes and genotypes have been identi-
fied in the most susceptible breeds.
e American Diabetes Association has recommended sub-
categorizing human type 1 DM (T1DM) into types 1A (immune-
mediated) and 1B (idiopathic severe insulin deciency).18 Type 
1A DM is characterized by lymphocytic inltration of the islets, 
known as “insulitis,” and by the presence of serum autoantibod-
ies to pancreatic components (e.g., insulin, intracellular glutamic

Federico Fracassi

Diabetes Mellitus in Dogs

eBooks.Health.Elsevier.com
acid decarboxylase 65 [GAD65] or insulinoma antigen 2 [IA-2]) 
prior to the development of hyperglycemia.19 ere is evidence 
that a small percentage of diabetic dogs have an immune-
mediated component contributing to the beta cell loss, leading to 
insulin deciency. However, studies are conicting, and pancre-
atic lymphocytic inltration is rare in diabetic dogs.11 20-22 Also, 
circulating serum antibodies to beta-cell autoantigens, such as 
GAD65, IA-2 and insulin, were only rarely identied in aected 
dogs at diagnosis.11 21 23 us, autoimmunity may not be a con-
tributory factor in most dogs. A few dogs with juvenile-onset 
DM (i.e., onset <1 year of age) have been reported, with higher 
prevalence in some breeds, e.g., Labrador Retrievers.24 25 Most 
reports describe various pancreatic histopathologic abnormali-
ties in diabetic dogs, but no clear etiology. Congenital hypoplasia 
of islet beta cells has been described in Keeshond puppies and 
other breeds.26-28
EXOCRINE PANCREATIC DISEASE
DM may occur secondary to disease of the exocrine pancreas 
or any process that diusely injures the pancreas, most notably 
pancreatitis (chs. 276, 277), a recognized cause of human dia-
betes.29 e incidence of histologically identiable, oen severe 
pancreatitis in diabetic dogs ranges from 30-40%. It is a likely 
contributor to the development of DM and DKA (ch. 290).30-32
Any inammatory process involving exocrine pancreatic tissue 
could negatively impact beta-cell viability because beta cells are 
particularly sensitive to the action of proapoptotic mediators, 
such as tumor necrosis factor alpha.33-35
INSULIN-RESISTANT DM
Progesterone
Insulin-resistant DM can follow the antagonistic eects of the 
progesterone (Pg)-dominated phase of diestrus or pregnancy 
(see Females in Diestrus, below). Following estrus, all normal 
bitches (pregnant or not) enter the luteal, PG-dominated phase 
(diestrus) of their ovarian cycle. Diestrus lasts 60 to 90 days, every 
6 to 12 months. Pg stimulates growth hormone (GH) secretion 
from mammary tissue and both hormones antagonize insulin 
action.36 Older females are frequently diagnosed with DM while 
in diestrus (whether or not pregnant), when serum Pg and GH 
concentrations are increased.37-39 Treatment with ovariohysterec-
tomy removes the source of Pg and, in turn, the stimulus for GH 
secretion. If an adequate population of functional beta cells is still 
present in the pancreas, hyperglycemia may resolve without insu-
lin treatment. Failure to quickly correct insulin resistance oen 
results in progressive loss of beta cells and a greater likelihood of 
permanent insulin dependency.38
Glucocorticoids
Glucocorticoids cause insulin resistance and decrease insulin secre-
tion directly by reducing the incretin eect and by direct cytotoxic 
eects (ch. 293).40-43 DM had been documented in about 10% of 
dogs with hypercortisolism.44 45 In 1 study, 38% of dogs with hyper-
cortisolism had moderate hyperglycemia.46 Sustained hyperglyce-
mia has been shown to damage canine islets and lead to DM.47 It 
is possible that hypercortisolism merely exposes pre-existing pan-
creatic damage that has otherwise remained subclinical, which can 
explain the progression of DM in some but not all dogs.
Obesity
Despite the increasing prevalence of obesity in dogs, there is no evi-
dence that insulin resistance unmasks beta-cell dysfunction result-
ing in type 2 DM as in people and cats (chs. 20, 150). Obese dogs 
show evidence of insulin resistance but compensate appropriately 
through increased insulin secretion.48
“HONEYMOON PERIOD”
A “honeymoon period” occurs in some dogs with newly diagnosed 
DM, characterized by excellent glycemic control in response to 
small doses of insulin (<0.2 U/kg/injection), presumably due to 
residual beta-cell function. Glycemic control in these dogs usually 
becomes more dicult (the honeymoon ends), requiring increas-
ing insulin doses within months of beginning treatment, as the 
residual functioning beta cells die and fail to be replaced.49
PATHOPHYSIOLOGY
DM is the result of a relative or absolute insulin deciency. Insu-
lin deciency causes decreased tissue utilization of glucose, amino 
acids, and fatty acids, accelerated hepatic glycogenolysis and gluco-
neogenesis, and accumulation of glucose in the circulation, caus-
ing hyperglycemia.50 Intestinal glucose absorption and glucose 
entry into erythrocytes, kidney or brain is not aected. As renal 
tubular capacity for glucose reabsorption is exceeded, usually when 
blood glucose (BG) concentrations are >180 to 220 mg/dL (>10-
12.2 mmol/L), glycosuria occurs. Glucose-induced osmotic diure-
sis causes polyuria (PU), water loss, and activation of the thirst 
mechanism leading to polydipsia (PD). e negative calorie bal-
ance resulting from an inability to utilize glucose, loss of calories in 
urine, and tissue catabolism leads to polyphagia (PP).
e physiological consequences of DM are similar to those of 
starvation. Protein metabolism shis from synthesis to proteolysis 
and available amino acids increase hepatic gluconeogenesis. ese 
are short-term protective mechanisms. But, in DM, enhanced glu-
cose synthesis exacerbates hyperglycemia, creates negative nitrogen 
balance, decreases muscle mass, and eventually leads to cachexia (ch. 
151).51 e continuing lack of insulin prevents glucose from entering 
cells, accelerates lipid catabolism, mobilizes triglycerides, and causes 
increased levels of plasma free fatty acids (FFAs). FFAs are transported 
to the liver where they undergo beta-oxidation to acetyl coenzyme A 
(CoA), which may exceed the need for adenosine triphosphate pro-
duction via oxidation in the Krebs cycle. Excess acetyl CoA is metabo-
lized into ketones. In starvation, ketones are an acceptable short-term 
source for energy but aer prolonged starvation or in DM, excessive 
production of ketones can lead to ketosis and, in DM, DKA. Increased 
hepatic concentration of FFAs also increases hepatic synthesis of both 
triglycerides and very low density lipoproteins (VLDLs), causing 
hyperlipidemia (chs. 63, 156) and hepatic lipidosis (ch. 274).
SIGNALMENT
DM is most commonly diagnosed in middle-aged and older 
dogs, 5 to 12 years of age.1-6 Rarely, DM is diagnosed in a juve-
nile dog.26-28 Peak prevalence is 7 to 10 years; most studies place 
females at a greater risk.2-6 Regional dierences in breed popu-
larity or variation in neutering practices may inuence sex pre-
dispositions within a population, although associations identied 
between neutering and diagnosis of DM also vary. Genetic predis-
position for or against the development of DM in dogs has been 
suggested (Table 291.1), e.g., Boxer dogs are seldom reported 
in epidemiologic studies on DM. Genetic dierences within the 
same breed due to dierent blood-lines also impact predisposi-
tions, e.g., Swedish and Norwegian Elkhounds, Australian Terri-
ers, and Samoyeds have the highest incidence of DM in Sweden 
while English Setter dogs are overrepresented in Italy.6,52
ANAMNESIS
Dogs with DM almost always have the classical clinical signs of 
PU/PD/PP and weight loss. PU/PD are constant in diabetic dogs. 
PP may be diminished by a concurrent condition that decreases 
appetite (e.g., pancreatitis, ketosis, DKA; chs. 277 and 290). 


---

## Page 2128

SECTION XIX
•
Endocrine Disease
1976
eBooks.Health.Elsevier.com
Weight loss may not be noticed in dogs that have only recently 
become diabetic. e most common reasons for dogs with undi-
agnosed DM to be brought to their veterinarian are their need to 
urinate at night or because they urinate in the home. In the event 
that a dog has free access to urinate out-of-doors, owner concerns 
oen include weight loss, ravenous appetite, sudden blindness 
caused by cataract formation (Figure 291.1), or signs associated 
with DKA (e.g., lethargy, anorexia, vomiting, weakness) (ch. 
290). Time from onset of clinical signs to development of DKA is 
unpredictable, ranging from days to months, and may be depen-
dent on type and severity of concurrent disorders causing insulin 
resistance and/or stimulating ketone production.
PHYSICAL EXAMINATION
Physical examination (PE) ndings depend on the duration 
of DM, presence of concomitant disorders, and whether DKA 
has developed. A thorough PE (ch. 3) is critically important, 
especially for identifying any concomitant disease. Dogs with 
“uncomplicated” DM are usually in good condition and their PE 
may be unremarkable. Diabetic dogs can be of normal weight, 
underweight or obese. Lethargy may be evident. e haircoat in 
newly diagnosed or poorly controlled diabetics may be dull. Hep-
atomegaly due to diabetes-induced hepatic lipidosis is commonly 
palpable. Cataracts are also common (ch. 13). Anterior uveitis 
and keratoconjunctivitis sicca may be present. e neurologic 
signs commonly observed in diabetic cats (e.g., weakness in the 
hind limbs, ataxia, plantigrade stance) are uncommon in dogs.
DIAGNOSIS
DIABETES MELLITUS AND KETOACIDOSIS (DKA)
e diagnosis of DM is conrmed in a dog with classic clini-
cal signs (with no other plausible cause) and random (fasted or 
unfasted) BG >200 mg/dL (>11.1 mmol/L).1 In some, clinical signs 
may not be reported by owners. If there is uncertainty regarding 
clinical signs, the diagnosis of DM can be conrmed by nding per-
sistent hyperglycemia over several days and/or documentation of 
increased glycated proteins and/or glycosuria.1 To diagnose DM in 
dogs with fasting BGs >130 mg/dL but ≤200 mg/dL (>7.2 mmol/L 
but <11.1 mmol/L), persistent fasting hyperglycemia on separate 
days or increased glycated proteins must again be documented.1
Testing for disorders that cause insulin resistance is indicated if a 
stable unstressed dog has mild but persistent fasting hyperglyce-
mia. Concurrent evidence of ketonuria establishes a diagnosis of 
diabetic ketosis (DK), whereas illness, ketonuria, and metabolic 
acidosis with increased anion gap conrms DKA (ch. 290).
COMPLETE DIAGNOSTIC EVALUATION
Once DM is diagnosed, it is extremely important to thoroughly 
evaluate the dog’s overall health in an attempt to identify clues for 
conditions that may have contributed to DM development (e.g., 
Cushing’s syndrome [ch. 293], hypothyroidism [ch. 285], pancre-
atitis [chs. 276-277]) or conditions resulting from DM (e.g., weight 
loss, weakness, urinary tract infection [ch. 307]). Minimum labora-
tory evaluation in dogs with newly diagnosed DM should include 
a complete blood count (CBC), a serum biochemical prole that 
includes a serum fructosamine concentration, and urinalysis (UA) 
with bacterial culture. Serum Pg concentrations should be assayed 
in any intact diabetic female. Abdominal ultrasound (US) is an 
excellent tool to search for evidence of pancreatitis, adrenal enlarge-
ment or masses, pyometra, ovarian cysts, and other concerns. 
Because of the prevalence of pancreatitis in DM, canine pancreatic 
lipase immunoreactivity (cPLI) could be considered, especially if 
US is unavailable. Other tests may be indicated (Box 291.2).
SUCCESSFUL MANAGEMENT STRATEGIES
INITIAL PLAN AND THERAPEUTIC GOALS
The Team Approach
Successful treatment of DM in a dog requires excellent owner 
compliance and owner/veterinary team interaction. e “owner” 
may be one person or several. e veterinary members of the team 
who will be involved in the long-term care of these dogs should 
include veterinarians, technicians, and non-technical personnel. 
e owner should be thoroughly educated regarding DM, how it 
is managed dierently in dogs when compared with people, and

the goals of therapy. Caring for a dog with DM can have a negative 
impact on the dog’s owner. e veterinarian must be able to com-
municate with the owner in the most appropriate way and should 
try to modify therapeutic strategies as much as possible to achieve 
success as dened by the dog’s owner (ch. 1).
Understanding and Responding to Common Owner 
Concerns
Establishing and agreeing on the denition of “successful manage-
ment” is extremely important (ch. 1). For most veterinarians, their 
primary goal in the long-term management of DM should be to have 
the owner pleased with the health of their dog. is is usually achieved 
if PU can be eliminated or signicantly reduced, body weight is stable 
and acceptable, and PP is tolerable. If the dog is dull at diagnosis, it is 
important for the dog to interact “normally” with others in the home. 
Open dialogue provides the best chance for owner adherence and will 
help avoid cessation of therapy or euthanasia. A survey of 1192 veteri-
narians reported that a median of 1 in 10 pets with DM are euthanized 
at diagnosis and another 1 in 10 are euthanized within 1 year of due to 
“lack of success or compliance.”53 One study described the social and 
psychological impact of DM on the quality of life of both dogs and 
owners.54 Aspects reported as most negatively impacting the owners’ 
quality of life included: general worry about DM, diculties in leav-
ing the dog with friends or family, worry about the dog becoming 
blind, boarding diculties, concerns about hypoglycemia, changed 
social and working life, costs of treating a diabetic, worry about future 
care, and owners wanting more control. Knowing these concerns can 
help veterinarians address key issues and concerns to adapt a treat-
ment and monitoring protocol specic for each owner, e.g., one can 
introduce intensive glucose monitoring (i.e., the use of continuous 
glucose monitoring devices) for owners who want control. Alterna-
tively, the clinician can simplify treatment for owners worried about 
their social or working life. Reviewing expenses carefully can address 
a top priority for owners concerned about costs. Avoiding tight glu-
cose regulation is an approach that can allay concerns for owners 
who are worried about hypoglycemia. While these measures may 
be imperfect, they can be ways of prolonging the life of the pet, with 
a good quality of life, via nding an approach that is sustainable for 
individual owners (and not triggering a decision to euthanize).
WHEN TO BEGIN INSULIN THERAPY AND AVOIDING 
HYPOGLYCEMIA
Once diagnosed, treatment for DM should begin. Dogs in good 
condition and with an excellent appetite can be managed as 
“uncomplicated diabetics,” even in the presence of ketonuria. e 
goals of therapy are to reduce or resolve clinical signs, prevent 
short-term complications (e.g., hypoglycemia, DKA), achieve 
the previously discussed areas of clinical improvement, and have 
good quality life. In DM dogs, this can be accomplished with 
exogenous insulin therapy, an appropriate diet, exercise, preven-
tion or control of concurrent inammatory, infectious, neoplas-
tic or hormonal disorders, and avoidance of insulin-antagonistic 
drugs (ch. 224). Aiming for normal or near-normal BG concen-
trations is not recommended, as it increases risk of hypoglycemia, 
a common, serious, and potentially life-threatening complication. 
Hypoglycemia is usually the result of overzealous insulin therapy. 
Most DM dogs are described as doing well if their BGs are main-
tained between 80 to 250 mg/dL (4.4-13.9 mmol/L).
ITEMS TO INCLUDE WHEN EDUCATING OWNERS
General Concepts
Owners should be aware of changes in water intake, urine output, 
appetite and body weight caused by DM. Poor appetite, vomit-
ing, diarrhea or unusual lethargy is always worrisome and worth 
veterinary consultation. It should be emphasized that consistency 
in timing of injections, meals, type and amount of food, and exer-
cise will make control of DM easier. Owners must learn the signs 
of hypoglycemia and DKA. Unfortunately, many dogs with DM 
become blind from cataract formation within 12 to 18 months 
of diagnosis (Figure 291.1). Treatment should follow a precise 
and understandable protocol (Box 291.3). While owners are the 
key to good DM control, decisions regarding insulin, dose and 
frequency of administration must always be made by the owner/
veterinary team.
Figure 291.1
Bilateral cataracts causing blindness in a diabetic dog.

Dogs With “Uncomplicated” Diabetes Mellitus
Complete Blood Count
1. Typically normal
2. Neutrophilic leukocytosis, toxic neutrophils if there is inflamma-
tion (pancreatitis, infection, etal.)
Biochemistry Panel
1. Hyperglycemia
2. Hypercholesterolemia
3. Hypertriglyceridemia (lipemia)
4. Increased alanine aminotransferase activity (typically <500 
IU/L)
5. Increased alkaline phosphatase activity (typically <500 IU/L)
Urinalysis
1. Urine specific gravity typically >1.025
2. Glycosuria
3. Variable ketonuria
4. Proteinuria
5. Bacteriuria
Ancillary Tests
1. Hyperlipasemia if pancreatitis is present
2. Serum trypsin-like immunoreactivity usually normal
a. Low with pancreatic exocrine insufficiency
3. Pancreatic lipase immunoreactivity usually normal
a. High with acute pancreatitis
b. Normal to high with chronic pancreatitis
4. Variable serum baseline insulin concentration
a. Insulin-dependent diabetes mellitus: low, normal
b. Insulin resistance induced: low, normal, increased

The Insulin
Insulin should be stored in a refrigerator in an upright position. 
Insulin must be mixed correctly. Vetsulin/Caninsulin should be 
shaken, some insulins do not require mixing, and some insulin 
products should be gently rolled. Owners must use appropriate 
syringes (40 U/mL for veterinary insulin products and 100 U/mL 
for human insulin products), learn to eliminate air bubbles, and 
administer the insulin subcutaneously (SQ) over the lateral wall 
of the chest. e confusing use of nonmatching syringes based on 
conversion tables for U40 and U100/mL or the owner’s calculation 
should be avoided. Oen U40 insulin syringes have scales in both 
mL and Units; the owner must be instructed to use the Units scale. 
e injection technique should be demonstrated until the owner 
is comfortable. If an insulin pen device is used for administration, 
owners must be specically trained. Written instructions are useful. 
Owners of DM dogs should be encouraged to keep detailed records 
of their dogs’ BG, urine glucose, urine ketones, and any change in 
other parameters.
Summary of the Protocol for Managing Diabetic Dogs
○
Diagnosis of diabetes mellitus (history, physical examination, 
hyperglycemia, glycosuria, increased fructosamine)
○
Routine laboratory evaluation (complete blood count, serum 
biochemistry, urine analysis, urine culture)
○
Abdominal ultrasonography if indicated
○
Canine pancreatic lipase immunoreactivity if indicated
○
Cessation of diabetogenic drugs
○
Administration of intermediate-acting insulin (Caninsulin/
Vetsulin, NPH): . U/kg qh SQ
○
Institution of treatment for concurrent problems (e.g., urinary 
tract infection)
•
Prescribe a commercial “diabetic” diet. Feed meals of equal size
just before each insulin injection. If overweight, aim for 1% weight 
loss/week. High-fiber, low calorie diets should not be fed to thin 
or emaciated diabetic dogs until glucose control is established and 
normal body weight is attained using a calorie-dense, lower-fiber 
diet designed for maintenance. Dietary recommendations for con-
current disorders (e.g., chronic kidney disease, food allergy, pan-
creatitis) have priority over specific diabetic diets.
•
Owner instructions (requires at least hour)
○
With intact bitches make an appointment with the owner for 
spaying. This procedure should be done soon as possible.
○
Inform about home monitoring options.
○
If an owner is motivated and the dog is quiet, discuss with the 
owner the application of a sensor of the flash glucose monitor-
ing system (FGMS) (FreeStyle Libre).
○
Provide written instructions
Re-evaluation  Week After Diagnosis
•
History, physical examination, body weight
In dogs monitored with blood glucose curve (BGC):
○
Administration of food and insulin at the clinic. For dogs that 
are unwilling to eat at the clinic, food and insulin are given at 
home, and the BGC started upon arrival at the clinic (as soon as

○

Owner insulin administration technique should be evaluated.

•

important variable for evaluation of metabolic control)

DIETARY RECOMMENDATIONS (CH. 155)
Goals and General Content
Food given to DM dogs must be palatable, nutritionally complete, 
and be consistent in composition, ingredients, and calories. ese 
factors improve the chance for consistent insulin requirements and 
response. Ideally, near optimal body condition should be achieved 
and maintained. A dog with DM may be overweight or under-
weight. In either, near ideal weight is a reasonable goal. Insulin is an 
anabolic hormone and insulin-treated diabetics gain weight easily, 
making weight loss in an obese dog with DM dicult. Weight loss 
diets should be considered for obese DM dogs as they are typically 
rich in insoluble ber, low in fat, and have decreased caloric density. 
Such diets should not be fed to underweight DM dogs. Once glu-
cose control is established and a near-normal body weight attained, 
owners should provide a consistent calorie-dense and lower-ber 
diet designed for maintenance.50 Several commercial prescription 
diets are available for DM dogs. To minimize postprandial hyper-
glycemia, no diet should contain signicant amounts of simple 
sugar. Calories should be derived primarily from complex carbohy-
drates and proteins. e amount of fat in the diet should be mini-
mized to avoid increasing circulating cholesterol, triglycerides, free 
glycerol, and FFAs.55 Dietary fat should be particularly restricted in 
any dog with a history of pancreatitis or persistent hyperlipidemia.
Fiber Content
Several, but not all, studies evaluating the role of high-ber diets in 
the management of DM dogs demonstrated improved glycemic con-
trol.56-58 One study failed to demonstrate benet from feeding nor-
mal weight DM dogs a high-ber diet.55 Most diets for DM contain 
a blend of soluble and insoluble bers. Common complications of 
feeding high-ber diets include increased frequency of defecation, 
constipation, and obstipation (insoluble ber); so to watery stools 
and excessive atulence (soluble ber).50 Problems with palatability 
can result from switching diets too quickly and can be avoided by 
gradually transitioning from one diet to another. With time, dogs 
may become less interested in ber-rich diets. eir palatability can 
be improved by adding minced boiled meat. Concurrent conditions 
may take priority over DM in diet choices (i.e., inammatory bowel 
disease, chronic kidney disease, food allergy/intolerance).
Feeding Frequency
To simplify management, most DM dogs should be fed 2 equal-sized 
meals daily, about every 12 hours. In dogs with a consistently good 
appetite, food can be given immediately aer the insulin injection, 
helping the dog associate the unpleasant injection with the more plea-
surable meal. In dogs with poor appetite, food can be given imme-
diately prior to giving insulin to avoid having dog the refuse to eat 
aer receiving a full dose of insulin. Ad libitum feeding is not recom-
mended, but nicky eaters should be fed at the time of insulin admin-
istration and have any uneaten food available throughout the day.
EXERCISE
Consistent exercise usually makes DM management easier and 
should be encouraged. Physical activity decreases BG concentrations 
by increasing absorption of insulin from its injection site, increasing 
blood ow and insulin delivery to exercising muscles, stimulating 
translocation (i.e., upregulation) of glucose transporters (primarily 
GLUT-4) in muscle cells, and increasing glucose disposal without need 
for more insulin.59 60 Sporadic strenuous exercise can induce hypogly-
cemia and should be avoided. When the occasional intense exercise 
(i.e., hunting) is planned for a DM dog, reduce the morning dose of 
insulin 50%. e percent reduction needed to prevent hypoglycemia 
is not predictable and adjustments should be based on experience, e.g., 
symptomatic hypoglycemia or PU during or aer exercise. Owners of 
DM dogs should always have a glucose source readily available in case 
signs of hypoglycemia are observed during physical activity.
INSULIN CHOICES
OVERVIEW
Various types of insulin are used to treat DM long-term. Based on 
duration of action and potency, they include intermediate-acting 
(porcine lente, neutral protamine Hagedorn [NPH]) and long-
acting insulins (protamine zinc insulin [PZI], insulin glargine, 
insulin detemir) (Table 291.2). Lente (Vetsulin, Caninsulin) is 
a porcine-origin zinc 40 U/mL insulin consisting of 30% short-
acting amorphous insulin and 70% long-acting, microcrystalline 
insulin. NPH (100 U/mL; Humulin N, Novolin N) is recombinant 
human insulin, usually administered q12h. A long-acting veteri-
nary PZI 40 U/mL insulin is made with recombinant human insu-
lin (ProZinc). is insulin may be useful for some dogs in whom 
duration of intermediate-acting insulins is too short.64 Glargine 
and detemir are synthetic long-acting insulin analogues designed 
to maintain basal insulin concentrations in diabetic people. eir 
use in dogs is increasing. An ultra-long-acting novel SQ insulin 
formulation given once weekly, not yet approved, controlled BG 
concentrations in 4 diabetic dogs.68 e ecacy and safety of this 
novel formulation must be further assessed in larger clinical trials.
INSULIN ANALOGUES
Glargine
Insulin analogues are synthetic modied human insulin peptides 
with similar physiologic eects. ey are commonly used in treat-
ing cats and dogs with DM.69 e amino acid asparagine in human 
insulin is replaced with glycine at position A21 and 2 positively 
charged arginine molecules are added to the C-terminus of the B 
chain to create insulin glargine (Lantus).70 is structure results in 
low aqueous solubility at neutral pH but complete solubility at a 
pH of 4. e acidic glargine solution injected into the pH-neutral 
milieu of the SQ forms insulin-analogue-microprecipitates that 
slow absorption and delays onset of action. e result is a relatively 
constant, peakless, basal supply of insulin.70 Due to the importance 
of pH, glargine should never be diluted or mixed with any solution 
that could alter pH. In dogs, insulin glargine has been shown to be 
safe and ecacious for good to moderate glycemic control.55,56
Insulin glargine is also available in a 300 U/mL (Toujeo) formu-
lation. Given the high concentration, it can only be administered 
using an insulin-dosing pen. Glargine 300 U/mL was compared to 
insulin detemir in 8 dogs and it was found to have a longer duration 
of action and a relatively peakless time-action prole.70a Glargine 
300 U/mL is now one of the author’s rst-choice insulin for treat-
ing dogs with DM. e initial dose should be 0.5 U/kg once daily. 
In one study 54% of dogs remained on once-daily insulin requir-
ing a median nal dose of 1.9 U/kg (0.2-5.2) once daily. Forty-six 
percent remained on this insulin, but the insulin was given q12h.70b
e decision for q12h is based on glucose concentrations not being 
controlled for 24 hours. When switching from once daily to q12h 
dosing, it is recommended that the dose is decreased by about 30% 
per injection (i.e., the total daily dose is increased by 40%). A small 
percentage of dogs are best managed by adding NPH (0.25 U/kg 
SC) insulin aer meals to the Toujeo. e decision to add pre-meal 
NPH is based on a substantial period of high glucose >300 mg/dL 
(>16.7 mmol/L) that consistently occurs for several hours following 
1 or more meals, while there is otherwise good glycemic control.70b
Detemir
Removing the amino acid threonine from position 30 of insulin’s 
B chain and then adding a 14-carbon fatty acid (myristic acid) 
to the amino acid lysine at B chain position 29 creates insulin 
detemir (Levemir). ese modications permit reversible bind-
ing to albumin, slowing absorption and prolonging its consis-
tent metabolic eect for as long as 24 hours in people.71 In dogs, 
insulin detemir is about 4 times more potent than other insulin 


---

## Page 2132

SECTION XIX
•
Endocrine Disease
1980
eBooks.Health.Elsevier.com
preparations. Insulin detemir, given SQ q12h, has been used to 
treat dogs with DM. Due to the higher potency of this insulin in 
dogs, lower dosages are required. A starting dose of 0.1 U/kg q12h 
is recommended. Detemir should be used with caution, especially 
in small dogs.57 Use of diluent specically designed for this insu-
lin allows safer delivery of small doses.
STORING, MIXING AND DILUTING INSULIN
Freezing or heating inactivates insulin. While storing insulin at 
room temperature is acceptable, it is safest for owners to store insu-
lin inside the refrigerator door. is maintains a consistent stor-
age environment, bottle to bottle. Some veterinarians recommend 
replacing insulin monthly to avoid inactivation or contamination. 
However, the “shelf-life” of commercially produced insulin, stored 
and handled appropriately, is far longer. Problems with loss of insu-
lin potency or sterility are extremely rare and monthly replacement 
is not recommended. While shaking rather than gentle mixing was 
thought to inactivate insulin, recent studies again demonstrate that 
insulin is resistant to being destroyed in this manner. For consis-
tency, however, gentle rolling is suggested for most insulin prod-
ucts. Vetsulin/Caninsulin should be “shaken thoroughly” until a 
homogeneous, uniformly milky suspension is seen (Video 291.1). 
Diluting insulin can be quite useful in small dogs, particularly 
when using the potent detemir. Only diluent solutions provided by 
the manufacturer should be used. It is important to emphasize that 
insulin glargine is pH-dependent and should not be diluted.
INSULIN PEN DEVICES
Dosing pens, designed for use by diabetic people, are also used in 
diabetic dogs and cats. In humans, insulin dosing pens have been 
shown to decrease anxiety, discomfort, and social embarrassment 
associated with insulin administration.72 Pens must be stored with-
out needles attached to prevent air from entering and “diluting” the 
insulin, also explaining the need for “priming” the pen before each 
use. One or 2 units must be loaded and then ejected while keeping 
the pen in an upright position. Lente porcine insulin is the only 
veterinary insulin product marketed with pens. Pens are available 
in 2 sizes: a maximum 8 U/dose VetPen (0.5 to 8 U) with 0.5 U 
dose increments and a maximum 16 U/dose VetPen (1 to 16 U) 
with 1 U increments. e insulin pen-injectors [e.g., VetPen (0.5 to 
8 U)] were more accurate at lower dosages (≤2 U) and syringes were 
more precise for higher dosages in 1 study.73 A prospective study 
reported that half of DM dog owners preferred using syringes and 
half preferred insulin pen devices.74 In humans, it is recommended 
that the needle be kept in the SQ for 10 seconds during dosing. For 
the low insulin doses typically used in dogs, the injection requires 
a few seconds and a 5-second wait is considered sucient. Pen 
needles are usually between 29 and 32 gauge and come in dier-
ent lengths, ranging from 4 mm to 12.7 mm. Longer needles are 
recommended for dogs to avoid intradermal injections. Owners 
choosing to use insulin dosing pens must be trained in their assem-
blage (Video 291.2) and use (Video 291.3).
INITIAL INSULIN RECOMMENDATIONS
e rst insulin preparations recommended for dogs with newly 
diagnosed DM are from the intermediate-acting group, such 
as porcine lente (Vetsulin, Caninsulin) or recombinant human 
NPH. With either, one should begin with about 0.25 U/kg SQ 
q12h. is conservative approach should be sucient to prevent 
DKA or symptomatic hypoglycemia, either of which might be 
quite distressing to an owner. It is not recommended to increase 
insulin dose in these initial days if glucoses remain high, because 
insulin action may improve aer a few days (so-called equilib-
rium).51 Insulin therapy is usually started on an outpatient basis, 
which is less expensive than hospitalization. As discussed under 
the heading “Glargine,” the 300 U/mL formulation is becoming a 
rst-choice insulin.
FEMALES IN DIESTRUS
Intact bitches diagnosed with DM should undergo ovariohys-
terectomy as soon as feasible, ideally 1 to 3 days aer starting 
insulin.51 Ovariohysterectomy is most important if the bitch is

in diestrus and under the inuence of Pg, because removing the 
ovaries should reduce insulin resistance by eliminating Pg and, 
in a few dogs, remission of DM may occur aer a few days or 
weeks.75 All intact bitches with DM should be spayed, even if no 
obvious relationship is established between diestrus and onset of 
DM. Aer spaying, glycemic control should be closely monitored 
with appropriate adjustments in insulin dosage. Although spaying 
does not lead to remission of DM in most dogs, it does prevent 
Pg-induced secretion of mammary-derived GH in subsequent 
cycles, insulin resistance, and worrisome decompensation. If 
spaying is not possible, administration of the Pg receptor antago-
nist aglepristone may reduce insulin resistance.76
CONCURRENT CONDITIONS
Concurrent inammatory, infectious, neoplastic and metabolic 
conditions are common in dogs newly diagnosed with DM. Many 
of these conditions interfere with cellular responsiveness to insu-
lin, negatively impacting DM management (ch. 224). Concurrent 
disorders and diabetogenic drugs can cause insulin resistance by 
altering insulin metabolism (pre-receptor issues), decreasing con-
centration or binding anity of cell membrane insulin receptors 
(receptor issues), interfering with the insulin receptor signaling 
cascade (post-receptor issues), or any of these in combination.50
Insulin resistance can be mild (e.g., obesity) to severe (e.g., Cush-
ing’s syndrome) or may uctuate (e.g., recurring pancreatitis). 
orough evaluation (history, PE and in-hospital testing) of dogs 
newly diagnosed with DM is extremely important. Identication 
and treatment of concurrent diseases are integral to successful 
management of DM. Some conditions (e.g., chronic pancreati-
tis) are, like DM, never permanently resolved and require long-
term management. Owners should be informed that treating 
DM alone can be challenging. Concurrent conditions can make 
successful therapy even more dicult. Frequent monitoring and 
dosage adjustments are almost always required. Administration 
of glucocorticoids or Pgs should be discontinued and alternative 
medications used if possible (ch. 224). If glucocorticoids are abso-
lutely required, dosage and frequency should be kept as low and 
consistent as possible.
MONITORING DIABETES MELLITUS
OVERVIEW
Long-term care of diabetics and insulin dose adjustments should 
be based, primarily, on owner observations supplemented by 
periodic in-hospital examinations. e in-hospital assessments 
should include body weight, PE, BG, and fructosamine concen-
trations. Glucose levels and glucose curves should initially be 
carried out in-clinic. Subsequently, if appropriately trained, most 
owners are able to perform home monitoring by measuring capil-
lary BG with a portable BG meter (PBGM). Motivated owners 
with a quiet dog can learn about the ash glucose monitoring sys-
tem (FGMS) (FreeStyle Libre).
FREQUENCY OF IN-HOSPITAL EVALUATIONS (BOX 291.3)
Veterinary team members must inform owners that most dogs 
require a minimum of 1 to 2 months to achieve reasonable and 
stable glycemic control. In some, the process is straightforward 
and takes little time. In others, adjustments in insulin and other 
factors never cease. Periodic monitoring is used to help ensure 
safe and eective treatment. Initially, reevaluations are recom-
mended at 1, 2 to 3, 3 to 4, and 8 to 10 weeks aer diagnosis. Dogs 
are then usually examined about every 4 months, or as needed.
REPEATED HISTORIES AND PHYSICAL EXAMINATIONS
e most important parameter for assessing glycemic control is 
owner opinion, specically status of their dog’s PU/PD/PP, body 
weight, and general health. Body weight is emphasized since 
insulin-underdosed diabetics tend to lose weight and insulin-
overdosed diabetics tend to gain weight. Well-controlled dia-
betics have stable, near ideal, body weight. Objective ndings 
on PE should be considered in the context of owner obser-
vations. If the owner is satised, body weight stable, and PE 
consistent with good glycemic control, further testing is only 
directed at avoiding overdose. Owners should specically 
report any possible hypoglycemic episodes (e.g., weakness, 
ataxia, “acting intoxicated”). Persistence or recurrence of clini-
cal signs or unwanted change in weight are suggestive of poor 
glycemic control and/or presence of a concurrent disease. A 
serum fructosamine and BG curves (BGCs) may help charac-
terize the issue, guide changes in treatment, or indicate need for 
additional testing.
SERUM FRUCTOSAMINE AND GLYCATED HEMOGLOBIN 
(HbA1c)
Background
Serum fructosamine and glycated hemoglobin (HbA1c) are gly-
cated proteins synthesized from irreversible non-enzymatic bind-
ing of glucose to serum proteins or hemoglobin in red blood cells 
(RBCs), respectively. In dogs, serum fructosamine reects the 
mean BG concentration over the preceding 1 to 2 weeks, while 
HbA1c provides an index of the average BG concentration over 
the preceding 2 to 3 months.77,78 Serum fructosamine and HbA1c 
are not aected by acute changes in BG. Higher average BG con-
centrations result in greater amounts of blood fructosamines and 
HbA1c. Hypoproteinemia, azotemia, hypoalbuminemia, hyper-
lipidemia, and hemolysis can decrease measured fructosamine, 
independent of BG concentrations.79,80 Increased serum fructos-
amines have been observed in hypothyroidism and in 2 dogs with 
hyperglobulinemia caused by multiple myeloma.81,82 HbA1c may 
be decreased in conditions that shorten RBC lifespans or increase 
erythrocyte turnover.83,84 Falsely increased HbA1c values might 
occur with extreme hypertriglyceridemia and hyperbilirubine-
mia, under conditions that prolong erythrocyte lifespan, or that 
are associated with decreased erythrocyte turnover (e.g., iron 
deciency anemia and splenic disorders).83,84 Each laboratory 
should generate its own reference range for serum fructosamine 
and HbA1c.
How to Best Use Results
It is important to remember that neither normoglycemia nor nor-
mal fructosamine or HbA1c results should be therapeutic targets 
when treating dogs for DM. BGs in well-controlled DM dogs uc-
tuate between normal and slightly above renal threshold. Well-
controlled dogs usually have some glycosuria and are moderately 
hyperglycemic throughout the day. us, serum fructosamines or 
HbA1c within reference intervals (especially in the lower half) are 
more suggestive of prolonged episodes of hypoglycemia due to 
insulin overdose than of excellent control. Serum fructosamine 
or HbA1c should be, ideally, slightly above the reference range. 
In a study comparing serum glycated protein concentrations with 
glycemic control in 46 dogs with DM, serum fructosamine and 
HbA1c correctly identied the level of glycemic control in only 
50% and 44%, respectively, conrming the poor performance 
of both glycated proteins in classifying glycemic control.85 For 
these reasons, serum fructosamine or HbA1c should never be 
used as the sole indicator of glycemic control and should always 


---

## Page 2134

SECTION XIX
•
Endocrine Disease
1982
eBooks.Health.Elsevier.com
be interpreted in context of owner opinion, clinical signs, body 
weight, and BG concentrations. Establishing a baseline glycated 
protein concentration for each dog and then monitoring trends 
rather than absolute changes is the most appropriate use of gly-
cated protein measurements in diabetic dogs.
URINE GLUCOSE MONITORING
Daily owner monitoring of urine for sugar and ketones can be use-
ful. Most diabetics have varying amounts of glucose present in vir-
tually every urine sample, with an occasional negative. Persistent 
absence of sugar may be indicative of insulin overdosage or excel-
lent control. Owners should not adjust insulin dosage on the basis 
of morning urine glucose because this commonly leads to overdose 
and increases likelihood of insulin-induced hypoglycemia.
Ketonuria suggests inadequate control and decompensation. 
Upon seeing ketones in the urine of a pet who rarely has them, the 
owner should contact the veterinarian (ch. 290).
RANDOMLY OBTAINED SINGLE BLOOD GLUCOSE
A single BG is rarely useful in monitoring DM unless the result is 
quite low, which always indicates overdose. Single glucose mea-
surements may be sucient when an owner believes the dog to 
be asymptomatic, the PE is unremarkable, and serum fructos-
amine concentrations indicate good glycemic control (e.g., <100 
mcmol/L above the laboratory upper reference range). In such 
dogs, BGs of 180-250 mg/dL around the time of the insulin injec-
tion are consistent with good glycemic control and additional BG 
measurements are not usually necessary.
PORTABLE BLOOD GLUCOSE METERS (PBGMs)
During the initial adjustment phase and in subsequent long-
term management phases of therapy, if signs of DM persist, signs 
recur, or fructosamine concentrations are high, single BGs should 
be avoided and a BGC obtained. Serial BGCs can guide rational 
adjustments in insulin therapy. BGs are typically determined by 
a hand-held PBGM. To avoid multiple venipunctures, one may 
collect capillary blood from the ear (Videos 291.4 and 291.5; ch. 
78).86 Several PBGMs are commercially available. e accuracy of 
PBGMs designed for people varies considerably when used in dogs, 
but some are suciently accurate and precise to monitor BGs in 
dogs.86-91 Most devices give lower results than laboratory reference 
methods. is bias may result in an incorrect diagnosis of hypogly-
cemia or the misconception that the animal’s glycemic control is 
better than it actually is. e AlphaTRAK PBGM (Abbott Animal 
Health) was designed for use in dogs and cats and is more accurate 
and precise in dogs than devices designed for human use.88 90 91
Additional advantages of the AlphaTRAK are the small volumes 
of blood needed (0.3 mcL) and the extended measurement range 
(20-750 mg/dL). e AlphaTRAK PBGM tends to overestimate BG 
values, potentially missing hypoglycemia. e meter is less accu-
rate in dogs with hematocrits <30% or with erythrocytosis.92 93 Vet-
Mate, another veterinary PBGM, is accurate and requires 0.4 mcL 
of blood for BG measurement.91 If there is any doubt regarding 
accuracy, BGs can be compared with laboratory results.
BLOOD GLUCOSE CURVES (BGCs) AND INSULIN DOSAGE 
ADJUSTMENTS
Protocol
When conducting a BGC, the dog should be seen early in the 
morning and BGs sampled every 1 to 2 hours throughout the 
day. One should begin just prior to the rst insulin dose and 
continue until the next dose is due. Most dogs respond similarly 
to insulin morning and evening.94 If dierent day vs night BGs 
are suspected (e.g., good BGC and control of clinical signs dur-
ing the day but presence of PU/PD during the night), a 24-hour 
BGC or use of a continuous blood glucose monitoring (CGM) 
device should be considered.50 When performing a BGC, the 
owner’s insulin and feeding schedules should be maintained. 
Poor appetite limits the value of any BGC. Food and insulin can 
be administered at the clinic aer the rst BG measurement. If 
a dog refuses to eat in the clinic, the BGC should be abandoned. 
Subsequently, the owner should feed the dog at home, adminis-
ter insulin, and then bring the dog to the clinic as soon as pos-
sible to begin the BGC.
Interpreting Blood Gluose Curves
BGCs are used to determine whether the administered insulin 
lowers BG eectively and to identify the glucose nadir, time of 
peak insulin eect, duration of insulin action, and degree of uc-
tuation in BGs. In well-controlled DM, BGs are between 80 and 
250-300 mg/dL (4.4 and 13.9-16.7 mmol/L). Insulin ecacy is 
evaluated, in part, by determining the dierence between highest 
and lowest BGs. A small dierence (e.g., 50 mg/dL) is acceptable 
if the highest BG is <220 mg/dL but not acceptable if it is >300 
mg/dL.51 e most important parameters are the BG nadir and 
the duration of the insulin eect. Ideally, the BG nadir should 
be 80-150 mg/dL. A lower nadir can be caused by insulin over-
dose, excessive overlap of the insulin action (common if long-
acting insulin analogues are used), prolonged periods without 
food (the dog refused to eat in-hospital), or strenuous exercise. 
A BG nadir >160 mg/dL can be due to an insucient insulin 
dose, insulin resistance, or owner technical problems. In a dog 
already treated with high dosages (e.g., >1.5 U/kg per injection), 
owner error and insulin resistance are the main dierential 
diagnoses.
Duration of Action
Duration of insulin action can be determined if the BG nadir is 
within the desired range. Duration is dened as the time from 
injection through the glucose nadir until the BG exceeds 250 mg/
dL. When duration is too brief (e.g., <8 hours), signs of DM are 
usually exhibited. When duration is too long (e.g., >14 hours), 
risk of hypoglycemia increases. Poor duration of action may be 
altered by changes in diet. One may need to choose an insulin 
product with a dierent action prole.
Reproducibility
Performing BGCs on consecutive days is not recommended and 
they should never be assumed to be reproducible. Day-to-day 
variables and DM itself are rarely static. Variables include the 
amount of insulin drawn into the syringe, the amount of insu-
lin absorbed aer each injection, and the interactions between 
insulin, diet, exercise, stress, excitement, presence of concurrent 
disorders, gastric emptying, and secretion of the counterregula-
tory hormones (e.g., glucagon, epinephrine, cortisol, GH). All 
these factors uctuate and prevent reproducible BGCs. Lack of 
consistency in BGC results can create frustration unless expected. 
It is better to view lack of consistency as common and caused 
by any variable that could alter glucose concentrations. When 
dose change appears appropriate, the change should not exceed 
10-25%. In documented or suspected hypoglycemia, the dose 
should be decreased by about 50%. Insulin doses should not be 
changed more frequently than every 5 to 7 days, except when 
hypoglycemia is documented.
