eBooks.Health.Elsevier.com
HOME MONITORING
Owners should be encouraged to assess their dog’s DM on a 
daily basis (see earlier section). Initially, body weight should be 
measured and recorded every week. Glucose measurement with 
PBGM using capillary blood is usually easy and acceptable for 
most owners and should be recommended as more accurate and 
less expensive. Capillary blood can be obtained using lancing 
devices on various sites: the inner aspect of the pinna is a good site 
for capillary sampling (Video 291.5; ch. 78); good alternatives are 
the buccal mucosa (Video 291.6)95 or the paw pad (Video 291.7). 
Owners must be thoroughly familiar with their PBGM and access 
to veterinary support should be available. In general, a good time 
for introducing the concept of home monitoring is aer the rst 3 
to 4 weeks of treatment, when the client is somewhat familiar with 
DM, will understand the time that DM requires, and appreciate 
the value of credible BGs. Once familiar with the procedure, the 
owner can perform a BGC by measuring the morning BG before 
giving insulin and food, and then every 2 hours until the next 
injection is due or even thereaer if a nadir is not obvious. Inter-
pretation of home BGCs should be the same as those performed 
in-hospital. Veterinarians should anticipate that some owners 
become overzealous, monitoring BGs too frequently. Some inter-
pret results and adjust the insulin dosages without veterinary con-
sultation, a practice that frequently leads to poorer control of DM, 
increased risk of overdose, confusion, and frustration.
CONTINUOUS GLUCOSE MONITORING SYSTEMS 
(CGMSs)
CGMSs are routinely used for monitoring people with DM and 
are beginning to be used in DM dogs.96 e CGMSs allow glucose 
to be monitored without need for repeated blood sampling. e 
CGMS measures interstitial glucose (IG) rather than BG. Inter-
stitial uid is easily accessible and results are virtually the same 
as BG.96-98 Many CGMS devices (e.g., Guardian REAL-time) 
have some limitations. ey need to be calibrated 2 to 3 times 
daily, which requires blood sampling. Sensors are quite expensive 
and can be used only for days. e monitor displays IGs from 
40 to 400 mg/dL; concentrations outside this range are correctly 
recorded but need to be downloaded to be seen.
A new human CGMS (Freestyle Libre [Abbott]; FGMS) con-
sists of a small, round, disposable, water-resistant sensor that 
continuously measures IG by means of a small (5 mm long × 0.4 
mm wide) lament inserted SQ. e FGMS generates informa-
tion every minute, and the readings are automatically stored in 
15-minute intervals for up to 14 days. IGs are displayed when the 
sensor is wirelessly scanned (or “ashed”) with a reader device or 
smartphone on demand. e reader device then displays the past 
8 hours of IG information, including current IG, a trend graph, 
and a trend arrow which indicates the direction of the patient’s 
current glucose concentration with respect to previous results. 
e data are transferred in cloud to create an easy-to-read report 
for the owner that can be shared with the veterinarian (LibreView; 
Figure 291.2). In dogs, the FGMS application is seemingly pain-
less, well-tolerated, and easy to use (Video 291.8, Figure 291.3). 
Mild erythema at the site of the application might be observed 
at the end of the wearing period. Good correlation between dog 
IG and PBGM results (rho = 0.94) have been found.99 e FGMS 
readings are less accurate for glucose concentrations <90 mg/dL 
(<5 mmol/L).99 Accuracy seems to be aected by the skin thick-
ness; dogs with thicker skin have more accurate measurements 
than dogs with thinner skin (<5 mm).100 In DM dogs, the FGMS 
detects IG nadirs and hypoglycemic episodes more frequently 
than PBGMs.102 A CGMS measuring IGs for up to 180 days 
(Eversense XL) has been developed for humans and will likely be 
studied and available for diabetic dogs.
PERSISTENCE OR RECURRENCE OF CLINICAL SIGNS
OVERVIEW
Persistence or recurrence of clinical signs are frequent issues 
in any diabetic. Recurrence of signs, especially PU/PD, can be 
noted at any time and common causes include technical issues 
in administering insulin, use of the incorrect (for this patient) 
insulin type, and less than ideal dosage or frequency of admin-
istration. Other common problems are insulin unresponsiveness 
caused by concomitant inammatory, infectious, neoplastic, or 
hormonal disorders (Table 291.3).
TECHNICAL ISSUES
Common reasons for poor glycemic control are errors in handling 
or injection techniques: specically, failure to mix the preparation 
correctly; use of an improper diluent; use of insulin that has been 
frozen, heated, or is out of date; incorrect injection technique 
using a syringe or insulin dosing pen, or inappropriate insulin 
dose or syringe size (U-40/mL vs. U-100/mL). ese problems 
can be identied by carefully evaluating the history and asking the 
owner to bring their insulin and syringes or pen device to every 
appointment. If concerns arise, the veterinarian or technician can 
observe the owner’s entire injection procedure. If needed, assis-
tance should be provided until the owner is able to master the 
technique.
INSULIN UNDERDOSE
Most dogs are well controlled with insulin doses ≤1 U/kg/injec-
tion SQ q12h. If the insulin dosage is considerably less than 1 U 
injection/kg and the dog is receiving insulin q12h, underdosing can 
explain poor glycemic control. In this scenario, the dose should be 
gradually increased by 10-25% per week.
EXPECTED GLYCEMIC VARIABILITY
In healthy dogs there is little BG variation within a day or from 
day to day (Figure 291.2, dog 1). In contrast, within day and day-
to-day glycemic variability is commonly observed in DM dogs, 
even in “well-controlled” dogs (Figure 291.2, dogs 2 and 3). BG 
variability has been documented even if the food, insulin dose, 
and environment in which the monitoring takes place (at home 
or in the clinic) are kept constant.101 Glycemic variability refers 
to wide glycemic excursions, including episodes of hypoglycemia 
and hyperglycemia during a single day or on dierent days.102
Since CGMSs have been increasingly used, glucose variability 
has been recognized commonly, but its cause cannot consistently 
be identied or resolved. In humans, high glycemic variabil-
ity is considered a risk factor for hypoglycemia, microvascular 
complications, neuropathy, retinopathy, stroke and all-cause 
mortality.103
No consensus gold-standard method has been identied for 
assessing the signicance of glycemic variability. Several indi-
cators have been proposed. Mean and standard deviation (SD), 
which describes the dispersion of values around mean BG, or per-
centage of the coecient of variation [%CV = (Standard Deviation 
of BGC values/Mean of BGC values) * 100] are used in diabetic 
people and may have value in DM dogs. High glycemic variability 
is a characteristic of poorly controlled diabetic dogs. Investiga-
tions for strategies to reduce the glycemic variability are the same 


---

## Page 2136

SECTION XIX
•
Endocrine Disease
1984
eBooks.Health.Elsevier.com
as those recommended for investigating the causes of diabetic 
instability (Table 291.3, Figure 291.4). When it is not possible to 
identify a clear cause for glycemic variability, a change in insulin 
should be considered, e.g., a dog that is treated with lente por-
cine insulin can be switched to insulin detemir, or another insu-
lin analogue (e.g., glargine insulin). In humans, the use of insulin 
analogs (e.g., insulin detemir) can reduce glycemic variability in 
comparison with other insulin preparations (e.g., NPH).104 If gly-
cemic variability cannot be reduced, insulin doses should be low 
enough to avoid worrisome hypoglycemia.
REBOUND HYPERGLYCEMIA (SOMOGYI RESPONSE)
e Somogyi response is dened as rebound hyperglycemia due to 
the hypersecretion of counterregulatory hormones (e.g., glucagon 
and epinephrine) during a hypoglycemic episode. In human diabet-
ics, inadequate insulin action (oen associated with low duration 
of insulin action) is considered to be responsible for the majority of 
these episodes of hyperglycemia following hypoglycemia as opposed 
to true rebound hyperglycemia associated with counterregulatory 
hormones. A recent study observed no signicant increase of cir-
culating plasma glucagon concentration during insulin-induced

<70 mg/dL, while yellow boxes highlight IG values >250 mg/dL. Apples represent meal time.
Dog 1: Healthy dog not receiving insulin. e glucose uctuations (variabilities) are almost absent.
Dog 2: English Setter, 9-year-old male, 27 kg, with diabetes mellitus (DM). e dog receives 18 U twice daily of porcine Lente insulin at mealtime. 
Glucose uctuations (glucose variability) are higher than the healthy dog (dog 1) but can be considered appropriate for a diabetic animal.
Dog 3: Mixed breed, 9-year-old male, 9.8 kg, with DM. e dog receives 6.5 U twice daily of NPH insulin at mealtime. It is evident there is high glucose 
variability within the 24 hours and also comparing 2 consecutive days.

hypoglycemia in 13 diabetic dogs. eir epinephrine concentra-
tions during insulin-induced hypoglycemia were lower than those 
observed in healthy dogs.105 ese observations contrast with the the-
ory of rebound hyperglycemia due to counterregulatory hormones. 
Additional studies may help understand the causes of dramatic gly-
cemic variability, but it is probable that the Somogyi phenomenon is 
not a special entity but part of the glycemic variability complex.
SHORT DURATION OF INSULIN ACTION
In some diabetic dogs, the eect of NPH and porcine lente insu-
lins may last <8 hours. As a result, hyperglycemia to levels above 
the renal threshold (∼175-225 mg/dL [9.7-12.5 mmol/L]) may 
continue for hours each day. Owners of dogs whose insulin is not 
lasting long enough usually report PU/PD. e issue may be dem-
onstrated on a BGC or with the use of a CGMS. Treatment involves 
switching to a longer-acting insulin q12h (i.e., PZI, glargine or 
detemir; see Table 291.2).
PROLONGED DURATION OF INSULIN EFFECT
Prolonged duration of action is usually dened as exogenous 
insulin lasting >12 hours. Insulin given q12h would overlap with 
the previous dose and increase risk of hypoglycemia. Prolonged 
duration of insulin action is usually observed when the glucose 
nadir occurs ≥10 hours aer injection. Treatment options include 
decreasing the frequency of administration from q12h to q24h 
when using detemir or glargine insulin. One may also consider 
changing insulin to one with a shorter duration of action.
ANTI-INSULIN ANTIBODIES
Canine, porcine and human insulin are similar. Development of 
signicant insulin antibodies has been reported but is uncom-
mon in DM dogs treated with porcine or recombinant human 
insulin.106
CONCURRENT DISORDERS CAUSING INSULIN 
RESISTANCE
Most DM dogs can be regulated with insulin doses ≤1 U/kg q12h. 
In dogs with higher insulin requirements, concurrent disorders 
should be suspected aer “technical” issues or short duration of 
insulin action have been ruled out. No insulin dosage clearly denes 
“insulin resistance.” It has been proposed that insulin resistance be 
suspected when glycemic control is poor despite insulin doses >1.5 
U/kg q12h, when high doses (>1.5 U/kg) are needed to maintain 
BGs <300 mg/dL (<16.7 mmol/L), or when glycemic control is 
erratic and insulin doses continuously adjusted.50 Any inamma-
tory, infectious, neoplastic or endocrine disorder can cause insulin 
resistance, as does obesity and a variety of drugs, especially gluco-
corticoids and progestagens. Insulin resistance is most commonly 
caused by glucocorticoid administration, diestrus, hypothyroidism, 
chronic pancreatitis, chronic kidney disease, infection, neoplasia, 
hyperlipidemia, severe obesity, and Cushing’s syndrome. e most 
common sites of infection are oral (dental) and urinary tract. Other 
less common causes of insulin resistance are pancreatic exocrine 
insuciency, cardiac disease, liver insuciency, glucagonoma and 
pheochromocytoma (see Table 291.3). Anamnesis and PE are the 
most important rst “tests” in identifying concurrent disorders. In 
most, a complete diagnostic work-up, including CBC, serum bio-
chemical prole, UA with bacterial culture, abdominal US and 
thoracic radiographs should be considered as initial “screens” for 
concurrent illness. When indicated, one may consider a low-dose 
dexamethasone suppression test, serum Pg concentration mea-
surement (in intact females), thyroid function tests, serum canine 
pancreatic lipase immunoreactivity (cPLI), serum trypsinlike 
immunoreactivity (TLI) and computed tomography or magnetic 
resonance imaging (pituitary mass).
LONG-TERM COMPLICATIONS OF DIABETES 
MELLITUS
HYPOGLYCEMIA
Hypoglycemia, rarely fatal, is an extremely common complication 
of insulin treatment.107 Hypoglycemia may follow a large increase 
in insulin dose, sudden increase in insulin sensitivity due to treat-
ment or improvement of concurrent disorders (e.g., treatment 
of hypothyroidism), inadvertent insulin overdose, overly strin-
gent attempts at glycemic control, excessive overlap of the insu-
lin action in dogs receiving insulin q12h, excessive exercise, and 
inappetence resulting in relative insulin overdose. Signs of hypo-
glycemia include lethargy, weakness, ataxia and increased appetite 
(chs. 61, 289). If hypoglycemia is allowed to progress, seizures, 
coma and death can occur. Symptomatic hypoglycemia should be 
treated with a glucose-rich food. If unable to eat, glucose can be 
applied to the dog’s oral mucous membranes in the form of glu-
cose gel or glucose solution, from which it is directly absorbed (ch. 
289). Aer recovery, the dog should be fed. Collapsed dogs should 
be given IV dextrose immediately as an initial IV bolus of 1 mL/
kg of 33% dextrose followed by a 5% dextrose infusion (ch. 289). 
Rates can be changed if necessary. Aer symptomatic hypoglyce-
mia, owners should be instructed to stop insulin until glycosuria 
recurs and then give 25-50% less. Subsequent insulin doses should 
be adjusted to avoid hypoglycemia and increase BG measure-
ments.50 Treatment of asymptomatic hypoglycemia also requires 
insulin dosage reductions of about 10-20%, or that insulin should 
be replaced with one that has a shorter duration of eect.
OCULAR COMPLICATIONS
Cataract formation is the most common long-term complication 
in diabetic dogs (Figure 291.1) (ch. 13). A retrospective-cohort 
study on 132 DM dogs referred to a university referral hospital 
found cataract formation in 14% at the time of diagnosis. In that 
study, the time interval for 25%, 50%, 75%, and 80% of the study 
population to develop cataracts was only 60, 170, 370, and 470 
days, respectively.108 Cataracts are thought to occur as a result of 
altered osmotic relationship in the lens induced by intra-cellular 
accumulation of sorbitol and galactitol following the metabolism 
of excess glucose and galactose by enzymes within the lens. e 
accumulation of sorbitol and galactitol in lens cells increases intra-
cellular osmolality, increases intracellular uid volume, ruptures 
lens bers, and causes cataract development.109 Once cataract 
formation begins, it is irreversible. ey can evolve quite rapidly. 
Complete blindness may be avoided by removing the abnormal 
lens. Vision is restored in 80-90% of DM dogs that undergo cata-
ract removal.110,111 Other ocular complications of diabetes can 
include uveitis, keratoconjunctivitis sicca, bacterial conjunctivitis 
and diabetic retinopathy.
DIABETIC NEUROPATHY
Diabetic neuropathy has been described in dogs, although its 
prevalence is unknown.112-115 Subclinical neuropathy seems to 
be most common. Clinical signs of diabetic neuropathy (weak-
ness, muscle atrophy, hyporeexia, hypotonia) are most com-
monly recognized in dogs that have been diabetic for 5 years 
or longer.50,115 Diabetic neuropathy in dogs is primarily a distal 
polyneuropathy, characterized by segmental demyelination and 
axonal degeneration.

## Page 2140

SECTION XIX
•
Endocrine Disease
1988
eBooks.Health.Elsevier.com
DIABETIC NEPHROPATHY
Diabetic nephropathy has occasionally been reported in dogs, 
but its clinical relevance remains unknown. Microscopic changes 
can include membranous glomerulonephropathy with fusion of 
the foot processes, glomerular and tubular basement membrane 
thickening, increase in the mesangial matrix material, presence of 
subendothelial deposits, glomerular brosis, and glomeruloscle-
rosis.116,117 In most DM dogs, chronic kidney disease is thought to 
develop as an independent condition.50
PROGNOSIS
e prognosis for dogs diagnosed with DM depends, in part, on 
the owner’s commitment to treating the disorder, ease of glycemic 
regulation, presence and reversibility of concurrent disorders, and 
avoidance of chronic complications associated with the diabetic 
state. In a large study involving insured dogs in Sweden, 347 dogs 
survived at least 30 days aer the rst DM claim. e propor-
tion of those surviving 1, 2, and 3 years was 40%, 36% and 33%, 
respectively.6 A recent study reported a median survival time of 
964 days in a population of dogs with DM treated in a referral 
center.118 A relatively high mortality rate exists during the rst 
6 months of treatment due to concurrent diseases such as keto-
acidosis, pancreatitis or infections. It is a common opinion that 
well-controlled diabetic dogs that survive the rst 6 months of 
treatment and have motivated owners have a life expectancy simi-
lar to that of non-diabetic dogs of the same age, sex and breed.

DEFINITION AND DIAGNOSIS
D
iabetes mellitus (DM) is a syndrome 
characterized 
by 
hyperglycemia 
due to defects in insulin secretion, 
insulin sensitivity, or both.1 2 Regardless of 
the pathogenic cause, relative or absolute 
deciency in insulin action on target tissues 
leads to myriad abnormalities in carbohy-
drate, fat, and protein metabolism. e crite-
ria for the diagnosis of DM in cats developed 
by an international consensus committee are 
provided in Box 292.1 3
Prediabetes is a state of abnormally 
increased blood glucose (BG) concentra-
tions (either fasting or aer a glucose load) 
that do not yet meet the criteria of DM. Pre-
diabetes is a risk factor for development of 
DM.4 Multiple studies have described cats 
with abnormal BGs in various clinical and 
experimental contexts5 6; however, no study 
has shown that a dened state of abnormal 
glucose in cats confers a risk for developing 
DM.1 erefore, criteria for diagnosis of pre-
diabetes in cats have not been established.
ETIOLOGY AND CLASSIFICATION
Multiple etiologies can lead to DM.1 Feline 
DM is mostly considered analogous to type 2 
DM in people, characterized by beta cell dys-
function of unknown etiology, usually with 
concurrent insulin resistance. is leads to 
either permanent or temporary states of 
exogenous insulin requirement.
Recent studies suggest that up to 25% of diabetic cats suer 
from DM secondary to hypersomatotropism (DM-HS; ch. 280).7
However, most studies on etiology, pathophysiology, and epide-
miology of feline DM were published before HS was commonly 
recognized. us, most cats with DM-HS were probably misdiag-
nosed as having uncomplicated type 2 DM.
Other types of diabetes resulting from processes that cause 
beta cell dysfunction or loss occur in a minority of aected cats. 
Pancreatitis can destroy beta cells (chs. 276, 278). Histopatho-
logic evidence of beta cell deciency associated with lymphocytic 
inltration (consistent with type 1 DM) has been described rarely. 
With the exception of the serum insulin-like growth factor 1 (IGF-
1) assay for identication of DM-HS, there is no ante mortem test 
to classify the other etiologic types of feline DM. From a clini-
cal perspective, virtually all cats with DM are handled similarly. 
Importantly, many cats that survive an episode of diabetic keto-
acidosis (DKA, ch. 280) can later achieve diabetic remission.8
EPIDEMIOLOGY
e prevalence of DM in cats ranges from 0.5-2% depending on the 
population studied.9-11 Numbers are steadily increasing due to the 
increasing frequency of predisposing factors, e.g., obesity and phys-
ical inactivity.12-14 is is similar to human type 2 DM. Importantly, 
DM in cats is generally diagnosed later in the disease process than 
in people, in whom diagnosis of DM is based on fasting hypergly-
cemia >126 mg/dL (>7 mmol/L).10 In contrast, the diagnosis in cats 
Diabetes Mellitus in Cats
Chen Gilor | Linda Fleeman
CHAPTER 292
This chapter is enhanced with the following electronic asset onElsevier eBooks+: 1 Video.
ALIVE* Criteria for Diagnosis of Diabetes Mellitus in 
Cats
The potential for stress hyperglycemia warrants caution in interpre-
tation of hyperglycemia of any magnitude in cats. Diabetes mellitus 
in cats is diagnosed:
1. In a patient with a random (fasted or unfasted) blood glucose 
concentration ≥270 mg/dL (≥15 mmol/L) with classic clinical 
signs of hyperglycemia (with no other plausible cause) or 
hyperglycemic crisis AND at least one of the following criteria:

•increased glycated proteins

•glucosuria on more than one occasion on a naturally voided
sample acquired in a home environment at least  days after 
any stressful events.
In some cases clinical signs may not have been reported by the 
owner.
2. In a patient with random (fasted or unfasted) blood glucose 
concentration >125 mg/dL to ≤270 mg/dL (>7 mmol/L to ≤15 
mmol/L) and at least two of the following:

•classic clinical signs of hyperglycemia (with no other plausi
ble cause) or hyperglycemic crisis

•increased glycated proteins

•glucosuria on more than one occasion on a naturally voided
sample acquired in a home environment at least  days after 
any stressful events.
Applying the above ALIVE criteria permits the possible existence 
of a subpopulation of cats where diabetes mellitus or stress hyper-
glycemia cannot be confidently confirmed or excluded; if concerns 
over presence of diabetes mellitus persist, periodic re-evaluation is 
warranted.

*Project ALIVE (Agreeing Language In Veterinary Endocrinology) was founded by 
the European Society of Veterinary Endocrinology in  and endorsed by the 
Society for Comparative Endocrinology in . It focuses on creating agreement 
over the definition of common terminology in veterinary endocrinology.

Diabetes Mellitus in Cats
1991
eBooks.Health.Elsevier.com
is oen delayed until BGs exceed the renal threshold (>270 mg/dL, 
>15 mmol/L)15 16 and clinical signs (polyuria/polydipsia/polyphagia 
[PU/PD/PP], weight loss) are evident.17 18 is delay in diagnosis 
may explain why most cats are insulin-dependent at diagnosis and 
why diet changes and oral hypoglycemic drugs are not typically suf-
cient to control DM early aer diagnosis.18
DM is most common in Domestic Long- and Shorthair cats. 
Most pure-breed cats are underrepresented.12 19 However, in the 
UK, Australia, and New Zealand, Burmese cats are over-represe
nted.11 19-21 DM appears to be an autosomal, partially penetrant, 
dominant trait in Burmese cats with about 4 times higher fre-
quency than other breeds.20 21 Genome-wide association studies 
have identied polymorphisms in genes related to beta cell func-
tion and lipid metabolism.22 23 Other susceptible breeds include 
Tonkinese and Norwegian Forest in the UK, and Maine Coon, 
Russian Blue, and Siamese in the USA.19 20 24
Increasing age is a risk factor; most cats are diagnosed at 
>8 years of age with peak incidence at 10-13 years.9 10 12 Neu-
tered male cats are at greater risk than neutered females, even 
when controlled for body weight.9 12 Other risk factors include 
indoor connement and little physical activity.25 Obesity (ch. 
150), increasingly prevalent in pet cats, causes insulin resistance, 
interferes with glycemic control, and confers a high risk of DM 
in cats.9 12-14 26-29 In the USA, it has been estimated that 25-35% 
of pet cats are overweight or obese.13 27 30 Environmental factors 
(e.g., living indoors, diet) predispose cats to obesity.27 30 e risk 
of obesity is highest among middle-aged, neutered male cats, the 
same group that is at increased risk of DM.27 30
FEATURES OF TYPE 2 DM IN CATS
Type 2 DM is a diagnosis of exclusion in cats—one to be consid-
ered only aer ruling out HS. e etiology of feline type 2 DM 
is unknown but likely similar to type 2 DM in people.1 Similari-
ties include risk factors, clinical presentation, epidemiology, and 
association with amyloid deposition in the islets of Langerhans.1
Periods when exogenous insulin is not required are common 
and genetic predisposition has been demonstrated in both cats 
and people.31 Type 2 DM is a progressive disease that can have 
a prolonged preclinical period. It is characterized by the com-
bination of impaired insulin secretion and insulin resistance. 
Impaired secretion results in insulin deciency, which is usually 
relative (rather than absolute) to the overall increased insulin 
requirement resulting from insulin resistance. Although obesity 
itself causes some degree of insulin resistance, it is not the cause 
of type 2 DM. Instead, obesity leads to a compensatory increase 
in insulin secretion. Obese diabetic individuals have impaired 
beta cell function whereas obese non-diabetics do not (ch. 150).
e melanocortin-4 receptor (MC4R), expressed in the hypo-
thalamus, mediates satiety when there is positive energy bal-
ance.31 It is also expressed in intestinal L cells and mediates the 
secretion of the incretin hormone glucagon-like peptide-1 (GLP-
1).32 In human obesity and DM, MC4R is a major susceptibility 
gene. Polymorphism in the MC4R gene was demonstrated in 
obese diabetic cats but not in obese non-diabetic cats.31 us, 
cats with this gene polymorphism may be predisposed to reduced 
incretin eect when obese, and so be more likely to develop DM.
FACTORS ASSOCIATED WITH DM REMISSION
REMISSION IS NOT A “CURE”
ere is no known cure for type 2 DM and remission of DM in 
cats is dened as a state of euglycemia with no insulin therapy 
for at least 4 weeks when insulin was previously administered.33
Achieving remission does not imply that the underlying disease 
has been cured but that endogenous insulin secretion is meeting 
or exceeding requirements. Cats in remission usually continue to 
display impaired glucose tolerance.34 Remission is a temporary 
state (although it might last for years) and, therefore, eorts to 
maintain it and treat any underlying disease should be consid-
ered. One possible exception is DM remission in cats treated for 
HS. In these cats, surgical removal of the growth-hormone secret-
ing tumor might be curative. Because it is by denition tempo-
rary, some question whether remission should be a treatment goal 
in all cats with DM. at also raises the question of which cats are 
more likely to achieve remission. Answers to these questions are 
not available, but remission is associated with longer survival in 
some studies.35 Here we discuss the available evidence and patho-
physiologic background.
PHYSIOLOGY OF REMISSION
Overview
For a cat with type 2 DM to achieve remission, beta cell capac-
ity to synthesize and secrete insulin must meet or exceed insu-
lin requirements. Insulin requirements decrease with decreased 
insulin resistance and decreased carbohydrate load. Insulin resis-
tance can be reduced with weight loss, resolution of inammatory 
conditions, pharmacological intervention (insulin sensitizers, 
discontinuing glucocorticoids and any drug that causes insulin 
resistance), and resolution of stress. Carbohydrate load can be 
decreased by reducing dietary carbohydrates or reducing carbo-
hydrate digestion (e.g., with acarbose), by suppressing hepatic 
glucose output with pharmacological interventions (e.g., metfor-
min, glucagon receptor antagonists), or by enhancing glucosuria, 
e.g., sodium-glucose cotransporter-2 (SGLT-2) inhibitors.
While the cause of underlying beta cell dysfunctions that 
lead to type 2 DM is unknown, some metabolic derangements 
that cause further beta cell dysfunction are somewhat reversible. 
Reversing metabolic derangements such as glucotoxicity, lipotox-
icity, and impaired incretin eect can lead to substantial improve-
ment in beta cell function that could make the dierence between 
whether or not remission is achieved.
Glucotoxicity and Lipotoxicity of Beta Cells
Longstanding hyperglycemia and hyperlipidemia are both toxic 
to beta cells, causing dysfunction and eventually beta cell death. 
Glucotoxicity causing beta cell dysfunction occurred within 48 
hours of IV glucose infusion in healthy cats.36 Aer 10 days of 
hyperglycemia, beta cell dysfunction and markedly reduced cell 
numbers persisted for some time aer discontinuation of glucose 
infusion. Hyperlipidemia does not have as dramatic an eect 
although it did seem to aect the cats’ ability to regulate BG.36 e 
eect of lipotoxicity on feline beta cells may have been underesti-
mated since mostly unsaturated fatty acids were infused. In other 
species, saturated fatty acids are more injurious than unsaturated 
fatty acids.37
Glucolipotoxicity and the Incretin Effect
Glucolipotoxicity can cause beta cell dysfunction by interrupting 
the incretin eect.38 Incretin hormones (glucose-dependent insu-
linotropic polypeptide [GIP]; GLP-1) are secreted from the intes-
tine in response to glucose and other nutrients in the gut lumen 
(but not in the blood).39 In a glucose-dependent manner, incre-
tins stimulate synthesis and secretion of insulin and inhibit gluca-
gon secretion. Incretin hormones also prevent beta cell apoptosis 
in response to injurious toxins (such as glucocorticoid drugs) and 
enhance beta cell dierentiation and proliferation.40,41 Incretin 
hormones are responsible for about 75% of postprandial insulin 
secretion in healthy people and abnormalities in incretin hor-
mone secretion or action lead to substantial beta cell dysfunction.
In people with type 2 DM, GIP secretion is normal or increased 
but beta cells become resistant to its eects. is resistance can be 


---

## Page 2144

SECTION XIX
•
Endocrine Disease
1992
eBooks.Health.Elsevier.com
partially reversed by treating DM. In contrast, GLP-1 secretion is 
suppressed in type 2 DM but it retains eect on beta cells, at least 
at pharmacological levels. GLP-1 receptor agonists (GLP-1RAs) are 
useful for people with type 2 DM and can delay the need for insulin 
therapy by years. Generally, the physiology of GLP-1 and the phar-
macology of its analogs are similar in cats and people.42 e role 
of GLP-1 analogs in inducing and maintaining DM remission or 
in treatment of prediabetes in cats has not been established. In one 
study, newly diagnosed DM cats were treated with insulin glargine 
U100 in addition to either the GLP-1RA exenatide-extended-release 
(ER) or placebo.43 Exenatide-ER caused no signicant adverse 
eects, improved metabolic outcomes, but did not increase remis-
sion rates. Studies in cats are hampered by the lack of a practical 
method of assessing pre-treatment residual beta cell function. It is 
likely a proportion of cats in this cohort was not capable of respond-
ing, diluting its perceived eect. Until practical beta cell function 
tests become available, it is not possible to identify good candidate 
cats with DM for GLP-1RA treatment. Similarly, there is potential 
for GLP-1RAs to maintain the prediabetic state in some cats, delay-
ing or preventing progression to an insulin dependent state.
PRACTICAL CONSIDERATIONS IN ACHIEVING REMISSION
Key Questions to Be Answered
e key questions that need answers pertaining to DM remission 
in cats are: 1) Can cats most likely to achieve remission be iden-
tied? 2) Which treatment strategies are most likely to result in 
remission? 3) Once achieved, how can remission be maintained? 
For all 3, there is little or no evidence on which to base recom-
mendations. Considering the pathophysiologic mechanisms, it is 
likely that tight glycemic control, reduced carbohydrate load, and/
or resolution of insulin resistance are needed. ese treatments 
should be implemented as early as possible in the course of the dis-
ease, before beta cell loss. e relative importance of each of these 
components is mostly unknown for the general population, let 
alone for the specic patient. Currently, all studies evaluate remis-
sion in cats that rst present with DM at the time clinical signs 
are evident. With earlier diagnosis, the medications and dietary 
manipulations may have had more success in inducing remission.
Cats Likely to Achieve Remission
e likelihood of achieving remission is an important question to 
consider aer diagnosing DM because it aects choice of treat-
ment and monitoring strategies. In a systematic review published 
in 2014,44 all available studies were ranked based on objective cri-
teria for scientic validity. Remission was more likely in cats with 
“milder” or earlier DM when BGs are relatively low, hyperlipid-
emia not present, and the reported disease duration is shorter. 
Remission is also more likely in older cats, potentially represent-
ing a dierent disease etiology or slower progression of the same 
disease, and in cats with a history of glucocorticoid therapy if dis-
continued when DM was diagnosed. Remission rates vary widely 
in studies (0-100%). Remission rates of 30-50% were most com-
mon. Most studies were conducted at academic institutions where 
cats received relatively intensive monitoring. Remission rates of 
only 13% were identied from medical records of 524 cats diag-
nosed and treated by general practitioners in the UK.45 Testing for 
HS was not consistent and this low remission rate may reect the 
high prevalence of unrecognized HS as a cause of DM. Another 
study of data from general practices reported much higher remis-
sion rates, although DM was diagnosed without measuring gly-
cated proteins and the criteria for remission were relatively lax.35
Insulin Treatment and Monitoring Strategies Most Likely 
to Achieve Remission
In the 2014 systematic review, remission was reported in cats that 
had been treated with various insulin formulations, including 
older insulins (e.g., protamine zinc insulin [PZI], lente) and newer 
formulations (e.g., glargine, detemir), oral hypoglycemic drugs 
(acarbose, glipizide), and a variety of diets.44 However, there were 
few side-by-side comparisons to better understand the ecacy 
of diets, medications, or treatment strategies, in the same clinical 
setting and using the same diagnostic criteria. us, insucient 
evidence was found to suggest that any particular insulin formu-
lation was more likely to lead to remission.44 In the only prospec-
tive randomized clinical trial, no dierence in remission rates was 
found between insulin glargine U100 and PZI in 46 cats fed a “low 
carbohydrate diet.”46
Diets Most Likely to Aid in Achieving Remission 
(ch. )
One prospective randomized clinical trial comparing 2 diets 
on the rate of DM remission in cats with similar body weights47
showed higher remission rates (68% versus 41%) in cats fed a 
canned low carbohydrate (3.5 g/100 kcal), low fiber (0.1 g/100 
kcal) diet (N = 31; Science Diet Feline Growth, Hill’s Pet Nutri-
tion) when compared with cats fed a canned moderate car-
bohydrate (7.6 g/100 kcal), higher fiber (3.1 g/100 kcal) diet 
(N = 32; Hill’s Prescription Diet Feline w/d, Hill’s Pet Nutri-
tion). These 2 diets had similar protein content from different 
sources, but fat content was 20% higher in the low-carbohy-
drate, low-fiber diet.
Monitoring Strategies Most Likely to Achieve Remission
One retrospective, non-randomized study compared monitoring 
strategies in the same clinical setting on remission rates.48 Cats 
with newly diagnosed DM fed a “low carbohydrate” diet (Purina 
DM and Hill’s MD, dry vs. canned not specied) and treated 
with insulin glargine U100 to maintain near-euglycemia (target 
BG 60-160 mg/dL, 3.3-8.9 mmol/L) as measured with a human 
point-of-care glucose meter designed to overestimate hypoglyce-
mia had a higher remission rate (78%) when compared to a group 
managed with a traditional paradigm in which changes in insulin 
dose were based mainly on clinical signs (14% of 36).48 e fre-
quency of biochemical and clinical hypoglycemia was similar in 
both treatment groups.
Maintaining Remission
Remission does not equal cure and most cats in remission con-
tinue to display impaired glucose tolerance.34,49 Disease pro-
gression, increased carbohydrate load, and/or increasing insulin 
resistance can over time lead to recurrence of a transient or per-
manent insulin-dependent state. Currently, no studies have evalu-
ated specic interventions for the maintenance of remission or 
the rate of relapse with no treatment. Based on the pathophysi-
ologic mechanisms, it is likely that the same interventions that 
might lead to induction of remission would also help maintain it. 
ese include minimizing insulin resistance, minimizing carbo-
hydrate load, and optimizing beta cell function.
INSULIN THERAPY
THE CHALLENGES
Insulin therapy is associated with many inherent challenges that 
must be overcome for treatment to be safe, eective, and sustain-
able. ese challenges relate to the complex nature of glycemic 
control, pharmacological limitations of available insulin for-
mulations, and the lifelong burden on caregivers. ese issues 
have been signicantly diminished in human medicine with the 
shi from traditional insulin suspensions that require the use of 
syringe and needles, to the use of recombinant insulin analogues, 
novel application methods such as injection pens, and continuous 
glucose monitoring (CGM) systems (Box 292.2).


---

## Page 2145

CHAPTER 292
•
Diabetes Mellitus in Cats
1993
eBooks.Health.Elsevier.com
INITIATING EXOGENOUS INSULIN THERAPY
Most cats require exogenous insulin therapy when diagnosed 
with DM. Alternatives should only be considered when insulin is 
not possible. In a cat with clinical signs, delaying insulin therapy, 
even while implementing hypoglycemic drugs, weight loss, and/
or dietary carbohydrate restriction, increases risk of glucolipo-
toxicity and decreases chance of remission. Low doses of insulin 
should be used in cats with mild hyperglycemia and no clinical 
signs. In such cats, euglycemia and remission might be achieved 
with alternative treatments, but there is risk of continuing beta 
cell injury from glucolipotoxicity if alternative treatments fail.
GENERAL PRINCIPLES; THERE IS NO PERFECT INSULIN
ere is little evidence that any one insulin formulation is supe-
rior for treating cats with DM. Insulin formulations vary in their 
average time-action prole, day-to-day variability, cost, and 
methods of administration. Currently, there are 2 insulin formu-
lations specically for cats but more than 10 human formulations 
commercially available, some of which are routinely used in cats. 
e choice of an insulin formulation depends on many factors 
which dier cat-to-cat and may dier in one cat at dierent times. 
Factors include disease pathophysiology (including concurrent 
diseases), insulin-related factors (including insulin pharmacol-
ogy, cost, regional prescribing regulations), pet and owner com-
pliance, diet (composition and feeding frequency), monitoring 
strategy, and goals of therapy for that cat, taking into account risk 
of hypoglycemia and likelihood of remission (Figure 292.1).
e insulin chosen should best t the specic clinical situation. 
Oen, opposing needs will result in compromise. For example, for 
a fractious cat or for an owner struggling to give injections, mini-
mizing the number of injections by using a long-acting insulin 
formulation once-daily might be a necessary compromise even if 
glycemic control is not optimal. In contrast, an insulin formula-
tion that is shorter in duration of action but less expensive might 
be ideal for an owner that can easily administer insulin but has 
cost constraints.
INSULIN PHYSIOLOGY: PEOPLE VS. CATS
In healthy people, a physiologic post-eating “bolus” of insulin 
secretion lasts 2-4 hours with 5-fold increases in insulin peak con-
centrations from baseline,50 depending on the type and quantity 
of food. In healthy cats, bolus insulin secretion has a longer dura-
tion (6 to >12 hr), occurs later, and has a much lower peak (1.5-3 
times baseline) at 1-8 hours, depending on the diet fed (Table 
292.1).26 51 52 ese data may be misleading since cats in these 
studies were fasted for relatively long periods before being fed a 
single meal. When once daily feeding was compared to 4 feedings 
per day in healthy cats, insulin increased as described above with 
the single meal, but when the daily caloric intake was fed as 4 
meals, the increase in plasma insulin was minimal and sustained 
for the 24 hour period.53 is sustained insulin requirement with 
no clear bolus phase is likely more representative of clinical insu-
lin requirements of cats.
COMMERCIALLY AVAILABLE INSULINS
SUSPENSIONS: LENTE, NEUTRAL PROTAMINE 
HAGEDORN (NPH), PROTAMINE ZINC INSULIN (PZI)
Insulin’s natural tendency to crystallize is enhanced in these suspen-
sions by adding either zinc (lente), protamine (NPH), or both (PZI) 
to the formulation, causing precipitation in the vial and in tissue 
aer being injected.54 Slowing insulin absorption by enhancing its 
crystallization leads to slower onset and longer duration of action. 
ese insulins must be evenly resuspended before each use by gen-
tly rolling and inverting the vial or, in the case of Vetsulin/Caninsu-
lin (Merck/MSD Animal Health), to vigorously shake the vial until 
the insulin is completely mixed. Resuspension can be inconsistent 
and lead to inaccurate dosing.55 Breakdown of the precipitate at the 
injection site is highly variable and unpredictable, altering insulin 
absorption patterns.56-58 ese formulations do not have time-
action proles that mirror a cat’s insulin requirement. Onsets of 
action may be too slow or durations of action too long to mimic a 
cat “bolus” phase or their time-action proles are too peaked and 
duration of action too brief to mimic “basal” secretion.54 58
Treatment Goals That Improve Quality of Life for Cat and 
Owner and Improve Chances of Long-Term Survival
•
Avoid hypoglycemia.
•
Prevent diabetic ketoacidosis (DKA).
•
Prevent excessive weight loss.
•
Reduce polyuria/polydipsia (PU/PD) to a degree acceptable to
the owners.
•
Euglycemia? Consider only if diabetic remission is a realistic
goal.

RECOMBINANT ANALOGUE INSULIN SOLUTIONS
Overview
ese long-acting insulin analogues were developed as “basal” 
formulations for use in people. Since they do not need resus-
pension their eects are more consistent because the unpredict-
able resuspension process is bypassed. e vial or pen does not 
require rolling or shaking prior to drawing a dose. In addition, 
absorption from the subcutaneous (SC) depot is less erratic. 
Because of superior predictability (low day-to-day variability), 
these formulations are associated with lower risk of hypoglyce-
mia. Pharmacodynamics, pharmacokinetics, and guidelines for 
frequency of administration of specic insulin formulations are 
presented in Table 292.2
Insulin Glargine
Insulin glargine is soluble at pH 4.0 (in which it is supplied) 
but in neutral pH (such as SC tissue) it has a strong tendency 
to precipitate, slowing its absorption.59 e precipitation-
deprecipitation process does introduce a component of vari-
ability in absorption but glargine is more predictable than NPH 
and, likely, other suspensions, but might be less predictable than 
other insulin analogs.57 60 Insulin glargine U300 (Toujeo, Sano) 
is biochemically identical to insulin glargine U100 (e.g., Lan-
tus, Sano), but 3 times more concentrated.61 e concentrated 
glargine U300 delivers any dose in a smaller droplet with smaller 
surface area, its absorption is slower, its duration is longer, its 
time-action prole is atter, and it is associated with less day-
to-day variability when compared to insulin glargine U100.60-63
Emerging evidence from several small studies and clinical 
experience suggests that insulin glargine U300 provides better 
control of DM than insulin glargine U100, although its dura-
tion of action is oen insucient for once daily administration 
(Table 292.2).64 65
Insulin Detemir
In contrast to insulin glargine U100 and insulin suspensions, the 
actions of insulin detemir are highly predictable in people, with min-
imal inter- and intra-subject variability, key to minimizing hypogly-
cemic events (Table 292.2).66-70 Detemir inhibits hepatic glucose 
output more eectively than other basal insulin products, decreases 
adipose tissue lipogenesis, and weight gain is minimized.68-71 Prefer-
ential insulinization of the liver and low day-to-day variability have 
yet to be demonstrated in cats. e duration of action of insulin 
detemir is usually too brief for once-daily basal use.72
Because of its low potency in people, insulin detemir is formu-
lated at a concentration 4 times higher (2400 nmol/mL, 100 U/
mL; Levemir, Novo Nordisk) than other insulin formulations, e.g., 
Lantus, 600 nmol/mL, 100 U/mL. Low potency is likely explained 
by most insulin detemir molecules being bound to albumin in 
plasma, allowing only a fraction to be biologically active.73 is 
is likely true in cats considering that the dose range required for 
achieving good glycemic control is similar (median of about 2 U/
cat q12h) for insulin detemir and insulin glargine U100.74 75 Insu-
lin detemir should be started using the same dose used for other 
formulations in cats. Insulin detemir (or any insulin) should not 
be diluted.
Insulin Degludec
Insulin degludec, originally formulated for every-other-day use 
in people with DM, is now usually given once-daily without 
need for rigid injection schedules, improving patient compliance 
(Table 292.2). In people, it reaches steady state within days and 
has minimal inter-day uctuation even when the time of injection 
is inconsistent,76 making it an ideal basal insulin in people. In cats 
however, the duration of action of insulin degludec is limited to 
about 12 hours and it has a pronounced peak.65
“INTERMEDIATE”-ACTING INSULIN FORMULATIONS
Overview
Commercially available insulin suspensions (NPH, NPH/regular 
mixes, lente, and PZI), insulin glargine U100, and detemir are cat-
egorized as “intermediate”-acting formulations that require q12h 
administration for best results in cats (Table 292.2). ese insu-
lins are oen associated with dierent required vs delivered doses, 
causing uctuation in intra-day BGs. is may be less obvious in 
cats with residual beta cell function, because their endogenous 
insulin can “close the gap” between required and delivered insu-
lin. In contrast, cats with no residual beta cell function are more 
prone to substantial glycemic variability.
Goals With Intermediate-Acting Insulins
e usual goal when using q12h “intermediate”-acting insulin 
protocols is alleviation of DM-associated clinical signs. Since BG 
uctuates each day and there is substantial inter-day variability, the 
goals should be BGs that range from normal (80-100 mg/dL; 4.5-
5.5 mmol/L) at their lowest and 250-350 mg/dL (14-19 mmol/L) 
at their highest each day. Although uncommonly realized,77,78 the 
“ideal” curve would begin with BGs at the high end just prior to 
giving insulin and lowest (nadir) about 6 hours later.79,80 Glycated 
protein concentrations remain above reference intervals using this 
approach and absence of PU/PD may indicate good glycemic con-
trol, over-dose if episodes of hypoglycemia, or remission of DM.
Durations of Action, Induction of Remission
In cats, porcine lente and NPH insulins tend to have shorter dura-
tions of action compared with PZI, glargine U100, detemir, and 
degludec.72,81,82 Once-daily high doses of PZI, glargine U100, or 
detemir can achieve acceptable levels of clinical control, especially 
in cats with residual beta cell function, but with increased risk of 
hypoglycemia. Insulin glargine U100 has been touted as advanta-
geous in inducing remission in cats with DM but the studies were 
uncontrolled, non-randomized, and there is little compelling 
evidence to support this claim.44 In a small clinical study, q24h 
insulin glargine U100 was compared with q12h porcine lente in 
cats.83 Both treatment groups experienced improved glycemia, 
4 of 13 cats achieved remission of DM, but only 1 of these was 
in the glargine U100 group. Other studies comparing glargine 
U100 to either PZI or detemir found no dierences in remission 
rates.46,74,75
LONG-ACTING “BASAL” INSULIN FORMULATIONS
Commercially available basal insulins have time-action proles 
evenly distributed from soon aer injection until metabolized, 
usually eective for 8-24 hours without a distinct peak. Basal for-
mulations are appropriate for most cats with DM, since most do 
not have a postprandial bolus insulin requirement (Table 292.2). 
ey have slow food transit times, should be fed multiple times 
a day, and/or fed a low carbohydrate diet.84 Use of basal insulin 
may negate need for timing insulin administration to a meal. For 
a basal insulin to be eective and safe as a once-daily injection, 
it should provide relatively constant action throughout each 24-
hour period. From experience and studies, insulin glargine U300 
(Toujeo) comes closest to meeting these standards in cats.64,65
COMPLIANCE, ADHERENCE, CONVENIENCE, COST
Owner Quality of Life
Owners’ response to surveys about administering insulin injec-
tions to their cats with DM85 revealed that one great concern 
was the eect of this responsibility on the owners’ quality of life, 
rather than that of the cat (Box 292.2). Worries about who would 
care for their cat if the owner was unavailable were common. It

is surmised that veterinarians must be exible and oer insulin 
treatment options that are client focused, particularly with respect 
to injection timing. It is unknown how the act of injection aects 
cats’ compliance and the owners’ short- and long-term decisions 
to treat with insulin. It is reasonable to assume that the high 
euthanasia rate (40%) upon diagnosis of DM is associated, at least 
in part, with compliance.86
Factors in Adhering to Insulin Protocol
Studies on diabetic people show that adherence declines with 
increasing dosing frequency,87 if injections are painful, due to for-
getfulness or other priorities or regimen complexity (more than 1 
medication, need to draw up insulin doses), and cost.88,89 For cats 
and cat owners, strategies that reduce these barriers would likely 
result in greater treatment success, improved quality of life for pet 


---

## Page 2148

SECTION XIX
•
Endocrine Disease
1996
eBooks.Health.Elsevier.com
and owner, and an increase in percent of DM cats treated. In this 
context it is important to consider not just the treatment choice per 
se, but also how it aects monitoring intensity and cost. For exam-
ple, giving a human recombinant basal insulin q24h using a conve-
nient stress-free insulin pen may achieve reasonably good glycemic 
control with minimal day-to-day variability, low risk of hypoglyce-
mia (another owner concern), and less need for intense monitoring. 
Cost and availability vary by geographic location, e.g., veterinary 
formulations are relatively more expensive than human formula-
tions in some countries (Australia, Brazil) but the opposite is true 
in the USA. In the UK and parts of Europe, regulation requires that 
veterinary products be used rst in the prescribing cascade.
SYRINGE VS PEN FOR INSULIN ADMINISTRATION
Attributes of Pens
Multiple studies have identied advantages to using insulin 
dosing-pens rather than multi-dose insulin vials and syringe-
needles. Advantages include improved patient adherence,90 91
less frequent hypoglycemic events,92 93 and overall healthcare 
cost-savings.93 94 Most people preferred the pen over vial-syringe 
and consider it easier to use. Insulin pens are especially easier 
to use by those with visual impairments or dexterity problems. 
ey result in better patient satisfaction and improved quality of 
life.90 91 Also, injection pens are consistently shown to be more 
accurate and precise compared to insulin syringes,95-97 especially 
with small doses (<5 U). e relative imprecision of vial-syringe 
method can be critical in causing day-to-day variability, which 
subsequently makes monitoring more complicated and more 
expensive.
When using a traditional insulin syringe, for a target dose 
of 1 U, 50% of drawn doses were >20% dierent than the target 
dose and most syringe-measured doses exceeded the intended 
dose.98 99 Currently, one insulin dosing pen is produced for Vet-
sulin/Caninsulin (VetPen, MSD Animal Health) (Videos 291.2, 
291.3). Initial pilot studies have shown the device to be easy to use 
in dogs, well tolerated under real-life conditions, more accurate, 
wastes less insulin, and is preferred by a majority of owners.100-102
Other available insulin dosing pens were designed for use in 
humans. Used as directed by the manufacturer, such pens may 
be too expensive for common use in some countries (e.g., USA).
Delivery of . Unit Increments
Most pens are limited to deliver 1 U increments with no 0.5 U 
option. While a disadvantage when compared with vial-syringes, 
the latter is also associated with low accuracy at 0.5 U dose 
increments.98
Practical Modifications to Insulin Injection Pen Protocols
Clinical experience suggests that with some modications to the 
standard protocol of injection pen use, substantial cost reduc-
tions can be achieved without signicant compromise. Modica-
tions should be used with caution however, considering the lack 
of supportive clinical research. Insulin dosing pens are tested for 
short-term stability (<42 days) without refrigeration. Keeping 
pens refrigerated between dosing likely reduces the likelihood of 
bacterial contamination, allowing use until the insulin vial has 
been completely emptied. Some pens have been used for months, 
substantially reducing cost. In contrast to reports from people, 
injection of cold insulin does not seem painful to cats.
“Priming” the insulin dosing pen (“air shot”) with 1-3 U is 
recommended prior to every injection, potentially making use of 
pens less economical for cats (Figure 292.2). A new pen should be 
primed to verify that it works, but one USA-based author (CG) 
does not recommend further priming. Without regular priming, 
air bubbles in the pen might interfere with accurate dosing or a 
pen becoming faulty might not be noticed. ese possibilities 
must be considered and the pen checked if a cat does not respond 
to insulin as expected. It is also advisable to regularly check the 
insulin reservoir visually and then eject any air bubbles. e prob-
lem of air bubbles can also be avoided by tilting the pen during 
injection so that the plunger is higher than the needle tip, allow-
ing the air bubbles to move away from the needle. In people, it is 
recommended to keep the needle of the pen under the skin aer 
injection for at least 3 seconds to allow the dose to be completely 
delivered. is does not seem to be necessary with the small doses 
used in cats with DM. A more comprehensive review of the use of 
insulin injection pens can be found elsewhere.103
TIMING INSULIN ADMINISTRATION WITH FEEDING OR 
WITH PRE-INSULIN BGs
It is not necessary to match food ingestion with insulin injections 
in cats, regardless of insulin used. In cats treated with basal insu-
lin, there is usually no need to adjust insulin dose for a specic 
meal based on amount of food consumed. It is not necessary or 
eective to change an insulin dose based on pre-injection BGs, 
as a substantial eect of any administered dose is achieved many 
hours aer injection.
NON-INSULIN HYPOGLYCEMIC AGENTS
OVERVIEW AND SCENARIOS FAVORING THEIR USE
Non-insulin hypoglycemic agents include a variety of drug 
families with an array of pharmacological targets.103 Most have 
not been rigorously studied in cats (Table 292.3). Commer-
cially available non-insulin drugs may be useful as sole ther-
apy when sufficient beta cells are present, usually early in the 
disease process before BGs exceed renal threshold, preventing 
or postponing the need for insulin therapy. Such drugs may 
be used together with insulin, to improve glycemic control 
and potentially reduce dose or frequency of insulin injections. 
Figure 292.2
“Priming” the needle (“air shot”) with 1-3 U is recom-
mended prior to every injection. is potentially makes use of pens less 
economical for cats, since their doses are usually small. (From Feldman 
E, Fracassi F, Peterson M, eds. Feline Endocrinology. Edra; 2019.)

eBooks.Health.Elsevier.com
Non-insulin therapies are not usually sufficient for managing 
later stages of DM.
SPECIFIC AGENTS (TABLE 292.3)
GLP- Receptor Agonists (GLP-RAs)
GLP-1RAs, the most promising family of non-insulin hypoglyce-
mic drugs, stimulate insulin while inhibiting glucagon secretion 
only during hyperglycemia, making them exceedingly safe. By 
slowing gastric emptying, GLP-1 slows digestion and absorption 
of carbohydrates, curbing postprandial hyperglycemia. GLP-1RAs 
also decrease appetite and promote weight loss. Limited data sug-
gest the eects of GLP-1RA in cats include weight loss, insulin 
stimulation during hyperglycemia, and slowing gastric emptying 
rates.104-107 e GLP-1RA exenatide was shown to curb weight gain, 
lower insulin requirement, and decrease glycemic variability in dia-
betic cats.43 108 109 e duration of action of exenatide-ER is longer 
than 30 days in cats, with few side-eects.43
Glipizide
Glipizide, a second-generation sulfonylurea, requires some func-
tioning beta cells to have eect. When 50 newly diagnosed cats 
with DM were given 5 mg/cat q12h, 56% deteriorated within the 
rst 22 weeks and le the study.110 e remaining 44% improved, 
with 6 of the 50 cats (12%) achieving remission. Side-eects were 
mild and transient (hypoglycemia in 6 cats, anorexia and vomit-
ing in 8 cats, and transient icterus with abnormal liver enzyme 
activities in 4 cats). ere was no mortality.
Acarbose
Acarbose, an alpha-glucosidase inhibitor, decreases glucose 
absorption from the gastrointestinal tract. Twenty-four cats with 
DM were given acarbose in addition to insulin and a canned low 
carbohydrate diet.111 Remission was associated with weight loss 
but not with acarbose. No side-eects were described, but diar-
rhea and atulence occur in other species.
SGLT- Antagonists
SGLT-2 antagonists cause increased renal excretion of glucose. Short-
term clinical trials in diabetic cats have reported that bexagliozin 
and velogliozin are well tolerated in people and eectively decrease 
BG concentrations.112 113Long-term studies are required to assess 
the safety, survival and quality of life of cats treated with these drugs 
compared to insulin. ese agents do not require dose titration nor 
increase the risk of hypoglycemia when used as monotherapy, so less 
intensive glycemic monitoring is required compared with insulin 
therapy. However, ketosis occurs in a proportion of cats treated with 
SGLT-2 antagonists, especially in the rst 7 days. Ketosis can prog-
ress to either euglycemic or hyperglycemic ketoacidosis. erefore, 
treatment with SGLT-2 antagonists is only recommended for cats 
that are well. Ketones in blood or urine should be closely monitored 
for at least the rst week of treatment and whenever the cat becomes 
unwell. Prompt insulin treatment is required if there is ketosis.
MONITORING
MONITORING BASED ON GOALS OF THERAPY
Tight Control vs Risk of Hypoglycemia
It is not known if remission of DM in cats is proportional to 
their level of hyperglycemia, related to a certain threshold of 
hyperglycemia, to glycemic variability, or other factors. Regard-
less, there is scarce evidence supporting tight glycemic control 
to improve likelihood of DM remission,48 75 but it may be asso-
ciated with increased risk of clinical hypoglycemia.114 ere is 
abundant evidence from other species that over time, hypogly-
cemia (even when subclinical and/or intermittent and sporadic) 
and high glycemic variability lead to impaired counterregulatory 
responses and increased risk of fatal insulin-induced hypoglyce-
mia. Multiple well-established mechanisms explain this issue in 
people, rodents, and dogs.115-118 Because it is potentially fatal, 
avoiding “hypoglycemia” is critically important, but there are 
no guidelines for cats dening unacceptable levels of hypogly-
cemia (both in terms of absolute concentrations or duration), 
or how they translate to low interstitial glucose concentrations 
(IGcs). Heavier cats appear to be at greater risk of hypoglycemia, 
suggesting an association of insulin overdose with obesity.119
Increased glycemic variability appears to be more common in 
cats with concurrent diseases and those that fail to achieve DM 
remission, although causality has not been established.108 120
Potential for Long-Term Attrition in Owner Adherence 
and/or Pet Compliance
What may seem to be an ideal treatment and monitoring plan in 
the short term can result in problems over time if owners reach 
the limit of their resources (nancial and others). is is especially 
true if concurrent diseases develop (ch. 224). With some cats and 
owners, minimalist treatment and monitoring approaches might 
be preferable. It may help to identify goals that improve chances 
of survival and quality of life for both cat and owner (Box 292.2).

MONITORING BASED ON CLINICAL SIGNS
Using Only Clinical Signs
With any DM monitoring strategy, assessment of clinical signs 
is crucial. In many cats, it is possible to achieve reasonably good 
control when monitoring only clinical signs. While subclinical 
hypoglycemia cannot be ruled out, careful and strategic dose 
alterations based on clinical signs can minimize that risk without 
measuring BGs.
Body Weight
Aer ruling out other causes, weight loss in a cat with DM is a 
sign of overall insulin deciency while excessive weight gain might 
be a sign of overdosing. However, even in an animal that is los-
ing weight, hypoglycemia is a real possibility if insulin duration 
is too short and the dose is increased to compensate for its short 
duration.
Water Intake and Urine Output
Severity of PU/PD can reect glycemic regulation. Quantica-
tion of a diabetic cat’s water intake is ideal but not always feasible. 
Water bowls that measure and report individual water intake (e.g., 
Felaqua) are available. If exact quantication is not feasible, when 
questioning owners it is important to inquire about changes since 
the last time therapy was altered and not focus only on absolute 
values. Some owners report no change as “normal” because they 
have become accustomed to some degree of PU/PD.
Initial Low-Dose Insulin and PU/PD
Changes in BGs might not be reected in water intake or urine 
output, especially when BG concentrations uctuate below the 
renal threshold. us, over-regulation cannot always be distin-
guished from good glycemic control. One way to dierentiate 
the 2 is to begin insulin at a dosage likely to be insucient and 
then increase the dosage gradually every 2-4 weeks while carefully 
monitoring clinical signs. When a dosage is reached without fur-
ther improvement in PU/PD, it should be assumed that no better 
control can be achieved without signicantly risking hypoglyce-
mia. At that point, it is recommended to decrease the dosage of 
insulin (by about 20-25%) and to continue reducing it gradually 
(every 2-4 weeks) until PU/PD worsens. If no clinical dierence is 
observed between 2 dosages, it is always safer to choose the lower.
Changing Insulins
If no improvement is observed when an insulin dose is 
increased and the level of control is unacceptable to an owner 
(weight loss, other signs), it is important to determine if the 
owner observations are reliable and be certain that insulin 
injection technique is appropriate, the insulin formulation is 
correct and not mishandled, and concurrent diseases have been 
excluded. en, the formulation of insulin can be changed and 
the process should start over.
Suspected Hypoglycemia
Some have incorrectly suggested serial BG monitoring to be 
“mandatory” when hypoglycemia is suspected.16 However, 
BG curves are not sensitive for detecting hypoglycemia and 
there is usually a time gap between observing suspicious clini-
cal signs and performing a curve, especially when performed 
in the hospital. During this gap in time, the cat remains at risk 
of hypoglycemia. An alternative safe approach is to reduce the 
insulin dosage by at least 20-25% without measuring any BG. 
is approach eectively ensures use of a dosage previously con-
sidered safe and potentially even too low. If the clinical signs 
of suspected hypoglycemia resolve, even if PU/PD worsens, the 
dosage of insulin should not be increased again. e cat may 
benet from a dierent insulin formulation at this point or 
remain on the lower dosage of insulin. e choice depends on 
how intolerable the clinical signs are to the owners. If the clinical 
signs of suspected hypoglycemia (chs. 61, 289) do not resolve, 
either the dosage needs to be decreased further, or the clinical 
signs were not caused by hypoglycemia. Depending on the clini-
cal context, more diagnostic tests, including use of a continuous 
glucose monitor (CGM) and/or BG curve, may be indicated.
Remission Desired
Monitoring DM cats using clinical signs when clinical remission 
is desired is oen best accomplished by adding fructosamine and/
or daily urine glucose testing. Insulin dosage can be increased 
gradually as described but with goals of normalizing clinical 
signs, having the fructosamine within the reference interval, and/
or persistently negative glucosuria. en, the insulin dosage can 
be decreased gradually about every 1-4 weeks if clinical signs do 
not recur, fructosamine concentrations remain normal, and/or 
urine remains negative for glucose.
MONITORING BASED ON GLYCATED PROTEINS
Attributes and Disadvantages of Glycated Protein 
Measurements
Glycated proteins, usually fructosamine or hemoglobin A1c, are 
produced over time and are not aected by within-day, day-to-day, 
or acute BG uctuations on the day of testing (stress, feeding). Use 
of glycated proteins is convenient for assessing long-term treat-
ment response trends. Measuring protein glycation is especially 
useful when clinical signs are not apparent or when owners are 
unable to accurately report clinical signs. e main advantage of 
measuring protein glycation is also its main disadvantage: short-
term hypoglycemic episodes are not captured and high concentra-
tions of glycated proteins do not rule out episodes of hypoglycemia.
Inaccurate Glycated Protein Results
Use of glycated proteins assumes no abnormality in protein con-
centrations or half-lives. Fructosamine values increase when the 
half-lives of serum proteins increase (e.g., hypothyroidism) and 
decrease when the half-lives decrease (e.g., hyperthyroidism, 
protein-losing enteropathies). Results from lipemic and/or hemo-
lyzed samples are not reliable.
Fructosamine
Serum fructosamine concentrations are determined by BG con-
centrations over the previous 2 to 3 weeks. Fructosamine ref-
erence values for various degrees of glycemic control are not 
well-established for cats with DM, do not consistently reect its 
range of biological variability, and results do not always match 
clinical observations. Comparing results from 1 cat over time are 
more useful. One should obtain a baseline result to compare with 
any result aer a treatment change, using the trends rather than 
the absolute numbers.
Hemoglobin Ac
Glycosylated hemoglobin has been validated in cats and is com-
mercially available (A1Care, Baycom Diagnostics). It is marketed 
worldwide, requiring a few drops of dried blood on a test card to 
be mailed to the laboratory. e inter-day assay coecient of varia-
tion is ≤5% (within 3 weeks of testing) and the laboratory complies 
with the National Glycohemoglobin Standardization Program 
(NGSP.org). Hemoglobin A1c reects average BG concentrations 
over the previous 70 days in cats and therefore will not be useful 
for monitoring if frequent changes in therapy are made. It is useful 
when serum fructosamine cannot be interpreted with condence. 
ere is anecdotal evidence that it could be useful for diagnosis and 


---

## Page 2151

CHAPTER 292
•
Diabetes Mellitus in Cats
1999
eBooks.Health.Elsevier.com
monitoring of prediabetes in cats. Chronic anemia falsely increases 
A1c values but, unlike serum fructosamine, A1c is not aected by 
hemolysis or abnormalities in serum protein turnover rate. Also, in 
contrast to fructosamine, a recent study established the reference 
interval (1.9-3.1%) for A1Care in 48 healthy cats.120a
DIRECT MONITORING OF BLOOD GLUCOSE (BG) OR 
URINE GLUCOSE
Blood Glucose Curves
BG curves uctuate considerably hour-to-hour and day-to-day 
in DM cats treated with insulin. ese uctuations are caused by 
many factors, only one of which is the insulin dose. erefore, treat-
ment decisions based on BG curves are not oen successful. In one 
study, hypothetical insulin dosage recommendations frequently 
diered when based on BG curve results that were performed 
within days when conditions were otherwise equivalent (including 
insulin dosage, feeding, apparent stress levels, etc.).121 e most 
valuable and reliable nding on any BG curve is hypoglycemia, 
which should always warrant a change: either a decrease in insulin 
dosage or a change in formulation. However, because of day-to-
day variability, and especially when a BG curve is performed in the 
hospital or under other stressful conditions, hypoglycemia cannot 
be ruled out based on a single curve even if all BGs were increased. 
Hypoglycemia aside, other BG curve-derived parameters, includ-
ing perceived duration of insulin action, time of nadir, “insulin 
resistance,” etc., cannot be estimated with condence based on a 
single BG curve completed over the expected duration of a single 
insulin dose, especially with insulin formulations known to have 
marked day-to-day variation. ese parameters can be better esti-
mated based on CGM over a few days (ch. 78).
Glucosuria
Glucosuria in a cat with DM indicates that the BG concentrations 
have exceeded renal threshold during the time the urine was formed. 
us, measuring glucose in a single urine sample has limited value. 
However, because measuring urine glucose can be done by owners 
on a regular basis, they may warn when increased risk of hypoglyce-
mia is suspected. If no glucose is detected in the urine over e.g. 3-5 
consecutive days, the insulin dosage should be decreased.
CONTINUOUS GLUCOSE MONITORING (CGM; ALSO SEE 
CH. 78)
Interstitial Glucose Concentrations (IGcs) Are Not Blood 
Glucose Concentrations
Available CGM systems (CGMs) are similar in accuracy and dier 
mostly in cost and convenience. Since CGMs measure IGc, not BG, 
results oen dier both in absolute values and timing. ere is a delay 
of a few minutes (at least) between changes in BG concentration and 
changes in IGc. Depending on the rate and direction of BG change, 
changes in BG are not always fully reected in IGc. Comparing CGM 
absolute values to BG concentrations might not be relevant.
Scenarios for Using a CGM
CGMs might be used exclusively as a less invasive method for cats 
that otherwise require traditional BG curves, an approach that 
underutilizes this technology. e authors use the FreeStyle Libre 
(Abbott) when starting a cat on a new insulin formulation or inves-
tigating a challenging case. e use of a CGM can shorten time for 
determining an insulin dose, conserving owner resources. When 
the insulin formulation has low day-to-day variability (insulin 
detemir or glargine U300), we are aggressive in changing doses, 
not waiting 2-3 weeks to assess clinical response. Instead, insulin 
dosage can be increased by as much as 20-25% every day, if the 
IGc readings are consistently >350 mg/dL (19 mmol/L).
Once IGcs are <350 mg/dL (19 mmol/L), dosing changes are 
made aer waiting to determine the new insulin’s full eect and 
a consistent pattern is apparent. If owners can follow a strict daily 
routine, the interval between dosing changes might be only 2-3 
days. If owners are less consistent, or if a less consistent insulin for-
mulation is used, dosing changes are made less frequently, allowing 
the CGM to display the dierent day-to-day glycemic uctuations.
Long-Term Home Use of CGMs
When clinical signs have largely resolved, 1 of 2 strategies can be 
considered. Many owners are interested in using CGMs perma-
nently, they are shown how to apply the sensor, and communica-
tions are continued by sharing data online with visits to the clinic 
only as needed. In this scenario, ne tuning insulin doses can be 
ongoing, responding to transient changes in glycemia. Many own-
ers do not want to use a permanent CGM and less intense moni-
toring of clinical signs as described previously may be employed. 
CGM sensors may also be applied periodically, replacing periodic 
fructosamines or BG curves.
CGMs and a Goal of Euglycemia
In people with DM, CGMs aid in maintaining long-term near eug-
lycemia. is should slow progression of long-term complications. 
It is not known if using a CGM improves remission rates. While 
increasing the safety of tight glycemic control protocols in the 
short term, the long-term use of CGM should be assessed in terms 
of risking attrition in owner compliance and overall survival.
COMPLICATIONS OF FELINE DM
ere is no evidence that specic glucose targets should be main-
tained in cats with DM to avoid short- or long-term complications.
DIABETIC NEUROPATHY
Neuropathy in diabetic cats is exhibited as a plantigrade and less 
commonly a palmigrade stance and gait that interferes with walk-
ing and jumping (Figure 292.3). Irritability when touching the 
paws has been reported.122 123 Conduction decits can be identi-
ed in motor, sensory, and mixed nerves, with decreased postural 
reactions and tendon reexes. Structural nerve injury involves 
endoneurial microvascular abnormalities aecting Schwann 
cells and neurons, similar to the neuropathy in some people with 
DM.122 e condition is thought to result from a combination of 
chronic hyperglycemia, insulin resistance, and hyperlipidemia.124
Figure 292.3
Plantigrade stance in a cat, consistent with diabetic 
neuropathy. (From Feldman E, Fracassi F, Peterson M, eds. Feline Endo-
crinology. Edra; 2019.)
