Signs of neuropathy are oen reversible with 2-3 months of DM 
treatment (Video 292.1), but specic glycemic or other targets to 
optimize chances of resolution are not known.
URINARY DISEASES
Urinary Tract Infection (UTI) (ch. )
DM is considered a predisposing factor for UTIs secondary to 
systemic immune suppression125 and glucosuria providing a sub-
strate for microorganisms. UTIs are more common in diabetic 
people treated with SGLT-2 inhibitors that increase glucosuria.126
UTIs were reported in 12-13% of DM cats. Lower urinary tract 
signs are a good predictor for a positive urine culture.127 128 How-
ever, a more recent study did not identify DM as risk factor for 
subclinical bacteriuria, with 0 UTIs of 22 cats examined. DM was 
signicantly associated with candiduria in cats.129
Chronic Kidney Disease (CKD, ch. ), Protein-Losing 
Nephropathy (PLN, ch. )
Type 2 DM is a leading cause of nephropathy in people. e prev-
alence of microalbuminuria and proteinuria is high in diabetic 
cats130 and an association between CKD and DM was recently 
observed,131 but 2 other studies suggest that DM might decrease
risk of CKD in cats.132 133 Symmetric dimethylarginine (SDMA), 
a marker of early CKD, was lower in DM cats than in healthy con-
trols.133 e risk of CKD was about 20% lower in cats with DM 
based on a large epidemiological study of primary care data.132
POORLY CONTROLLED DM
DEFINITION, INCLUDING DKA
Poor diabetic control implies that an individual with DM is 
experiencing excessive hyperglycemia, hypoglycemia, and/or 
glycemic variability. Poor control suggests presence of continu-
ing PU/PD, polyphagia, weight loss, failure to gain weight, weak-
ness, and/or gait abnormalities. Transient hypoglycemia oen 
causes no clinical signs, but neuroglycopenia should always be 
avoided or treated immediately. DKA (ch. 290) is usually the 
result of a concurrent disease and should be treated as an emer-
gency. Importantly, even small doses of insulin are sucient to 
prevent DKA in an otherwise stable diabetic with no concurrent 
diseases. erefore, avoiding DKA does not require ne-tuning 
of insulin therapy.
EXCESSIVE HYPERGLYCEMIA
Failure to curb hyperglycemia might be related to use of a short-
acting insulin (which might also be associated with episodes of 
hypoglycemia), improper insulin administration, or an underlying 
disease that causes insulin resistance or increases glycemic vari-
ability (Box 292.3). Insulin resistance is usually relative and clinical 
improvement should be observed as the dose of insulin is increased. 
In contrast, improper insulin administration is usually associated 
with no response or an erratic response that is not dose dependent.
INSULIN RESISTANCE
Definition and Differential Diagnosis
Insulin resistance is dened as reduced sensitivity to insulin or 
impedance of insulin’s action in target tissue(s). Insulin resistance 
is suspected when BG concentrations are perceived to be insu-
ciently lowered by insulin therapy. Resistance should be considered 
aer other causes have been ruled out (Box 292.3). From a clinical 
perspective, potential causes of insulin resistance include inam-
matory diseases such as obesity (ch. 150) or pancreatitis (ch. 278), 
bacterial infections due to periodontal disease (ch. 258) or UTI (ch. 
307), major organ failure, and concurrent endocrinopathies such as 
HS (ch. 280), hyperthyroidism (ch. 287), hypercortisolism (ch. 294), 
hypothyroidism (ch. 286), or obesity (ch. 150). Iatrogenic causes 
include giving any form of progestagen or glucocorticoid. About 10% 
of cats treated with prednisolone, particularly at high dosages and for 
long durations, develop DM.134
Pancreatitis (ch. )
Pancreatitis poses a diagnostic challenge, especially in a cat with 
DM. ere is some evidence that pancreatitis can precede DM, 
be the cause of DM, be the consequence of DM, or present as an 
unrelated comorbidity.1 Feline pancreatic lipase immunoreactiv-
ity (fPLI) is oen used as a screening test for pancreatitis in cats 
with vomiting, abdominal pain, or decreased appetite; however, 
results are frequently increased in DM cats with no clinical signs. 
Acute pancreatitis is a systemic inammatory disease that can 
lead to insulin resistance, but it is unclear if the situation is similar 
for chronic or subclinical pancreatitis.
Endocrine Diseases
Endocrine diseases causing insulin resistance usually cause clini-
cal and clinicopathologic changes distinguishable from those 
of DM. Some overlap occurs. HS is a diagnostic challenge since 
many aected cats only have signs of DM. Further, the insulin 
deciency of DM suppresses IGF-1. Concurrent hyperthyroidism 
can be a dicult diagnosis because of overlap in clinical signs, 
stress hyperglycemia in hyperthyroid cats, high rates of protein 
turnover that can falsely decrease fructosamine concentrations, 
and the euthyroid-sick syndrome in DM.135

Obesity
Obesity results in predictable insulin resistance but does not 
increase insulin requirement as markedly as do other endocri-
nopathies (ch. 150). Weight gain should be expected when giving 
insulin and should not prompt increases in dose “to correct for 
insulin resistance.”
HYPOGLYCEMIA AND GLYCEMIC VARIABILITY
Short- and Long-Term Consequences of Hypoglycemia
If not for hypoglycemia, patients with DM could have normal 
glycemic control throughout a lifetime.136 Hypoglycemia should 
be avoided whether or not it is associated with clinical signs, 
not only because severe hypoglycemia is life-threatening but 
also because of the long-term consequences of milder hypo-
glycemia. Repeated episodes of mild hypoglycemia in people 
lead to impaired counterregulatory responses to hypoglycemia, 
hypoglycemia-unawareness (absence of systemic clinical signs 
of hypoglycemia before neuroglycopenia ensues), and increased 
risk of future more severe hypoglycemia. Although these long 
term consequences have not yet been documented in cats, they 
have been well documented in humans and other species.137
In people, signs of hypoglycemia, e.g., hunger, shaking, and 
trembling, are caused by activating the central and autonomic 
nervous systems. Episodic hypoglycemia (as well as hypergly-
cemia) leads to progressive dysfunction in activation of these 
systems and is manifested as progressively lower hypoglycemia 
thresholds of activation.137 is leads to a decrease in the fre-
quency and severity of the warning signs of hypoglycemia and 
eventually results in sudden onset of seizures, coma, or death 
due to neuroglycopenia. If the same phenomenon exists in cats, 
it might explain the variability in threshold for appearance of 
clinical signs. Although some cats have clinical signs with BGs 
<60 mg/dL (<3.3 mmol/L), others are not clinical despite BGs 
<40 mg/dL (<2.2 mmol/L).138 Similarly, while the majority of 
cats that have BGs <20 mg/dL (<1.1 mmol/L) are comatose or 
stuporous, about a third only exhibit mental dullness, lethargy, 
weakness, and/or ataxia.138 Other clinical signs reported in 
hypoglycemic cats include vocalizing, twitching, seizures, bra-
dycardia, hypothermia, vomiting, panting, decreased appetite, 
inappropriate urination, diarrhea, and drooling.138 Importantly, 
BG concentrations in the above study were measured with a glu-
cose meter designed and calibrated for feline BGs (AlphaTrak, 
Zoetis) and might not apply when other methods are used to 
measure BGs and certainly not when IGc is measured. We rou-
tinely observe cats with IGc <40 mg/dL (<2.2 mmol/L) for hours 
that have no clinical signs. As is the case for BG meters designed 
for humans, CGMs that measure IGc might have a negative bias 
in the hypoglycemic range, i.e., they report values that are lower 
than the BG concentration.139
Impaired Counter-Regulatory Responses and the 
“Somogyi” Hypothesis
In people, dogs, and rodents, counterregulatory responses are 
oen impaired in DM.118 137 Frequency and severity depend on 
etiology, stage of disease, and glycemic control. e impairment 
results in decreased activation of counterregulatory hormones. 
e Somogyi hypothesis that hypoglycemia leads to exaggerated 
secretion of counterregulatory hormones with resultant hypergly-
cemia and insulin resistance is not likely. ere was no evidence 
of insulin-induced hyperglycemia based on lack of correlation 
between hyperglycemic events and preceding hypoglycemia, in 
one study.114 Yet, clinical experience suggests that in some cats, 
lowering insulin doses results in improved glycemic control. 
is might happen in cats in which counterregulation is still not 
impaired. In such cats, increasing insulin doses cause the BG to 
decrease below the threshold for activation of counterregulatory 
hormones sooner with the result that the glucose-lowering eect 
of insulin is masked sooner and for a longer time.
High Glycemic Variability
High glycemic variability is a risk factor for hypoglycemia and 
other DM complications in people. is is probably because it cap-
tures both the occurrence of hypo- and hyperglycemia excursions 
that separately lead to autonomic dysfunction and impaired coun-
terregulation.140 In cats, increased glycemic variability is associ-
ated with a decrease in glycemic control but it is unknown whether 
it is an independent risk factor for hypoglycemia or other compli-
cations.141 In diabetic cats treated with exenatide-ER, reduced gly-
cemic variability was associated with higher rates of remission.108

interstitial glucose concentrations. J Vet Intern Med. 2021;35:1279-1287. https://

THE HISTORY OF 
HYPERADRENOCORTICISM AND 
DEFINITIONS
T
he most common manifestation of 
hyperadrenocorticism (hypersecre-
tion of adrenocortical hormones) 
in dogs is hypercortisolism (HC). Chroni-
cally increased circulating glucocorticoids 
can be caused by long-term glucocorticoid 
administration (iatrogenic) or it can be 
naturally occurring, caused by excessive 
endogenous cortisol production.1 e clini-
cal condition associated with chronic corti-
sol excess is oen referred to as Cushing’s 
syndrome, aer the eminent neurosurgeon 
Harvey Cushing. Cushing’s clinical descrip-
tion of his patient Minnie G., published in 
his monograph in 1912, incorporated all the 
features that are now considered classic for 
HC. He speculated that the disorder may be 
related to the adrenal gland (hyperadrenal-
ism). In his later survey, published in 1932, 
he associated the condition with a basophil 
adenoma of the pituitary gland. e descrip-
tion of this fascinating discovery makes 
Harvey Cushing one of the greatest gures 
in endocrinology.2 In veterinary medicine 
the rst mention of HC appeared in 1939, 
when Verstraete and oonen reported the 
rst 2 dogs with “pituitary disorders.”3
Naturally occurring HC is usually 
the result of inappropriate excess secre-
tion of pituitary adrenocorticotropic hor-
mone (ACTH) or due to autonomous 
adrenal glucocorticoid secretion. ese 
are classied as ACTH-dependent HC 
and 
ACTH-independent 
HC, 
respec-
tively. 
ACTH-dependent 
HC 
includes 
pituitary-dependent HC (PDH; Cush-
ing’s disease) and the ectopic ACTH syn-
drome. ACTH-independent HC includes 
functional adrenocortical tumors (ATs) 
and primary ACTH-independent adreno-
cortical hyperplasia, such as food-induced 
HC. While PDH and functional ATs are 
well-recognized, reports of primary ACTH-
independent adrenocortical hyperplasia 
and ectopic ACTH syndrome are limited to 
individual case reports.4
PHYSIOLOGY: HYPOTHALAMUS-PITUITARY-
ADRENOCORTICAL AXIS (HPAA)
HYPOTHALAMUS AND PITUITARY GLAND
e hypothalamus is a part of the diencephalon located dorsal 
to the pituitary gland and ventral to the thalamus (ch. 241). e 
hypothalamus is intimately related to the pituitary gland through 
a portal vascular system and an anatomic link, as the pituitary is 
attached to the ventral part of the hypothalamus (median emi-
nence) via part of the neurohypophysis, the infundibular stalk.5
In dogs, the pituitary gland is divided into 3 functional units: the 
anterior lobe, the neurohypophysis or posterior lobe, and between 
them, the pars intermedia. e mature anterior lobe is populated 
by at least 5 distinct endocrine cell types distinguished by the 
tropic (stimulating) hormone each synthesizes: somatotrophs: 
growth hormone (GH); lactotrophs: prolactin; thyrotrophs: 
thyroid stimulating hormone (TSH); corticotrophs: the precur-
sor molecule pro-opiomelanocortin (POMC), which is broken 
down to ACTH and related peptides; and gonadotrophs: lutein-
izing hormone (LH) and follicle-stimulating hormone (FSH). e 
pars intermedia contains 2 distinct cell types that can synthesize 
POMC: predominant “A” cells are similar to corticotrophs and the 
less common “B” cells in which ACTH is cleaved into alpha-mela-
nocyte stimulating hormone (alpha-MSH) and corticotropin-like 
intermediate-lobe peptide (ACTH18-39 or CLIP).5
Secretion of ACTH from the anterior lobe is regulated by the 
hypothalamus and central nervous system via neurotransmit-
ters that release corticotropin-releasing hormone (CRH) and 
vasopressin (VP). CRH is released from small neurons in the 
paraventricular nucleus in response to neural signaling. VP is 
primarily released from CRH-containing neurons and is distinct 
from the VP involved in water homeostasis. When CRH and VP 
are released, the ACTH precursor, POMC, is secreted from the 
anterior lobe.6 e POMC is cleaved to produce ACTH. e pars 
intermedia is poorly vascularized and is predominantly inner-
vated by dopaminergic nerve bers from the hypothalamus.7 e 
POMC-producing cells of the pars intermedia are resistant to 
glucocorticoid suppression due to an absence of glucocorticoid 
receptors.
ADRENAL CORTEX
e adrenal cortex consists of three functional and morphologi-
cal layers: the outermost zona glomerulosa (zG), the middle zona 
fasciculata (zF) and the inner zona reticularis (zR). e zG is 
responsible for the production of the mineralocorticoid aldoste-
rone, regulated by plasma concentrations of potassium (K) and 
angiotensin-II. Acute increases in circulating ACTH also induce 
aldosterone secretion. e zF and zR function together as a unit 
Hyperadrenocorticism (Cushing’s Syndrome) in Dogs

eBooks.Health.Elsevier.com
and are the sole source of glucocorticoids, 
strictly regulated by the HPAA. e zR also 
produces androgens, e.g., dehydroepian-
drosterone (DHEA) and androstenedione.4
Adrenal steroidogenesis is initiated 
by ACTH, which binds to the G-protein 
alpha subunit (GNAS)-coupled melano-
cortin 2 receptor (MC2R), assisted by the 
melanocortin 2 receptor accessory protein 
(MRAP), and activates the cyclic adenos-
ine monophosphate (cAMP) and protein 
kinase A (PKA) pathways. Cholesterol is 
the precursor molecule for all adrenocor-
tical hormones and its availability is the 
limiting step for adrenal steroidogenesis. 
Cholesterol is converted to adrenocortical 
hormones in several steps, each catalyzed 
by enzymes (Figure 293.1).
Each zone in the adrenal cortex 
expresses a dierent set of steroidogenic 
enzymes. Dog steroidogenesis is unique 
with 
only 
one 
11-beta-hydroxylase 
enzyme (CYP11B1). Canine adrenals lack 
aldosterone-synthetase (CYP11B2), a spe-
cic enzyme involved in the last step in 
aldosterone synthesis in humans and many 
other species. is implies that in dogs the 
expression of the 17-alpha-hydroxylase 
enzyme (CYP17A1) in the zF is crucial for 
cortisol synthesis, while lack of CYP17A1 
in the zG restricts steroidogenesis to 
mineralocorticoid production.8 Activa-
tion of several transcription factors and 
steroidogenic enzymes in adrenocortical 
cells increase cortisol production, which 
in turn exerts negative feedback suppress-
ing hypothalamic CRH and VP while also 
suppressing ACTH secretion from the 
anterior lobe. Adrenal cortisol secretion has no eect on ACTH 
secretion from pars intermedia. ACTH and cortisol secretion are 
pulsatile and there is no circadian rhythm.9
PATHOGENESIS
ACTH-DEPENDENT HYPERADRENOCORTICISM
Pituitary-Dependent Hypercortisolism (PDH)
PDH, diagnosed in 80-85% of dogs with HC, is the most common 
naturally occurring form. PDH is caused by a functional, auton-
omous, ACTH-secreting, pituitary tumor that causes bilateral 
adrenocortical hyperplasia and chronic excessive secretion of glu-
cocorticoids (Figure 293.2).1 Pituitary tumors may arise from the 
anterior lobe or the pars intermedia. Tumors in the pars interme-
dia tend to grow larger and are resistant to dexamethasone sup-
pression. Endocrine testing is oen not discriminative, as large 
tumors from the anterior lobe may also be dexamethasone-resis-
tant.9 Most of these pituitary tumors are histologically classied 
as adenoma (well delineated from adjacent parenchyma). Inva-
sive adenomas (exhibiting local invasion of brain parenchyma or 
adjacent structures), carcinomas (with distant metastasis), and 
pituitary hyperplasia have also been described.10
Not much is known about the genetic background of canine 
HC. An activating mutation in the corticotropin releasing hor-
mone receptor gene (CRHR1) was detected in just 1 Poodle from a 
series of 50 dogs, failing to support the theory that ACTH-secreting 
adenomas develop aer chronic increases in positive feedback sig-
nals by constitutively activated CRH receptors.11 e research on 
oncogenesis of canine pituitary tumors has predominantly focused 
on candidate genes involved in pituitary tumorigenesis in humans. 
e high prevalence of HC in dogs and similarities in clinical 
presentation make the dog a suitable animal model for study-
ing Cushing’s disease in humans, but more molecular studies are 
needed. e gain-of-function of the Ubiquitin Specic Peptide 8 
gene (USP8) is a main driver behind the formation of corticotroph 
adenomas in humans. is peptide was not identied in dogs.12
Mutation of a T-box transcription factor (Tpit), obligate for the 
expression of POMC in humans and dogs, is not present in canine 
pituitary tumors.13 14
Ectopic ACTH Syndrome
Various tumor types, from well-dierentiated and indolent car-
cinoids to highly aggressive small-cell carcinomas, can secrete 
ACTH which, in turn, leads to HC. Dogs with resistance to dexa-
methasone, negative pituitary imaging, severely elevated endog-
enous ACTH (eACTH) concentrations, severe hypokalemia, 
and obvious clinical signs are candidates for the diagnosis of 
ectopic ACTH syndrome. A neuroendocrine tumor originating 
in the pancreas has been described as source of ectopic ACTH 
in dogs (Figure 293.2).15 16 Ectopic ACTH syndrome is rare and 
likely underdiagnosed in dogs. Ectopic ACTH-secreting tumors 
are usually small, dicult to visualize, and only identied aer 
extensive diagnostic imaging. Furthermore, treatment targeting 
the adrenal glands will resolve clinical signs of HC regardless 
of the ACTH source. Although typically slow-growing, most 
ectopic ACTH secreting tumors are malignant. e prognosis 
is unpredictable.
ACTH-INDEPENDENT HYPERADRENOCORTICISM
Autonomous Cortisol-Secreting ATs
Such tumors occur in about 15-20% of dogs with naturally occur-
ring HC, causing “adrenal-dependent” HC (AHC). Excessive 
production of cortisol by the AT negatively feeds back on the 
hypothalamus and pituitary gland, suppressing CRH and ACTH 
secretion. is causes atrophy of cortisol-secreting cells within 
the contralateral adrenal gland and the non-neoplastic cortisol-
secreting cells of the aected adrenal gland (Figure 293.2).1 One 
of the mechanisms behind ACTH-independent cortisol secretion 
is an activating mutation in the G-protein alpha-subunit gene 
(GNAS), described in about one-third of investigated cases.17
An activating GNAS mutation constitutively induces the PKA
pathway, which can lead to continuous and uncontrolled cor-
tisol production, can stimulate proliferative activity, and may 
be responsible for AT growth. is mutation is present in both 
benign and malignant ATs.
e histological criteria for dierentiating adrenocortical 
adenomas from carcinomas include tumor size, peripheral bro-
sis, capsular invasion, a trabecular growth pattern, hemorrhage, 
necrosis, single-cell necrosis, hematopoiesis, brin thrombi, 
cytoplasmic vacuolation, and the Ki67 proliferation index.18 In 
the largest case series of functional ATs, most were carcinomas 
(63% and 75%).18 19 e dierentiation, however, is challenging 
and the most reliable criterion of AT malignancy remains metas-
tases. Histopathological parameters can also serve as prognostic 
indicators aer adrenalectomy. ree parameters were signi-
cantly associated with survival: the percentage cells with clear/
vacuolated cytoplasm, the presence of necrosis, and the Ki67 
proliferation index. Together, they comprise the Utrecht score, 
an easy-to-use histopathological scoring system that can help to 
assess post-surgery prognosis (Figure 293.3) 20

Non–Cortisol-Secreting Adrenocortical Tumors
Some adrenocortical carcinomas secrete hormones other than 
cortisol (ch. 295). Most of these dogs have clinical signs of HC. 
Excessive 17-alpha-hydroxy-progesterone secretion and sub-
normal response to exogenous ACTH were demonstrated in 
published cases.21 22 Secretion of cortisol-precursors, instead of 
end-product cortisol, is presumably a result of malignant dedier-
entiation of the adrenocortical cells. In this process, a highly spe-
cialized adrenocortical cell loses its hormonal capacity and gains 
proliferative characteristics. Also loss of MC2R may be ascribed 
to the process of malignant transformation of an AT.4
Unilateral vs Bilateral Masses
Functional ATs are usually solitary and unilateral. Bilateral ATs 
have been diagnosed and may be dicult to distinguish from bilat-
eral adrenocortical hyperplasia in dogs with PDH.23 24 Concurrent 
pituitary and adrenal-dependent HC has been reported in 5% of 
all dogs with HC and in 10% of dexamethasone-resistant dogs.25
ACTH-Independent Adrenocortical Hyperplasia
ACTH-independent adrenocortical hyperplasia results from 
expression of ectopic receptors in the adrenocortical cell or from 
overactivity of eutopic receptors.26 In normal canine adrenal 
cortices and in a subset of ATs, the expression of LH and gastric-
inhibitory polypeptide (GIP) receptors has been documented 
(Figure 293.2). ese G-protein receptors bind with its ligand to 
induce cAMP and PKA pathways leading to steroidogenesis. Ecto-
pic GIP receptor expression underlies food-induced HC, which is 
the only documented form of ACTH-independent adrenocortical 
hyperplasia in dogs.27 Suspicion of food-induced HC is based on 
negative endocrine test results in a dog with strong clinical suspi-
cion of HC and in which there is no AT present. e major char-
acteristic is direct and indirect (via CRH) suppression of eACTH 
concentration by excess circulating cortisol concentrations ([corti-
sol]) secreted aer binding of GIP to its receptor at the membrane 
of the adrenocortical cell. Food-induced HC is a rare but likely 
underdiagnosed form of HC. Dogs with sub-diagnostic HC (see 
below) represent a potential category of food-induced HC, because 
endocrine testing will be negative if performed in fasted dogs. In 
food-induced HC, cortisol excess can only be documented aer 
food intake, because food consumption induces GIP secretion 
which binds to the GIP receptor and results in hypercortisolemia.
SUB-DIAGNOSTIC HC (REPLACING “ATYPICAL” OR 
“OCCULT” HC)
Dogs with clinical signs of HC but with “normal” endocrine test 
results are categorized as sub-diagnostic HC, a term introduced by 
the ALIVE group (http://esve.org). Dogs with sub-diagnostic HC 
have relatively high [cortisol] and adrenal size that is comparable to 
the hyperplastic adrenals of dogs with PDH.28 e most likely expla-
nation for non-diagnostic endocrine tests may be testing too early 
in the disease, or chance. Since HC is a chronic and progressive dis-
ease, repeating a screening test or tests 2-3 months later conrms the 
diagnosis of HC in most of these dogs. Among possible explanations 
for those with persistent sub-diagnostic HC are increased sensitivity 
to the glucocorticoid receptor, aberrant expression of adrenocortical 
receptors, intra-adrenal ACTH production, and others.29

PREVALENCE AND SIGNALMENT
HC is relatively common, but its true prevalence is not known. 
e incidence of new cases of HC is 1-2 per 1000 dogs per year, 
and the prevalence of PDH is estimated around 0.2% based on 
several studies.30-32 HC is a disease of middle-aged to older dogs. 
e age at diagnosis ranges from 6 months to 20 years, but the 
mean is about 11 years. A predisposition for HC has been shown 
for several breeds, including the Miniature Poodle, Dachshund, 
Terriers, Boxer, and Standard Schnauzer.31 33 34 “Blanket state-
ments” about breeds can be misleading because of varying breed 
distribution and popularity in dierent geographic areas. HC 
aects both males and females. A potential predisposition for 
females has been suggested, but lack of control groups weakens 
any conclusive relationships. Neutering of either sex seems to 
be associated with increased risk for HC. is could be related 
to the expression of the LH receptor in the adrenal cortex.35 If 
eutopic activity of this receptor is responsible for the develop-
ment of HC, it remains to be elucidated. Another explanation 
could be that neutered dogs develop HC because they live longer 
than intact dogs.34
HISTORY AND PHYSICAL EXAMINATION (PE)
COMMON SIGNS
Overview
e most common clinical signs of HC are a consequence 
of the combined gluconeogenic, immune-suppressive, anti-
inammatory, protein catabolic, and lipolytic eects of gluco-
corticoids.1 4 Clinical manifestations usually progress slowly and 
owners oen correlate health with appetite: the better the appetite, 
the better the health. Dogs with HC have excellent to ravenous 
appetites. Signs can be subtle and mistaken for aging, especially 
when dogs are diagnosed at an earlier stage of disease. Clinical 
signs, however, appear similar to those published in earlier studies 
that included many dogs with more chronic disease.34 36
Polyuria/Polydipsia (PU/PD), Urinary Tract Infections 
(UTIs)
e rst step in gaining a suspicion of HC should always be based on 
the history. PU/PD and polyphagia (PP) are the most common owner 
concerns. Potential causes of PU/PD (ch. 53) include cortisol-induced

Figure 293.2
Multiple pathogenic mechanisms of cortisol excess in dogs. A, Normal pituitary-adrenal axis: adrenocorticotropic hormone 
(ACTH) secretion from the pituitary gland stimulates cortisol production from both adrenal glands. Secretion is regulated by the feedback system. 
B, Pituitary-dependent hypercortisolism: pituitary corticotrope adenoma is secreting excessive amounts of ACTH and leads to hypercortisolism 
and bilateral adrenocortical hyperplasia. C, Adrenal-dependent hypercortisolism caused by cortisol-secreting adrenocortical tumor. Endogenous 
ACTH is suppressed, and contralateral adrenal cortex is atrophic. D, Ectopic ACTH secretion originating from a neuroendocrine pancreatic tumor. 
Excessive ACTH secretion causes bilateral adrenal hyperplasia. E, Food-induced hypercortisolism: gastric-inhibitory protein (GIP) is secreted 
from the intestinal tract and binds to aberrant GIP receptors in adrenal cortex. Elevated cortisol concentrations lead to suppression of pituitary 
ACTH production.

## Page 2160

SECTION XIX
•
Endocrine Disease
2008
eBooks.Health.Elsevier.com
inhibition of VP action at the renal tubular level, an increased neuro-
hypophysis VP release threshold, or central diabetes insipidus (CDI) 
if a large pituitary tumor compresses and/or invades the neurohy-
pophysis (ch. 282).37 Severe PU may lead to urine leakage, especially 
when sleeping, i.e., owner-perceived urinary incontinence. UTIs (ch. 
307) are common and most likely due to cortisol-induced immuno-
suppression and/or decreased bactericidal properties of dilute urine.38
Polyphagia (PP), Potbelly, Panting
Most dogs with HC have an “excellent appetite” or PP (ch. 19), a 
direct and unique eect of glucocorticoids in dogs. Other common 
clinical signs include abdominal distension and panting. Abdomi-
nal distension (potbelly, ch. 21) is believed to occur because of hep-
atomegaly (due to glycogen deposition), fat accumulation within 
the abdomen, and an enlarged bladder. Together, these increase 
the weight of the abdominal contents and together with decreased 
muscle tone (a result of protein catabolism) HC dogs oen have a 
pot belly. Panting is attributed to decreased pulmonary compliance, 
respiratory muscle weakness, or pulmonary hypertension.39
Dermatologic Signs
Skin changes typical for HC include alopecia, hyperpigmenta-
tion, comedones, thin skin, poor hair regrowth, and calcinosis 
cutis (ch. 12). Chronic glucocorticoid excess commonly induces 
follicular atrophy resulting in bilateral symmetrical truncal 
“endocrine alopecia.” Some dogs only retain coat on their head 
and distal extremities (Figure 293.4). in skin may be due to 
the anti-proliferative eects of glucocorticoids on broblasts and 
inhibition of collagen synthesis, causing epidermal atrophy. e 
thin skin and suppressed immune system predispose dogs to pyo-
derma. Calcinosis cutis, pathognomonic for HC, is characterized 
by irregular plaques in or under the skin due to dystrophic cal-
cium (Ca) deposition40 (Video 293.1).
PSEUDOMYOTONIA, LIGAMENT RUPTURE, TESTICULAR 
ATROPHY, ANESTRUS
A myopathy characterized by persistent active muscle contrac-
tions aer cessation of voluntary eort, known as Cushing’s 
pseudomyotonia, can aect the pelvic or all 4 limbs, causing a 
sti gait, but not pain (Video 293.2).41 When severe, dogs are 
unable to walk. Electromyography is characteristic (ch. 111) and 
muscle histology reveals noninammatory degenerative myopa-
thy. Uncommonly in larger breed dogs, HC can result in ligament 
laxity and tearing, causing lameness. Testicular atrophy in males 
and persistent anestrus in females are less commonly mentioned.

cytoplasm; and + 3 if necrosis is present. e adrenocortical tumor in this example had a Ki67 proliferation index of 22.2%, clear/vacuolated cytoplasm

Cortisol negative feedback to the pituitary suppresses synthesis 
and secretion of FSH and LH.
“SPACE-OCCUPYING” SIGNS (SIGNS DUE TO LARGE 
MASSES COMPRESSING OR INVADING ADJACENT 
TISSUES)
Clinical manifestations may develop secondary to the space-
occupying eect of a pituitary tumor or AT. Large pituitary 
tumors have been associated with dull mentation, circling, aim-
less wandering, pacing, ataxia, and behavior alterations (Videos 
293.3 and 293.4).42 43 While inappetence or anorexia are com-
mon owner concerns for dogs with large pituitary tumors, poor 
appetite is extremely atypical for a dog with HC. Poor appetite is 
always a concern in a dog with HC and should be taken seriously.
Large ATs may invade the phrenicoabdominal vein, caudal 
vena cava, or both. Such tumors have caused retroperitoneal 
hemorrhage, blood loss, abdominal pain, or formation of a tumor 
thrombus in a vein that leads to blockage and subsequent ascites 
or hind limb paresis (Video 293.5; ch. 238).44 e clinical mani-
festations of HC are usually chronic and progressive, but rupture 
of an AT or major vessel can result in acute life-threatening illness.
CLINICOPATHOLOGIC FINDINGS
OVERVIEW
Clinicopathologic abnormalities must be interpreted in the con-
text of the history and PE. Various alterations are common; none 
is pathognomonic. An absence of common abnormalities should 
decrease suspicion of HC. Conversely, failure to identify abnor-
malities does not, by itself, rule out HC. When clinical suspicion is 
strong, endocrine tests should be performed45 (Table 293.1).
COMPLETE BLOOD COUNT (CBC)
Common CBC ndings include eosinopenia (54-81% of dogs 
with HC), thrombocytosis (37-78%), lymphopenia (14-80%), 
neutrophilia (approximately 24%), monocytosis (approximately 
30%), and erythrocytosis (10-14%).45 ese white blood cell 
changes are referred to as a “stress leukogram” (ch. 58). Lympho-
penia is likely due to steroid-induced lympholysis, eosinopenia 
to bone marrow sequestration, and neutrophilia and monocyto-
sis to steroid-enhanced capillary demargination. Erythrocytosis 
(chs. 57, 173) and thrombocytosis likely result from direct bone 
marrow stimulation. Erythrocytosis may be due to hypoxemia 
in some dogs with HC as a result of hypoventilation caused by 
muscle weakness and obesity (Pickwick syndrome) or pulmonary 
thromboembolism (PT, ch. 219).39
SERUM BIOCHEMISTRY PROFILE
Liver Enzymes, Blood Urea Nitrogen (BUN), Blood 
Glucose (BG)
Common biochemical ndings in dogs with HC include 
increased alkaline phosphatase (ALP; 76-100%) and alanine ami-
notransferase (ALT; 80-95%) activities, hypercholesterolemia 
(73-90%), decreased BUN (34-56%), and mild increases in BG 
(20-57%). Increased ALP activities are the most consistent labo-
ratory abnormality and can be severe (>10 times above reference 
intervals), but these results remain nonspecic. ere is no corre-
lation between ALP activity and severity of HC, response to ther-
apy, or prognosis. e increase of ALP is due to steroid-induced 
expression of an ALP isoenzyme unique to dogs and to vacuolar 
hepatopathy (ch. 274).45 Healthy-appearing dogs with no owner 
concerns, regardless of increases in ALP measured for screening 
purposes, are extremely unlikely to have HC. Increased serum

•

2010
eBooks.Health.Elsevier.com
cholesterol (90% of HC dogs) is caused by steroid-induced lipoly-
sis. Hyperglycemia due to increased hepatic gluconeogenesis and 
decreased peripheral glucose utilization is caused by glucocorti-
coids interfering with insulin action at the cellular level (receptor 
and post-receptor defects). Carbohydrate tolerance, maintained 
by hyperinsulinemia, is not sucient to completely normalize 
BGs in some dogs. Mild fasting hyperglycemia is common, but 
their BGs usually remain below the renal threshold (175-224 mg/
dL [9.7-12.4 mmol/L]).46 When diabetes mellitus (DM, ch. 291) 
occurs in a dog with Cushing’s syndrome, it is oen insulin-resis-
tant. Decreased BUN is caused by increased renal clearance due 
to the PU.
Phosphate (PO), Calcium (Ca), Sodium (Na), 
Potassium (K)
HyperPO4 occurs frequently in dogs with PDH while circulating 
Ca is largely unaected. Both intact and whole parathormone 
(PTH) concentrations are increased.47 e combination of these 
seemingly contradictory ndings is called adrenal second-
ary hyperparathyroidism and is proposed to be a consequence 
of lower urinary PO4 secretion and increased renal Ca excre-
tion. Mild hyperNa and hypoK can be seen in dogs with HC. 
High [cortisol] saturates the protective 11-beta-hydroxysteroid 
dehydrogenase and enables cortisol to access the type I min-
eralocorticoid receptor. HypoK is a result of cortisol-induced 
mineralocorticoid eect and may reect the severity of 
hypercortisolemia.36
Canine Pancreatic Lipase Immunoreactivity (cPLI) and 
Bile Acids
Dogs with HC may have increased cPLI, supporting the diagnosis 
of pancreatitis in a few dogs, but the results should be interpreted 
with caution to avoid an incorrect diagnosis (ch. 277). Lipase 
activity measured by the 1,2-o-dilauryl-rac-glycero-3-glutaric 
acid-(6′-methylresorun) ester (DGGR) or 1,2-diglyceride (1,2-
DiG) assays lacks specicity in aiding the diagnosis of pancreatitis 
in HC dogs and is not recommended.48 49 e cause of increased 
serum pre- and postprandial bile acids, reported in 12% and 37% 
of dogs with HC, respectively, is not known but is not indicative 
of clinically signicant liver dysfunction.36
URINALYSIS (UA) AND URINE SPECIFIC GRAVITY (USG)
Most dogs with HC have USG <1.020; a USG >1.030 is rare in 
the absence of glucosuria.49 Proteinuria is common. A urinary 
protein : creatinine ratio >1 has been reported in about 45%, and 
>0.5 in about 70%, of dogs with HC. Proteinuria decreases with 
successful treatment and should be monitored.50 Bacteriuria or 
positive urine bacterial culture was identied in 10-50% HC 
dogs, whose predisposition for UTI is likely due to immunosup-
pression, dilute urine, and urine retention. Less than 5% of HC 
dogs with a positive urine culture exhibit lower urinary tract 
signs (chs. 70, 184, 302, 307).
COAGULATION PARAMETERS
A hypercoagulable tendency (ch. 171), dened as nding of at 
least 1 abnormality in a coagulation panel, has been observed in 
>80% of dogs with HC. e most common abnormalities include 
shorter prothrombin time, higher brinogen concentrations, and 
increased thrombin-antithrombin complexes.51 52 romboem-
bolism, however, is not common (see below).
THYROID FUNCTION TEST RESULTS
About 40-60% of dogs with HC have decreases in basal serum 
total thyroxine (TT4) and/or triiodothyronine (T3) (ch. 285). 
About 24% have decreased free thyroxine (fT4).53 Decreased 
thyroid hormone concentrations in dogs with HC are caused 
by alterations in serum thyroid hormone binding or periph-
eral hormone metabolism. Circulating TSH concentrations are 
normal in HC dogs with a low TT4. Clinical signs and rou-
tine laboratory changes associated with hypothyroidism and 
HC overlap and differentiation may be challenging. If a dog 
is suspected to have both, HC should be treated and thyroid 
replacement postponed. Once HC is controlled, thyroid func-
tion should be reassessed.
MACHINE LEARNING
Models aimed at predicting individual likelihood of HC are being 
developed and utilized in humans to aid clinical decision-making. 
Machine-learning-based classication algorithms include risk fac-
tors and estimate probability that a disease is present. A suciently 
large dataset is necessary to ensure reliability.54 A machine-learning 
tool to predict HC in dogs was introduced based on age, weight, 
sex, breed, clinical signs, USG, and ALP. e tool provided a good 
predictive performance and could be used in rst-line practice to 
support decision-making and increase condence in the diagnosis 
of HC in dogs.34,55
ENDOCRINE TESTING
INDICATIONS FOR TESTING
e initial suspicion of HC begins with the history and PE. Any 
continued suspicion should be supported by expected changes 
on routine blood and urine test results. If HC remains likely, 
the diagnosis should be conrmed by endocrine testing. Inci-
dental identication of an adrenal mass on imaging performed 
for a problem initially not related to HC (ch. 295) and/or docu-
menting a large pituitary mass on computed tomography (CT) 
or magnetic resonance imaging (MRI) to evaluate neurologic 
signs are additional indications to test for HC. Testing should 
be avoided if a dog is seriously ill, but concurrent illness must 
be considered.45
“CONFIRMING” A DIAGNOSIS OF HC AND DISCRIMINAT-
ING PITUITARY FROM ADRENAL HC
Conrming a diagnosis of HC requires demonstration of both 
increased cortisol production and decreased sensitivity of the 
HPAA to negative glucocorticoid feedback in a dog with clinical 
signs consistent with HC.56 e diagnosis of HC is a two-step pro-
cedure: rst, “screening” tests are used to distinguish dogs with 
HC from dogs without HC. Second, “discrimination” tests are 
used to dierentiate pituitary from adrenal HC, which is essential 
for choosing the optimal treatment for each dog. Some test results 
can be used for both screening and discrimination, e.g., the low-
dose dexamethasone suppression test (LDDST). Results of the 
urine cortisol to creatinine ratio (UCCR) when combined with 
the oral high-dose dexamethasone suppression test (HDDST) can 
also be used for both screening and discrimination. e ACTH 
stimulation test (ACTHst) only screens for HC.
No test is 100% accurate; each has advantages and disadvan-
tages. Testing depends on laboratory facilities and preferences 
of owner and veterinarian. Recent administration of glucocorti-
coids or progestagens may suppress the HPAA and alter endo-
crine test results. e cortisol assay used for plasma, serum, or 
urine is of utmost importance as reference ranges cannot be used 
interchangeably. Various methods of cortisol measurement gen-
erate variations in [cortisol], and ideally, reference ranges for 
each laboratory method should be established and periodically 
re-evaluated.45

Hyperadrenocorticism (Cushing’s Syndrome) in Dogs

eBooks.Health.Elsevier.com
THE LOW-DOSE DEXAMETHASONE SUPPRESSION TEST 
(LDDST)
Healthy vs HC Response to LDDST
e LDDST is commonly used to “conrm” a diagnosis of HC.49
In healthy dogs, dexamethasone circulates throughout the body 
including to the hypothalamus and pituitary where, within min-
utes, it suppresses secretion of CRH and ACTH. Absence of ACTH 
leaves the adrenal cortices with no stimulus to synthesize and 
secrete cortisol. us, aer giving dexamethasone, endogenous 
adrenal-source [cortisol] decreases to negligible amounts within 
an hour or so, and remains suppressed as long as the dexameth-
asone is active. Dogs with HC are abnormally resistant to dexa-
methasone suppression, i.e., their [cortisol] does not decrease as 
in the healthy dogs. e reported sensitivity and specicity of the 
LDDST range from 85-100% and from 44-73%, respectively.49 56
Protocol
Serum or plasma samples for [cortisol] are collected before, 4- and 
8-hours aer IV administration of 0.01 mg/kg dexamethasone. 
e test can begin at any time of the day. If some dexamethasone 
extravasates from the vein, the test is likely to remain reliable. 
Feeding should be avoided because lipemic serum can inuence 
cortisol measurements.
Interpretations
e basal and 8-hour post-dexamethasone cortisol results are 
used as the “screening” test to distinguish dogs with HC from dogs 
without HC. In healthy dogs, the plasma [cortisol] 4- and 8-hours 
aer dexamethasone decrease to or below the detection limit of 
assays. Complete suppression rejects the diagnosis of HC. [Cor-
tisol] consistent with HC is above the laboratory cut-o of 1 or 
1.4 mcg/dL (28-39 nmol/L), 8-hours post-dexamethasone. In dogs 
with HC, several patterns in [cortisol] response have been identi-
ed. Suppression of >50% from the basal [cortisol] at 4 or 8-hours, 
in dogs with conrmed HC, is compatible with PDH, i.e., “par-
tial suppression pattern.” An “escape” pattern is when [cortisol] at 
4-hours post-dexamethasone is below and the [cortisol] at 8-hours 
above the laboratory cut-o. e denition of “no suppression” 
aer an LDDST: cortisols at 4- and 8-hours above the laboratory 
cut-o and >50% of basal [cortisol]. No suppression is consistent 
with a diagnosis of HC but does not help in discriminating pitu-
itary from adrenal HC. Lack of suppression is suggestive of an AT 
or a large pituitary tumor and further evaluation with imaging is 
warranted. When the [cortisol] at 8-hours is below the laboratory 
cut-o, it should exclude HC. But if the [cortisol] at 4-hours post-
dexamethasone is increased, an “inverse” pattern is diagnosed. 
is result should be considered “equivocal” and further testing 
for HC pursued by diagnostic imaging (Figure 293.5A).56 e 
“lack of suppression pattern” had the highest positive predictive 
value for diagnosing HC (94%). e “partial suppression pattern” 
(68%) and “escape or inverse pattern” (37%) had lower predictive 
values.49
THE URINARY CORTISOL TO CREATININE RATIO (UCCR) 
AND ORAL HIGH-DOSE DEXAMETHASONE 
SUPPRESSION TEST (HDDST)
e UCCR represents an integrated measure of corticoid produc-
tion over time (usually 1 night), smoothing the eects of short-
term uctuations in plasma [cortisol]. Determination of basal 
UCCRs can be performed in tandem with an oral HDDST. e 
combination has the advantage of potentially demonstrating both 
increased cortisol production and decreased sensitivity to glu-
cocorticoid feedback, i.e., to dierentiate pituitary from adrenal 
HC. Reported UCCR sensitivity and specicity for HC diagnosis 
ranges from 75-100% and 20-25%, respectively.45
e urine for a UCCR should always be obtained at home by 
the owners, at least 2 days aer a visit to a veterinary clinic. e 
owner is instructed to collect 1-2 teaspoons (5-10 mL) of urine 
from the suspect HC dog on 2 consecutive mornings for baseline 
UCCR. Aer collecting the second urine sample, dexamethasone is 
administered PO in a dosage of 0.1 mg/kg, at 12:00, 18:00 and 24:00 
hours. e next morning, about 8 hours aer giving the last dose of 
dexamethasone, the third urine sample is collected.4 e reliability 
of the UCCR test depends also on the availability of a trustworthy 
assay and the method specic reference ranges and cutos.57
THE ACTH STIMULATION TEST (ACTHst)
Sensitivity, Specificity, Interpretation
e ACTHst assesses adrenocortical reserve. Its use is based on 
the concept that dogs with HC have excess response to ACTH. 
It is the gold standard test for diagnosing hypoadrenocorticism 
(ch. 296) and iatrogenic HC but does not assess the sensitivity of 
the HPAA to negative glucocorticoid feedback. Results provide 
no information for dierentiating pituitary from adrenal HC.45
e sensitivity of the ACTHst for naturally occurring canine 
PDH ranges between 80-83% and 57-63% for adrenal-dependent 
HC. e low sensitivity of the ACTHst in adrenal-dependent 
HC is likely explained by downregulation of the ACTH-receptor 
(MC2R) in adrenocortical carcinomas; specicity ranges from 
59-93%.56
Protocol
e ACTHst requires an hour and can be performed at any time 
of the day. Plasma [cortisol] is measured before and 1 hour aer 
administration of synthetic ACTH; peak cortisol secretion occurs at 
60-90 minutes. Synthetic ACTH is commercially available in liquid, 
lyophilized, or aqueous porcine ACTH gel forms. Liquid synthetic 
ACTH is given IV or IM at a dosage of 5 mcg/kg, or 250 mcg/dog 
>10 kg or 125 mcg/dog <10 kg. Aer 5 mcg/kg IV, no dierence was 
detected between 60- and 90-minute [cortisol].45 e use of 1 mcg/
kg of synthetic ACTH is not recommended since results caused a dif-
ferent interpretation in about 25% of dogs suspected of HC. No dif-
ference in response following liquid (250 mcg/kg IV) or lyophilized 
(250 mcg/kg IM) solutions were detected in healthy dogs. Synthetic 
ACTH gel formulation is administered IM at a dosage of 2.2 IU/kg. 
Use of compounded ACTH preparations is discouraged because 
they may contain variable concentrations of ACTH, may lead to 
lower post-ACTH [cortisol], and could lead to an incorrect clinical 
interpretation. Because of its low sensitivity for adrenal-dependent 
HC and the inability to dierentiate pituitary from adrenal HC, the 
diagnostic usefulness of the ACTHst is inferior to the LDDST (Figure 
293.5B).
ENDOGENOUS ACTH (eACTH)
ACTH is secreted from the pituitary gland in an episodic, pul-
satile fashion. Random measurement of circulating eACTH 
concentrations overlap considerably between healthy dogs and 
those with PDH. us, its measurement is not useful to screen 
for HC but it is the most accurate stand-alone biochemical test 
for discriminating pituitary from adrenal origin in dogs with 
proven HC.56 In dogs with adrenal-dependent HC, the eACTH 
is expected to be low or undetectable. Low eACTH is character-
istic of food-induced HC. e accuracy for discriminating pitu-
itary from adrenal HC depends upon the analytical sensitivity, the 
working range of the assay, and proper sample handling. eACTH 
is rapidly degraded by proteases if samples are not cooled. Blood 
should be collected into chilled, silicon-coated glass or plastic 
tubes containing EDTA, centrifuged within 15 minutes (ideally in 
a cooled centrifuge), and the plasma transferred to plastic tubes 
and frozen immediately.45

THE HIGH DOSE DEXAMETHASONE SUPPRESSION TEST 
(HDDST)
e IV-HDDST is used to help dierentiate pituitary from adre-
nal HC when results of the LDDST demonstrate no suppression. 
e protocol is the same as for the LDDST except that the dosage 
is 0.1 mg/kg IV. When suppression is >50% in a dog with HC, 
it is of pituitary origin. However, in dogs exhibiting no suppres-
sion on LDDST (the indication to obtain a HDDST), the HDDST 
provides dierentiation in only 12% additional dogs and is no 
longer recommended. Instead, diagnostic imaging and eACTH 
measurements are advised to dierentiate pituitary from adrenal 
HC in dogs with non-suppressible LDDST.45
DIAGNOSTIC IMAGING
INDICATIONS
Diagnostic imaging should be performed aer HC has been con-
rmed with endocrine testing. Diagnostic imaging, preferably of 
both pituitary and adrenal glands, should be included in the diag-
nostic evaluation of dogs with naturally occurring HC to dierenti-
ate pituitary from adrenal HC, to choose the most optimal treatment, 
and to estimate prognosis.58 Finding a normal pituitary and normal 
adrenal glands on diagnostic imaging does not rule out HC.
PITUITARY: COMPUTED TOMOGRAPHY (CT) OR 
MAGNETIC RESONANCE IMAGING (MRI)
Estimating Pituitary Enlargement
e pituitary gland area can be visualized with CT or MRI (chs. 
112, 245) and enlargement can be estimated using the ratio of 
pituitary height vs the area of the brain (P/B value), measured 
through the midline of the pituitary on an axial image. A P/B 
value >0.31 mm/mm2 indicates pituitary enlargement.9 e P/B 
value can be used regardless of head dimension, breed, or size of 
the dog. Pituitary tumors in dogs with PDH range from micro-
scopic nests of cells to large masses. Small pituitary masses may 
not cause enlargement of the pituitary gland (normal P/B value) 
but may still be identied with contrast-enhanced CT and MRI.59
Dynamic Contrast-Enhanced CT
e blood supply of the posterior pituitary gland is direct (arte-
rial) whereas that of the anterior pituitary gland is mainly indirect 
via the pituitary portal system. Dynamic contrast-enhanced CT 
takes advantage of this dierence. In healthy dogs given IV con-
trast medium, the posterior pituitary gland is identied before the 
anterior pituitary: the “pituitary ush.” Its absence can indicate 
atrophy of the posterior pituitary due to compression by an ante-
rior pituitary tumor. Displacement or distortion of the ush can 
be used to identify and localize pituitary tumors60 (Video 293.6).
Classifying Tumor Size
Classifying canine pituitary tumors as microadenomas (<10 mm 
height) and macroadenomas (>10 mm height), adapted from 
humans, is not useful and should be avoided. Pituitary adenomas 
6-10 mm in height enlarge the gland of small breeds and can result 
in neurological signs. Such masses cannot be classied as microad-
enomas.61 e P/B value has the highest discriminatory power for 
distinguishing enlarged from non-enlarged pituitaries. Pituitary 
enlargement is detected in 64% of dogs with dexamethasone-resistant 
HC. Imaging may provide additional information on the prognosis 
and quality of life of dogs with HC.25 Advanced imaging with a CT/
MRI scan is indicated not only in dogs intended to be treated by sur-
gery or radiation, but also before starting medical treatment.
ADRENALS
Abdominal Ultrasonography (US)
Abdominal US is an excellent tool for evaluating the symmetry, 
size, shape, and echogenicity of the adrenal glands. US is also valu-
able in identifying vascular or local so tissue invasion of an AT. 
Adrenal thickness is the most reliable indicator of enlargement, 
considering breed and body size dierences.62 Using separate 
upper limits of “normal” for le and right adrenal gland thick-
ness, obtained from healthy dogs, results in high sensitivity (96%) 
for identifying adrenomegaly in dogs with HC (Video 293.7).23
In dogs with PDH, bilateral symmetrical enlargement of the 
adrenal glands is expected; however, normal-sized adrenals and 
mild asymmetry are common. Moderate to severe asymmetry 
with contralateral adrenocortical atrophy (adrenal thickness 
<4-5 mm) is consistent with an AT (Video 293.7).63 Measuring 
the smaller adrenal gland may best dierentiate HC dogs with 
functional unilateral AT from dogs with PDH. Equivocal adre-
nal asymmetry occurs in about 40% of dogs with HC, making 
discrimination of pituitary from adrenal HC dicult. Also, con-
current conditions such as non-functional AT or pheochromo-
cytoma, and bilateral ATs may also result in adrenal asymmetry 
in dogs with HC.
Some adrenal structural features can increase suspicion for 
malignancy.64 65 A heterogeneous appearance, reecting destruc-
tion of normal tissue architecture with necrosis and hemorrhage, 
is suggestive of AT rather than adrenocortical hyperplasia. Vascu-
lar invasion and adrenal size >2 cm are the 2 most reliable criteria 
consistent with a malignant AT.18
Contrast-Enhanced Ultrasonography (C-EUS)
C-EUS is a non-invasive method for quantifying adrenal gland 
vascular patterns. In C-EUS, a contrast agent is administered IV 
and time-intensity curves are generated for the adrenal cortex, 
adrenal medulla, and ipsilateral renal artery of both adrenals. 
Peak perfusion contrast intensity, adrenal blood ow, and blood 
volume in hyperplastic adrenals of dogs with PDH was twice 
that of healthy controls. Further studies are needed to evaluate 
its usefulness as a dierentiating test between adrenocortical 
hyperplasia and bilateral ATs as well as between dierent types 
of ATs.66 67
Adrenal Gland CT
CT imaging of the adrenal glands is a sensitive tool for assess-
ing size, structure, and symmetry. It is more sensitive than US for 
identifying vascular invasion. In dogs with an AT intended for 
surgery, CT imaging is extremely valuable to screen for metas-
tases in abdomen and thorax. CT imaging of an AT commonly 
shows a heterogeneous pattern of contrast enhancement on late 
phase CT images.68 69 Studies on intra-tumoral contrast attenua-
tion with regular and triple-phase helical CT scans showed more 
rapid excretion of contrast medium from adrenocortical adeno-
mas than carcinomas; however, diagnosis of malignancy based on 
CT imaging alone is unreliable due to overlapping characteristics 
between dierent tumor types.68 70
CT imaging of the adrenals and pituitary is advised in dogs 
with HC.25 CT imaging, particularly using reformatted images, is 
100% sensitive and 98% specic in calculating the adrenal diam-
eter ratio (maximal diameter of the larger gland/minimal diam-
eter of the smaller gland). is can be helpful for discriminating 
pituitary from adrenal HC or for identifying dogs with concur-
rent pituitary and adrenal HC.69 Lack of advanced imaging to 
allow diagnosis of concurrent pituitary and adrenal HC may lead 
to unsuccessful control of HC and an unrealistic estimate of the 
prognosis and quality of life.25

TREATMENT
GOALS AND TREATMENT OF CHOICE
Ideally, treatment of HC would eliminate the source of either 
ACTH or autonomous cortisol excess, normalize cortisol, elim-
inate clinical signs, reduce long-term complications, decrease 
mortality, and improve quality of life.58 Surgical removal of the 
causal tumor and radiotherapy are the only treatment options 
with the potential to eliminate the source of either ACTH or 
autonomous cortisol excess but these options are not widely 
available and not suitable for every dog. Medical therapy is 
commonly used to eliminate clinical signs of HC. Various com-
binations of radiation, medical, and surgical treatments are 
possible. Since treatment choice depends on the form of HC, 
discriminating pituitary from adrenal HC is essential. Other 
important factors are the severity of the condition, presence of 
concurrent disease, age, breed, and client preferences. With-
out treatment, dogs with PDH have a median survival time of 
359 days (95% condence interval [CI], 271-829)71 to 506 days 
(95% CI, 292-564).72 ere are no survival data for untreated 
dogs with an AT.
TREATMENT AT THE PITUITARY LEVEL
Hypophysectomy (Removal of the Entire Pituitary)
Hypophysectomy in dogs is performed using a transsphenoidal 
approach.73 In a recent study of 306 dogs with PDH that under-
went hypophysectomy, 91% were alive aer 4 weeks, and remis-
sion was conrmed in 92% of survivors.74 Of the dogs that went 
into remission, disease recurrence was observed in 27%. e 
median survival time was 781 days (range, 0-3808 days) and the 
median disease-free interval of the dogs in remission was 951 
days (range, 31-3808 days). Replacement therapies aer hypophy-
sectomy include life-long glucocorticoids and thyroxine. Desmo-
pressin, a synthetic VP analogue, is needed temporarily in all dogs 
and permanently in about 10%. e tissue removed via hypophy-
sectomy includes the posterior lobe which stores and secretes 
VP.75 Transient CDI (ch. 282) occurred more frequently (62%) 
in dogs that had enlarged pituitary glands as compared with the 
non-enlarged (44%).76
e most common complications of hypophysectomy are 
perioperative death, transient mild postoperative hypernatremia, 
prolonged or permanent CDI, recurrence of HC, and transient 
reduction or cessation of tear production. Factors that nega-
tively inuence the prognosis include a high P/B value, old age, 
increased preoperative eACTH and UCCR, and dexamethasone 
resistance on endocrine testing.74 77 An early diagnosis of cor-
ticotroph adenoma is expected to have the best outcome when 
hypophysectomy is the rst treatment for dogs with a non-
enlarged or moderately enlarged pituitary. Although a high P/B 
value is a negative prognostic indicator, hypophysectomy remains 
a good treatment option (Figure 293.4).74 78
e main limitation of hypophysectomy is availability, being 
limited to large veterinary centers with an established team of 
experienced surgeon(s), anesthetist(s), critical care specialist(s) 
and endocrinologist(s). Prior to hypophysectomy, dogs must have 
a thorough work-up to ensure they are acceptable anesthetic and 
surgical candidates. Aer hypophysectomy, excellent owner com-
pliance is mandatory to successfully manage hormone replace-
ments. e owner needs to follow medication schemes tightly and 
learn to anticipate extremely stressful situations. In the long term, 
screening for recurrence is recommended by checking a UCCR 
twice yearly.75
Pituitary Radiotherapy
Pituitary irradiation can cause gradual reduction in tumor size 
over a period of 1-16 months but does not usually eliminate the 
autonomous hypersecretion of ACTH or clinical signs. Tempo-
rary or permanent trilostane therapy may be required to manage 
HC.61,79 Usually a total dose of 36-48 Gy is administered in 3-4 Gy 
fractions, requiring 12 anesthesias. Factors predictive of outcome 
for dogs with PDH treated with radiation therapy include tumor 
size and severity of neurologic signs. Dogs with severe neurologic 
signs have a poorer prognosis than dogs with mild or no signs.61,71
e most common acute adverse eects (i.e., occurring within 
4 months of completing radiation therapy) include anorexia and 
depression due to pituitary hemorrhage. e most common late-
onset adverse eect is chronic otitis media, which does not usu-
ally cause the typical clinical signs of head tilt, nystagmus, and/
or pain. Some develop hearing impairment. e reported median 
survival time of dogs with PDH following radiation therapy 
ranges from 539 to 1405 days.71,79 Radiotherapy can be used as 
an adjuvant treatment aer hypophysectomy for recurrence or if 
surgical mass debulking was performed to relieve neurological 
signs.42
Stereotactic Radiotherapy
is involves the precise delivery of highly conformal dose-
intense radiation to well-demarcated tumors. is unique bio-
logical mechanism distinguishes stereotactic radiation therapy 
from other forms of external beam radiotherapy (ch. 318). Ste-
reotactic radiation therapy has emerged as an attractive treatment 
for various canine brain tumors due to the shortened treatment 
course and fewer anesthesias as compared with standard radia-
tion therapy.80 Studies on stereotactic radiation therapy for canine 
pituitary tumors have been limited and have included dogs with 
ACTH-secreting tumors and dogs with nonfunctional tumors. 
Dogs with PDH received 24 Gy in 1 dose or divided into 3 frac-
tions. Median overall survival was 245 days (range 2-626 days), 
shorter than reported with conventional radiation therapy.81 e 
small number of treated dogs precludes detailed survival analysis 
but a trend towards shorter survival in dogs with larger tumors 
was observed.82 Dogs with nonfunctional masses had longer 
medial overall survival than dogs with HC.
Stereotactic Radiosurgery
is precise image-guided targeted form of radiotherapy is deliv-
ered in a single high-dose session using the “Gamma Knife.” It is 
well tolerated in humans with pituitary tumors and may require 
less time for a response than aer conventional radiation therapy. 
e same technique has recently been applied to dogs with pitu-
itary tumors and showed promising results in reducing tumor 
volume.83,84
TREATMENT AT THE ADRENAL LEVEL
Adrenalectomy: Treatment of Choice for a Solitary 
Tumor
Complete tumor removal is curative, resolving current or future 
issues related to mass compression or tumor invasion. Success-
ful surgery resolves clinical signs of HC without need for lifelong 
replacement medication. Glucocorticoid-producing cells in the 
opposite adrenal are usually atrophied at the time of surgery due 
to chronic cortisol excess suppressing CRH and ACTH secretion. 
us, temporary glucocorticoid replacement should be antici-
pated.4 Aer bilateral adrenalectomy, dogs require both glucocor-
ticoids and mineralocorticoids lifelong. Dosages are monitored 
according to the treatment protocol for Addison’s disease (ch. 296).

## Page 2167

CHAPTER 293
•
Hyperadrenocorticism (Cushing’s Syndrome) in Dogs

eBooks.Health.Elsevier.com
Adrenalectomy: Laparoscopic vs Open Surgery
Adrenalectomy is commonly performed via ventral or paracos-
tal open laparotomies but laparoscopic adrenal tumor removal is 
gaining popularity.85 86 Its advantages over open surgery include 
reduced manipulation of other abdominal organs, an excellent 
view of abdominal structures, decreased surgical wound compli-
cations, improved postoperative comfort, shorter hospital stays, 
and shorter recovery periods. e two major selection criteria for 
choosing a modality are tumor size and presence of vascular inva-
sion. When an AT is >5 cm in diameter and/or invasion of the 
caudal vena cava is recognized, the open technique is preferred to 
laparoscopy by many surgeons.
Adrenalectomy: Complications and Survival Times
Perioperative mortality rates are as low as 6-8% in dogs with a 
small AT without evidence of vascular invasion87 to 24% with 
invasive ATs.88 e main perioperative complications include 
minor to severe hemorrhage, hypotension, hypertension, bra-
dycardia, tachycardia, and death.87 89 90 e tumor capsule can 
rupture, possibly more oen in laparoscopic than in open adre-
nalectomies, but does not necessarily lead to tumor regrowth. 
e main complications that can occur postoperatively include 
pancreatitis and thromboembolism. Reported HC recurrence 
rates vary from 12-30%. Recurrence of HC can be due to AT 
regrowth, functional metastases, or development of PDH. 
Median survival times for dogs with adrenal-dependent HC 
undergoing adrenalectomy range from 778 days91 to 953 days.89
AT diameter was signicantly associated with survival, while 
venous invasion, capsule rupture, surgery duration, or medical 
treatment before surgery had no inuence on survival. Dogs 
with an AT diameter <3 cm had signicantly better survival 
times aer adrenalectomy than those with a tumor ≥3 cm.20
Adrenalectomy is not recommended in dogs with metastasis, 
underscoring the importance of presurgical imaging. Vascu-
lar invasion does not necessarily exclude adrenalectomy. Some 
studies indicate that AT with vena cava invasion does not aect 
perioperative mortality since techniques to remove tumor and 
tumor thrombus have improved (Video 293.5).24 92 However, 
when vascular invasion extends beyond the hepatic hilus, peri-
operative mortality rates increase.88 93 Forty percent of dogs 
with a thrombus extending beyond the diaphragm survived sur-
gery as compared with 73-81% when the thrombus terminated 
before the diaphragm, suggesting a poorer prognosis for dogs 
with a tumor thrombus that extends beyond the diaphragm.88
Stereotactic Radiotherapy
Hypofractionated stereotactic volume-modulated radiotherapy 
was reported in 9 dogs with invasive ATs.94 e dogs received 
30-45 Gy in 3 or 5 consecutive daily fractions. eir median sur-
vival time was 1030 days. e diameter and volume were reduced 
32% and 30%, respectively. ese encouraging results may pro-
vide a feasible and eective therapeutic option for dogs that have 
an AT with vascular invasion.
TRILOSTANE MEDICAL THERAPY
Description and Mechanisms of Action
Trilostane is a synthetic steroid analogue that competitively 
inhibits the steroidogenic enzyme 3-beta-hydroxysteroid dehy-
drogenase, required for synthesis of all steroid hormones (Figure 
293.1).95 Trilostane inhibits cortisol and mineralocorticoid pro-
duction. Inhibiting cortisol synthesis decreases negative feedback 
to the pituitary, leading to increases in ACTH. Inhibiting aldo-
sterone causes a compensatory increase in plasma renin activ-
ity.96 Additionally, trilostane possibly also aects other enzymes 
in the steroidogenesis cascade, such as 11-beta-hydroxysteroid 
dehydrogenase.97
Dose
Trilostane is registered for the medical management of both pitu-
itary and adrenal HC, but most studies have been limited to dogs 
with PDH. Trilostane is absorbed rapidly from the gastrointestinal 
(GI) tract and should always be given with food. ere are marked 
variations in individual trilostane dose requirements. Current rec-
ommendations are to start with much lower doses than was origi-
nally recommended by the manufacturer. Lower doses have been 
eective and induce fewer adverse eects.98 99 Larger dogs generally 
need a lower dose/kg body weight than smaller dogs. Because the 
duration of cortisol suppression is (far) less than 12 hours in most 
dogs, giving the drug q12h improves the clinical response, keeps 
the total daily dose relatively low, and signicantly reduces inci-
dence of adverse eects. e initial recommended dosage is 0.5-1 
mg/kg PO q12h for dogs with PDH and 0.2-0.5 mg/kg for dogs 
with adrenal-dependent HC (Figure 293.6).100 101
Time to a Clinical Response
Within weeks, an adequate dose of trilostane can increase the 
dog’s activity and reduce PU/PD/PP. Several months may be 
needed to observe notable improvements on the skin and hair 
coat. e haircoat can sometimes initially appear to worsen, due 
to shedding of telogen hairs and dry skin scales.102
Adverse Effects
Trilostane is usually well tolerated. A signicant number (>10%) of 
trilostane-treated dogs fail to respond. e most common adverse 
eect is transient hypocortisolism (insucient glucocorticoids), 
possibly combined with or followed by complete hypoadrenocor-
ticism (shortage of both glucocorticoids and mineralocorticoids). 
One study suggested that dogs have a 15% chance of at least 1 
episode of clinical hypocortisolism within the rst 2 years of tri-
lostane treatment.103 In most dogs, adverse eects resolve quickly 
aer trilostane is withdrawn. In such dogs, continuing treatment 
with a lower dose is recommended when clinical signs of HC 
recur. However, hypoadrenocorticism can be permanent or fatal 
in some dogs, possibly the result of adrenal necrosis.104 105
The “Best” or “Key” Monitoring Aid
ere has been much frustration in identifying a consistent and 
reliable monitoring tool when treating a dog with trilostane. Ide-
ally, test results identify proper dose, frequency, and would reliably 
indicate if a dog is over dosed or under dosed. No available blood 
or urine test meets these criteria. e only “test result” that has 
been consistent and reliable is owner opinion. Regardless of any in-
hospital test chosen to monitor trilostane treatment, it is imperative 
to understand the owner’s opinion. e value of owner opinion is 
enhanced dramatically through owner education and by assuring 
owners that their observations are extremely important.
Monitoring with the ACTHst
Results of the ACTHst reect adrenocortical reserve capacity for 
secreting cortisol (Figure 293.7).106 Since trilostane has its main 
eects for only a few hours aer administration, timing the start of 
the ACTHst is crucial.107 108 us, the ACTHst should be started 
2-4 hours aer trilostane administration. Despite its widespread 
use, ACTHst results have never been validated as a monitoring 
tool for trilostane therapy and several reports have indicated their 
lack of reliability. Results can be completely altered by starting the 
test earlier or later, rather than indicating level of control.109 110
Possibly overdosed dogs with baseline and post-ACTHst [corti-
sol] <2 mcg/dL (<55 nmol/L), 3-6 hours aer trilostane, had much 
higher cortisols if the test was repeated 6 hours later. Continuing 


---

## Page 2168

SECTION XIX
•
Endocrine Disease
2016
eBooks.Health.Elsevier.com
trilostane therapy without reducing the trilostane dose did not 
lead to clinical hypoadrenocorticism and seemed safe. Failure to 
respond adequately to ACTHst at a time when trilostane is at its 
peak, is not the same as a trilostane overdose.
Monitoring with Pre-Pill Cortisols
A proposed alternative to using the ACTHst is to measure the pre-
pill [cortisol] and compare it with the clinical signs observed by 
owners. e pre-pill cortisol was found to better reect clinical 
control than ACTHst results.110 e question remains, however, 
whether a single cortisol measurement in trilostane-treated dogs 
with HC is suciently reliable. Cortisol uctuates and is inuenced 
by various factors, e.g., stress. Indeed, the agreement between two 
pre-pill [cortisol] taken 1 hour apart and 11-13hours aer the last 
trilostane application is only moderate. Stress during any evalu-
ation can inuence [cortisol]. When using pre-pill [cortisol] to 
monitor trilostane therapy, one should note any stress during han-
dling of the dog to more condently interpret test results.111 In dogs 
in which pre-pill [cortisol] is below the target range, a trilostane 
dose reduction should be considered, because it is possible that the

adrenal reserve is not optimal. In unwell and possibly overdosed 
dogs, pre-pill cortisol should be replaced by the ACTHst (Figures 
293.6 and 293.7). e principle of pre-pill cortisol is not compa-
rable to that of the ACTHst: it is not a measure of adrenocortical 
reserve. is is relevant, because dogs with excellent clinical con-
trol and an adequate pre-pill cortisol can have insucient adre-
nocortical reserve capacity. is can become clinically relevant in 
stressful situations (illness).103 is leaves the ACTHst superior in 
evaluating the safety of trilostane therapy.
Other Monitoring Tools
Among other monitoring variables, the UCCR, cortisol/ACTH 
ratio, serial serum cortisols aer trilostane administration, and 
USG have not been reliable for monitoring trilostane therapy in 
dogs with HC. None consistently correlated with owner opinion 
and none reliably discriminated dogs receiving an adequate trilo-
stane dose from dogs that might benet from an increase in dose 
or frequency.100 101 112
Haptoglobin (Hp), a moderate acute phase protein, has 
been demonstrated to increase in dogs with hypercortisolemia 
and a reduced Hp concentration is detected during trilostane 
treatment.113 Measuring serum Hp in dogs with HC provided 
additional information on the response to the therapy with 
trilostane.114 However, the Hp is a positive acute-phase protein, 
and the results could be biased if a dog has a concomitant inam-
matory condition (i.e., UTI). For this reason, it is advisable to 
interpret Hp concentrations individually and to consider the con-
comitant comorbidities as well.
Prognosis
In dogs with PDH, trilostane eectively controls HC clinical signs 
but does not aect the pituitary tumor. Continued tumor growth 
should be expected, even in dogs with non-enlarged pituitary 
glands at the time of diagnosis (i.e., P/B value <0.31 mm/mm2). In 
healthy dogs, the P/B value signicantly increases following trilo-
stane therapy, but this has not been studied in dogs with PDH.115
Nonetheless, especially in younger dogs, a CT or MRI scan aer 
1 year of treatment should be considered to re-evaluate pituitary 
size (ch. 112). e reported median survival times of dogs with 
PDH treated with trilostane range from 549 to 662 days when 
treated once daily,116 and about 852 days when dogs were on once 
or twice daily regimens.117 In dogs with a cortisol-secreting AT, 
trilostane is only palliative. It can reduce clinical signs but does 
not aect AT growth or development of metastasis. e reported 
median survival times of dogs with ATs treated with trilostane 
range from 353 to 596 days.118 119
