MITOTANE MEDICAL THERAPY
Description and Mechanisms of Action
Mitotane (o,p′-DDD) is an adrenocorticolytic agent derived 
from the insecticide DDT that causes progressive adrenocorti-
cal necrosis and atrophy in dogs. Mitotane inhibits the steroido-
genic enzymes cytochrome P450 cholesterol side-chain cleavage 
enzyme (CYP11A1) and CYP11B1, which contribute to inhibi-
tion of cortisol synthesis and the activation of other cytochrome 
P450 enzymes such as cytochrome P450 enzyme 3A4 (CYP3A4), 
which leads to increased metabolic clearance of glucocorti-
coids. Although mitotane has been used to treat HC in dogs for 
decades, the exact mechanism of action was poorly understood. 
A recent study identied inhibition of sterol-O-acyl-transferase 1 
(SOAT1), an enzyme that catalyzes the conversion of free choles-
terol to cholesterol esters, as one of its actions. Inhibition of this 
conversion increases the amount of free cholesterol in adrenocor-
tical cells, which can lead to endoplasmic reticulum stress and cell 
apoptosis.120
Indications and Dose
Use of mitotane to treat PDH in dogs has largely been replaced 
by trilostane. Trilostane is as effective, safer to handle, and 
causes fewer adverse effects. However, in dogs with an AT, 
mitotane remains a good option because it can destroy AT 
cells.58 The goal of mitotane therapy in AT is not only to reduce 
cortisol production but also to destroy the neoplastic cells. A 
non-selective protocol that affects the entire adrenal cortex is 
recommended using daily doses of 50 to 75 mg/kg PO for 5 
days and then q48h for 40 days.4 For dogs of small breeds, a 
higher dosage of up to 100 mg/kg PO may be required. Daily 
doses should be divided into 3 or 4 portions. For sufficient 
absorption, mitotane should be given with food. Replacement 
therapy starts on day 3, consisting of daily glucocorticoids (e.g., 
2 mg/kg cortisone acetate), mineralocorticoids (e.g., 0.0125 
mg/kg fludrocortisone acetate), and salt (0.1 g/kg NaCl), all 
divided q12h. After completing the initial course of mitotane, 
the glucocorticoid dose can be reduced (e.g., 0.5-1 mg/kg 
cortisone acetate) but doubled for 1 or 2 days in the event of 
injury, severe physical stress, or anesthesia. To prevent recur-
rence, mitotane should be administered at the initial daily dose 
once weekly for at least 6 months, or even lifelong.121
Adverse Effects, Monitoring, Prognosis
Adverse eects of mitotane include anorexia, lethargy, weak-
ness and diarrhea. If seen, mitotane must be temporarily dis-
continued, but not the substitution therapy. To evaluate whether 
the AT has been destroyed completely, the UCCR can be mea-
sured aer the initial course of mitotane (i.e., aer day 45) and 
every 6 months thereaer. In addition to UCCR, regular blood 
Na and K assessment is important to help determine the dose 
of mineralocorticoids. e median survival times of dogs with 
ATs treated with mitotane range from 102 to 476 days and did 
not dier signicantly from the survival times for dogs treated 
with trilostane.118 119 However, these studies used only partial 
adrenocortical destruction and did not use the mitotane pro-
tocol as described herein (aimed at complete adrenocortical 
destruction).
NEW MEDICAL ADRENAL-TARGETED TREATMENTS
Concerns regarding current medical adrenal-targeting treatment 
options include low selectivity for specically decreasing cortisol, 
possibility of overdosage, and occurrence of disruptive changes 
in the adrenal cortex which necessitate careful dosing schemes 
and regular check-ups. Current research is exploring therapeutic 
candidates that might have superior selectivity, eectivity, and/or 
tolerability. ese candidates include MC2R antagonist, steroido-
genic factor 1 inverse agonists, and novel formulations of SOAT1 
inhibitors.
PHARMACOTHERAPY: DRUGS TARGETING THE 
PITUITARY
Overview
Medical management of PDH would ideally target the pituitary 
tumor. Because dopamine (DA) and somatostatin (SST) both 
have inhibitory functions in the pituitary gland, research on 
pituitary-targeting drugs focuses on 3 receptor subtypes: DA 
receptor subtype 2 (DRD2), SST receptor subtype 2 (SSTR2), and 
SST receptor subtype 5 (SSTR5). In canine corticotroph adeno-
mas, the primary receptor subtype expressed is SSTR2, while 
DRD2 and particularly SSTR5 are expressed, but at much lower 
levels.122 ere are currently no pituitary-targeted drugs that are 
registered for use in canine PDH.
Cabergoline
Cabergoline is a DA agonist that binds to DRD2. In line with the 
moderate DRD2 expression in canine corticotroph adenomas, 
canine corticotroph cells responded only modestly to cabergoline 
invitro.123 However, invivo experiments demonstrate that 43% 
of dogs with PDH responded to cabergoline therapy with fewer 
clinical signs, smaller pituitary adenomas, and lower UCCRs. 
is observed dierence in ecacy could possibly be explained 
by the dierent durations of treatment. Pro-apoptotic and anti-
proliferative eects on the pituitary gland, potentially reducing 
tumor size, make cabergoline an interesting option.
Pasireotide
Pasireotide is a multiligand SST analog that binds to the SST 
receptors 1, 2, 3 and 5. In 20 PDH dogs, pasireotide decreased 
plasma eACTH and improved clinical signs, while no severe 
adverse eects were observed.124 In a recent study, pasireotide 
plus trilostane or mitotane was given to PDH dogs with large pitu-
itary tumors. Pituitary tumor volume decreased in 6/9 dogs and 
increased in 3. No neurologic signs or grossly apparent adverse 
eects were observed.125
Octreotide
Octreotide, an SST analog, binds to SSTR2 with high anity and 
to SST receptor subtype 3 (SSTR3) and SSTR5 with moderate 
anity. In line with the high SSTR2 expression in canine corti-
cotroph adenomas, octreotide inhibited ACTH release from cor-
ticotroph cells invitro more eectively than either pasireotide or 
cabergoline.122 In humans, octreotide can cause GI side eects, 
but this is less well documented for dogs. For other cancers, e.g., 
insulinoma, octreotide is sometimes used as adjunctive treat-
ment to inhibit insulin secretion (ch. 289). Due to the high SSTR2 
expression in canine corticotroph tumors, the availability of oral 
octreotide treatment could be an interesting treatment approach 
for dogs with PDH.
COMPLICATIONS AND COMORBIDITIES
SYSTEMIC HYPERTENSION (CH. 236)
Hypertension, dened as a systolic blood pressure (BP) >160 mm 
Hg, has been diagnosed in 37-86% of untreated HC dogs and 
nearly half had severe hypertension (>180 mm Hg). Routine BP 
evaluation is advocated in all dogs with HC (ch. 91). Dogs with 


---

## Page 2171

CHAPTER 293
•
Hyperadrenocorticism (Cushing’s Syndrome) in Dogs
2019
eBooks.Health.Elsevier.com
thrombocytosis, hypokalemia, and/or proteinuria have the high-
est risk of target organ damage recognized in eyes, kidneys, brain, 
heart, and blood vessels. Proposed pathophysiological mecha-
nisms for the hypertension include increased mineralocorticoid 
activity, decreased nitric oxide concentrations, and increased 
renal vascular resistance.126 127 Systemic hypertension warrants 
treatment. Prevalence and severity of systemic hypertension are 
not correlated with clinical control of HC and some develop 
hypertension only during medical treatment with trilostane. 
erefore, continuous monitoring of BP during treatment for HC 
is recommended.126
DIABETES MELLITUS (DM, CH. 291)
DM is one of the most common comorbidities of HC.33 45 Corti-
sol excess induces a state of insulin resistance and favors hepatic 
gluconeogenesis, but it also aects beta-cell function by decreas-
ing insulin secretion, blunting the incretin eect, and by direct 
cytotoxicity.128 is combination commonly causes mild hyper-
glycemia in dogs with HC, changes that resemble feline diabe-
tes more than canine, and changes that resolve with successful 
management of HC (chs. 291 and 292). Restricting the diagnosis 
of DM to BG concentrations which exceed renal threshold (175-
225 mg/dL [9.7-12.5 mmol/L]) causing persistent glucosuria in 
dogs with pre-existing HC is usually straightforward. However, 
diagnosing HC in a dog with poorly controlled DM can be chal-
lenging because of overlapping clinical and clinicopathological 
features. Further, uncontrolled DM can result in false-positive 
adrenal function test results and therefore diagnostic imaging is 
needed to conrm the diagnosis.45 Trying to obtain reasonable 
management of DM before testing for HC is usually advised. 
Management of dogs with concurrent DM and HC should take 
into consideration both conditions. Adequate control of DM is 
unlikely if HC is not properly managed.
GALLBLADDER MUCOCELE (CH. 273)
Gallbladder mucocele has been reported in 1.6-23% of dogs 
with HC (Video 293.8).33 129 HC is reported in 3.4-23% of dogs 
with gallbladder mucocele.130 Concurrent diagnosis of HC at 
the time of cholecystectomy in dogs with gallbladder mucocele 
is associated with about a 2-fold increase in odds of death-in-
hospital than dogs without HC.131 The association between 
gallbladder mucocele and HC is thought to be due to signifi-
cantly higher concentrations of unconjugated bile acids within 
the extrahepatic biliary tree resulting from steroid excess. 
Unconjugated bile acids are more hydrophobic and may lead 
to injury to the biliary epithelium and subsequent mucinous 
hyperplasia, one of the defining histological features of muco-
celes. Recent work with adrenal testing in dogs with both HC 
and mucocele showed increased basal and post-ACTHst con-
centrations of DHEA, suggesting that these dogs have impaired 
secretion of DHEA. Altered transport or increased androgen 
synthesis by the adrenals is also possible.132 In dogs with HC 
and mucocele, higher dosages of trilostane were required 
to control HC. Since trilostane is a lipid-soluble drug, the 
higher dose requirement may be the result of decreased abil-
ity to secrete bile, similar to what happens with lipid-soluble 
nutrients known to be decreased when an extrahepatic biliary 
obstruction is present.132
UROLITHIASIS (CH. 308)
Urolithiasis was reported in about 3% of dogs with HC.33 Hyper-
calciuria appears to be a predisposing factor for Ca oxalate urolith 
formation. In dogs with HC, a 10 times higher predisposition for 
Ca-containing uroliths has been reported compared to control 
dogs.
PULMONARY THROMBOEMBOLISM (PT, CH. 219)
PT is extremely uncommon in dogs with HC. When present, it 
may cause acute tachypnea, orthopnea, and dyspnea. is com-
plication is likely a consequence of the hypercoagulability that 
occurs in dogs with HC. Increased brinogen concentrations, 
decreased brinolysis, and/or thrombocytosis have been sug-
gested to be the cause of hypercoagulability in aected dogs. Also, 
changes in thromboelastography consistent with a hypercoagula-
tive status can be observed.51 133

SECTION XIX

Endocrine Disease

INTRODUCTION
F
eline hyperadrenocorticism (fHAC; 
Cushing’s syndrome; hypercortisolism) 
is less common, has more worrisome 
consequences, and poses greater challenges to 
diagnose and treat than canine hyperadreno-
corticism (cHAC). fHAC has a worse progno-
sis than the condition in dogs. Unlike in dogs, 
fHAC is commonly associated with diabetes 
mellitus (DM), a skin hyperfragility syn-
drome, and other conditions that can compli-
cate management. Basic pathophysiology and 
other aspects of fHAC are similar to cHAC 
(ch. 293). is chapter focuses on the clinical 
condition in cats caused by chronic exposure 
to excess cortisol or other steroid hormones 
with cortisol-like action. For example, cats 
with progesterone-secreting adrenocortical 
tumors, with or without aldosterone secre-
tion, may have clinical signs similar to fHAC 
(ch. 295).1-3 Importantly, insulin-resistant 
DM in cats is more commonly associated 
with hypersomatotropism (HS; feline acro-
megaly) than with fHAC (ch. 280).
PATHOPHYSIOLOGY
Naturally occurring fHAC is a multi-systemic disorder resulting 
from excessive production of cortisol by the adrenal cortices. 
ere are single case reports and several case series of cats with 
fHAC that together total only about 100 cases.4-25 Pituitary-
dependent hyperadrenocorticism (PDH) aects about 85% of 
cats with fHAC, most commonly caused by an autonomous 
adrenocorticotropic hormone (ACTH)-secreting adenoma. 
Adrenal-dependent hyperadrenocorticism (ADH) aects about 
15% of cats with fHAC, usually caused by an autonomously 
functioning adrenocortical adenoma or carcinoma.5 A small 
number of cats have a pituitary tumor that secretes more than 1 
hormone.19 26-29 Iatrogenic fHAC has been described aer glu-
cocorticoid or progestogen administration,30-32 either of which 
can severely suppress endogenous cortisol and cause adrenocor-
tical atrophy.33
CLINICAL SIGNS
OVERVIEW
Early reports of cats with fHAC described obvious clinical signs, 
oen present for months before diagnosis. As subtleties and 
variations in signs and associated biochemical disorders are bet-
ter appreciated, fHAC is being diagnosed in cats with fewer clini-
cal signs (Table 294.1).6 Early diagnosis and treatment may avoid 
development of severe clinical signs or DM.34
AGE, BREED, SEX
fHAC is most common in middle-aged to older cats (median age 
in the various studies was 9.5 to 13 years; range from 3 to 15 years) 
with no apparent sex or breed predilection.
PHYSICAL CHANGES
Skin
Cutaneous manifestations of fHAC include truncal and abdominal 
alopecia, but far less frequently than in cHAC. An unkempt hair coat 
and cornication defects/seborrhea are common. eir thin skin is 
prone to tears, bruising, and secondary infection (Figure 294.1). e 
hyperfragile skin is particularly distressing to owners and unwary cli-
nicians. Cats with fHAC should always be handled with great care. 
Calcinosis cutis has not been reported in naturally occurring nor iat-
rogenic fHAC. Recurrent sebaceous cysts have also been seen.
Other Physical Examination Findings
Cats with fHAC oen have a pendulous abdomen, muscle wast-
ing, hepatomegaly, and weight gain. A minority lose weight. Some 
have soened tongue barbs or tongue ulcers.
METABOLIC ALTERATIONS
Diabetes Mellitus (DM)
Polyuria, polydipsia (PU/PD) and polyphagia (PP) are the most 
common metabolic signs of fHAC but are almost always second-
ary to DM with its consequent glycosuria and osmotic diuresis 
(ch. 292) or to chronic kidney disease (CKD) with its consequent

inability to concentrate urine (ch. 301). Most cats with fHAC 
have insulin resistant DM; in 1 case series, 27 of 30.11 Several 
other series have indicated that a majority, but not all, cats with 
hyperadrenocorticism (HAC) have concurrent DM.7 10 Insulin 
resistance is reected in large daily insulin doses oen required 
(∼2 units/kg) to control their hyperglycemia and glycosuria.4 7
However, clinicians should not exclude the possibility of HAC in 
a well-controlled diabetic on a typical dose of insulin. PU/PD is 
not common in fHAC in the absence of DM or CKD.
Chronic Kidney Disease (CKD, ch. )
PU/PD in fHAC can also be associated with CKD, a rare con-
comitant condition in cHAC. ere is no evidence of a direct 
correlation between the two nor that presence of one makes the 
other more dicult to manage.11 Most cats with fHAC and a urine 
specic gravity (USG) <1.020 have CKD.
Infection
Many cats with HAC develop infections, sometimes with unusual 
organisms, that are frequently dicult to resolve.4 9 11 17 is appar-
ent predisposition to infection suggests that cats with fHAC are 
either quite immunosuppressed or their fragile skin, DM, and other 
concurrent conditions increase susceptibility to severe infection.
Pancreatitis (ch. )
Cats with fHAC have been described as having pancreatitis, but 
fHAC is a severe anti-inammatory condition, making this an 
unlikely pairing of conditions. Feline pancreatic lipase immuno-
reactivity (fPLI) may not be reliable in cats with fHAC.11 35
Systemic Hypertension (ch. ), Blindness (ch. )
About 20% of cats with fHAC are hypertensive, a lower prevalence 
than in cHAC.11 36 Acute blindness due to bilateral retinal detach-
ment was reported in a hypertensive cat with HAC.23
GENERAL LABORATORY TESTING AND 
DIAGNOSTIC IMAGING
SERUM BIOCHEMISTRY PROFILE, COMPLETE BLOOD 
COUNT (CBC), URINALYSIS (UA)
Hyperglycemia, seen in about 80% of cats with fHAC, is com-
mon. Most but not all of these cats have overt DM (Table 
294.2; Figure 294.2). About 50% of cats with fHAC have hyper-
cholesterolemia, probably related to poorly controlled DM 
and increased lipolysis (ch. 63). In 1 review, >50% of cats with 
fHAC had increases in blood urea nitrogen (BUN) and >25% 
had increases in both BUN and creatinine concentrations, 
consistent with CKD (ch. 62).37 Cats lack a steroid-induced 
isoenzyme of alkaline phosphatase (ALP) and only ∼30% of 
cats with fHAC have even mild increases in ALP and/or ala-
nine aminotransferase (ALT) activity, likely secondary to DM 
(ch. 65). e “stress leukogram” (lymphopenia, eosinopenia and 
neutrophilia) is not common in cats with fHAC (ch. 58). Cats with 
fHAC that do not have DM or CKD usually have USG >1.020. In 
comparison to dogs with HAC, cats tend to have more dramatic 
physical changes and less dramatic clinicopathological changes.
DIAGNOSTIC IMAGING
Overview
Diagnostic imaging allows identication of pituitary and adrenal 
size (large or small) and can provide evidence of masses, local 
vessel or organ invasion, or metastases, aiding in choosing indi-
vidualized treatment strategies. Seeing a mass does not indicate 
function. Images should be interpreted in the context of clinical 
signs and endocrine test results.
Radiography
Radiographic examination of the thorax and abdomen is advisable 
in all cats suspected or proven to have fHAC. Survey radiographs 
of the thorax and abdomen uncommonly reveal an adrenal mass 
(Figure 294.3, A), but also may identify concurrent abnormalities
